\title%
[\disclaimer]%
{Filtered Lie algebroids\\ and parabolic geometry}
\author{Anthony D.~Blaom}
\address{E-mail: {\tt anthony.blaom@gmail.com}}%
% \keywords{Lie algebroid, Cartan algebroid, equivalence, geometric
% structure, Cartan connection, deformation, pseudogroup,
% prolongation}%
% \subjclass{Primary
% 53C15, % General geometric structures on manifolds
% 58H15% Deformations of structure
% ; Secondary 53B15, % Other connections
% 53C07, % Special connections and metrics on vector bundles
% 53C05, % Connections, general theory
% 58H05, % Pseudogroups and differential groupoids
% 53A55, % Differential invariants (local theory), geometric objects
% 53A30, % Conformal differential geometry
% 58A15% Exterior differential systems (Cartan theory)

\thispagestyle{empty}
\begin{center}
  \disclaimer
  \vspace{\baselineskip}
\end{center}
\begin{abstract}
  This work is an exposition of parabolic geometry with some notable novelties. The
  filtered Lie algebroids introduced here generalize the filtered manifolds in Tohru
  Morimoto's study of (what are now called) parabolic geometries, a rich class of finite type
  structures generalizing conformal and projective structures. We propose a new
  abstraction of parabolic geometry based on Lie groupoids whose associated Lie algebroids
  are filtered. In particular, we present a new prolongation procedure to recover the
  abstraction from its ``underlying geometric structure'', using semi-direct products in
  the Lie groupoid category. The construction is predicated on the observation that Lie
  groupoids with filtered Lie algebroids admit a semi-direct product structure whenever
  they also admit a Weyl gauge, an ``oid'' version of lifts of grading elements of a Lie
  algebra. Our approach allows for the parallel treatment of infinitesimal and global
  aspects, the simpler infinitesimal apsect typically informing global constructions.

  A parabolic geometry is essentially a deformed generalized flag manifold $G/H$ where, in
  previous formulations, $G$ is assumed to have a semisimple Lie algebra. Here we show, with
  little additional effort, that it is suffices to suppose the Lie algebra is reductive.
\end{abstract}
\maketitle
%\vspace{-1\baselineskip}\tableofcontents

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% here %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\refreshpage
\section{Introduction}
Parabolic geometry is an abstract framework that unifies a rich collection of finite-type
geometric structures, such as conformal, projective, contact projective,
CR,\footnote{non-degenerate and partially integrable \cite[p.414]{Cap_Slovak_09}.} almost
Grassmannian, almost quaternionic, conformal Lorenzian, almost Lagrangean, Lagrangean
contact, Lie contact structures, finite-type $n$-plane fields, and many others
\cite[Chapter 4]{Cap_Slovak_09}. In the first place, it provides for a unified treatment
and interpretation of ``curvature'', and among its important applications is the
systematic derivation of invariant differential operators
\cite{Cap_etal_01,Calderbank_Diemer_01,Cap_Soucek_07,Cap_Soucek_12}.

These notes are an outgrowth of our attempts to tame some of the theory's more technical
aspects by approaching it from new points of view.  We prove the basic existence,
normalization and uniqueness theorems, culminating in a solution to the equivalence
problem for a large subclass of parabolic geometries. This treatment is suitable for
non-experts well versed in Lie groupoids and Lie algebroids, but we also flag points of
difference and novelty for parabolic geometry experts. Our results on firmly filtered Lie
groupoids/algebroids and our version of prolongation are new.

\subsection{The main new ideas}\label{firstfirst}
According to Cartan's generalization of Klein's Elrangen program \cite{Sharpe_97},
geometric structures of finite type are viewed as ``symmetries deformed by curvature''. In
the contemporary formulation, the object deformed is the principal bundle associated with
some homogeneous space, $G/H$, as we describe further in \ref{contemporary} below.  The
parabolic geometries arise when $H \subset G$ is a parabolic subgroup and $G$ is
semisimple (or even reductive, as we will show here). Such homogeneous spaces, known as
generalized flag manifolds, are in rich supply. The alternative approach promoted here is
to consider deformations of action groupoids, which in the transitive case have the form
$G \times M$, $M \cong G/H$. In this approach, the key concept of {\em prolongation}
manifests, we believe, more a more natural way.

For the sake of concreteness, consider the conformal sphere $S^n$, a generalized flag
manifold with action groupoid $\SO(n + 1, n) \times S^n$. Now, differentiating conformal
automorphisms, i.e., elements of $\SO(n + 1, n)$, we obtain a Lie groupoid
morphism $$\SO(n + 1, n) \times S^n \rightarrow \CO[TS^n]$$ onto the frame groupoid
$\CO[TS^n]$, consisting of conformal isomorphisms between tangent spaces. The kernel of
this morphism is a Lie group bundle with commutative, simply-connected fibres. By
arguments recalled later, it has a natural identification with $T^*\!S^n$. A fundamental
observation, not immediately obvious, but following from the general results presented
here, is that the morphism above splits (non-canonically), delivering an identification of
$\SO(n + 1, n) \times S^n$ with the Lie groupoid semi-direct product
$\CO[TS^n] \times T^*\!S^n$. This is significant, for in the ``curved'' case of an
arbitrary conformal manifold $M$, where the action groupoid is gone, {\em a proxy for the
  action groupoid persists}, namely the semi-direct product $\CO[TM] \times T^*\!M$. This
is what we call the {\df prolongation} of the conformal structure.

In the flat case, we can give the kernel $T^*\!S^n$ the interpretation of all $2$-jets of
conformal automorphisms vanishing to first order. Relevant arguments can be found in
\cite{Abadoglu_etal_08}. This connects prolongation to the usual idea of introducing
derivatives as new variables. The reader is warned, however, that prolongations appearing
in the Cartan geometry literature, and prolongations in the naive sense of PDE's,
generally diverge in the {\em curved} case, and this is true here also.

To make the prolongation $\CO[TS^n] \times T^*\!S^n$ a formal parabolic geometry, it
suffices to equip its Lie algebroid $A$ with a suitable $\so(n + 1, n)$-bundle structure,
and this structure, which we call {\df model data}, is identifiable with the trivial
$\so(n + 1, n)$-bundle structure of the action algebroid $\so(n + 1, n) \times S^n\cong A$
in the flat case. As we will see in \ref{conformal}, the Lie algebroid $A$ of
$\CO[TM] \times T^*\!M $ has a natural filtration
$A = A^{-1} \supset A^0 \supset A^1=T^*\!M$, and while the Lie algebra bundle structure on
the associated graded $\graded(A)$ determined by the bracket on $A$ is {\em not} modeled
on $\so(n + 1, n)$, there is a second, related Lie algebra bundle structure that {\em
  is}. Model data on $A$ is constructed by finding a canonical way to lift this latter Lie
algebra bundle structure from $\graded(A)$ to $A$. A ``canonical'' lift is one that
normalizes the corresponding {\df model curvature}, whose direct analogue in the standard
approach is known simply as ``curvature''.

More generally, prolongation allows us to recover, in the typical case, an abstract
parabolic geometry from it's ``underlying geometric structure'', such as the conformal
structure above, which manifests in the standard theory as an ``infinitesimal flag
structure'' \cite[Definition 3.1.6, p.~248]{Cap_Slovak_09}. For reasons explained at the
end of \ref{levi_}, we prefer to call the Lie groupoid version of these objects {\df Levi
structures}.

Our version of prolongation is anticipated by the observation that natural candidates for
parabolic geometries are certain Lie groupoids that {\em always} admit a semi-direct
product structure. Indeed, splittings like the one above exist for a large class of Lie
groupoids, even intransitive ones, provided they admit what we call a Weyl gauge. As
a special case, we can show that the action groupoid of any generalized flag manifold is a
semi-direct product (\ref{all}, Corollary C), a fact that does not seem to have
been noticed before. Weyl gauges are discussed further in \ref{intro_prolong} below.

Various algebraic prolongation techniques leading to the solution of the equivalence
problem for parabolic geometries, which have a long and complicated history \cite[Appendix
A]{Cap_Slovak_09}, were discovered by Tanaka \cite{Tanaka_79}, Morimoto \cite{Morimoto_93}
and Cap and Schichl \cite{Cap_Schichl_00}. The conceptually simple approach sketched above
is probably the most noteworthy novelty of the present exposition. The idea, in the
present context, of replacing principal bundles with Lie groupoids, dates back to
Ehresmann's 1959 paper \cite{Ehresmann_59}.\footnote{At this time the infinitesimal
  concept of a Lie algebroid had not been formalized, and this concept did not mature
  substantially until Jean Pradines' work in the late 1960's. This may explain why the
  groupoid approach did not immediately gain traction.}  Our work is strongly guided by
{\v{C}}ap and Slov{\'a}k's seminal monograph \cite{Cap_Slovak_09} and our debt to that
work necessarily extends beyond those places explicitly noted.

\subsection{The contemporary point of view}\label{contemporary}%
In the principal bundle formulation, which originates in Ehresmann's earlier 1951 paper
\cite{Ehresmann_51}, a Cartan geometry, modeled on some homogeneous space $G/H$, is
a smooth manifold $M$ and a principal $H$-bundle $P$ over $M$, equipped with: (i) A
parallelism, understood as a $V$-valued one-form $\omega$ on $P$ (the {\df connection}),
required to be suitably compatible with the principal bundle structure; and (ii) A
compatible identification of the vector space $V$ with the infinitesimalization
${\mathfrak g}$ of $G$ (the {\df model data}). Combining (i) and (ii), we can view
$\omega $ as ${\mathfrak g} $-valued one-form, which is the object commonly known as a
{\df Cartan connection}. The precise definition is recalled in
\ref{cartan_geometries_defined}.

In the case of a principal $H$-bundle $G \rightarrow G/H$, the Maurer-Cartan form $\omega$
is a Cartan connection, and in the general case there is a notion of curvature,
$d \omega - [\omega,\omega]_{\mathfrak g}$, which vanishes in the homogeneous case, by
virtue of the Maurer-Cartan equations for $G$. Since it depends on the choice of model
data (it involves the bracket on the Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g} $ of $G$) we refer to this
as the {\df model curvature}; it's significance is that $M$ is locally identifiable with
the model $G/H$ whenever the model curvature vanishes. (As explained later, an alternative
notion of curvature, which does not depend on model data, but is nevertheless an
obstruction to symmetry, will play an ancillary role in the present exposition.)

The connection and model data allow one to transfer certain structures from the model
space, such as a $G$-invariant quadratic form on ${\mathfrak g} /{\mathfrak h} $, to
structures on the underlying manifold $M$, for example a Riemannian metric, and this
latter constitutes the ``underlying geometric structure'' of the Cartan geometry. In the
parabolic case, the Lie algebra ${\mathfrak h} $ of $H$ defines a filtration of
${\mathfrak g} $ which, among other things, implies that $TM$ is non-trivially filtered in
the typical case. A simple example is CR-geometry, where this filtration is defined by the
contact structure ${\mathcal H} $, namely
$0=:T^0M \subset T^{-1}M:={\mathcal H} \subset T^{-2}M:=TM$.

Different choices of $\omega $ may lead to the same underlying geometric structure, which
leads to the first fundamental problem of the theory, how to choose $\omega $ so as to
``normalize'' the model curvature, for then $\omega$ (and its curvature, for example)
encode basic invariants of the underlying geometry. The second fundamental problem is how
to reconstruct an abstract Cartan geometry from the underlying geometric structure.  Where
this is possible one obtains a one-to-one correspondence between (isomorphism classes) of
normalized Cartan geometries and the concrete geometries of interest, solving the
``equivalence problem'' for those structures. The technical tool for making this
reconstruction is known as prolongation, because of its roots in the procedure in the
theory of PDE's of the same name.

\subsection{The new approach}
In \cite{Morimoto_93} Tohru Morimoto introduced a novel prolongation technique to prove
existence and normalization results for Cartan connections in parabolic geometry. In it he
formulates the notion of filtered manifolds, now a part of the contemporary formalism
\cite{Cap_Slovak_09}. The filtration of $TM$ for CR-geometry above is a simple
example. Morimoto also demonstrates the usefulness of separating the notions of connection
and model data, a theme of our own work cited shortly, and the present exposition, but not
emphasized more generally.

Rather than pursuing Morimito's original strategy, however, we synthesize an approach
based on a new analysis of ``firmly'' filtered Lie algebroids, a natural generalization of
Morimoto's filtered manifolds. In this recounting, Lie groupoids, whose corresponding
infinitesimalization are filtered Lie algebroids, replace the principal bundles of the
conventional approach, along the lines intimated in \ref{firstfirst}. Previous work of
ours, reviewed in Section \ref{cartan_section}, shows how to view Lie algebroids or Lie
groupoids as (possibly intransitive) ``symmetries deformed by curvature'', by equipping
them with connections {\em without} model data
\cite{Blaom_06,Blaom_12,Blaom_13,Blaom_16,Blaom_16b} (but nevertheless called Cartan
  connections). In the groupoid case, the connection is a multiplicatively closed
horizontal $n$-plane field, $D$, and in the infinitesimal case, it is a Koszul connection,
$\nabla $, suitably compatible with the Lie algebroid structure. The type of symmetry, if
it exists, is not specified {\em a priori}, and the natural definition of curvature (the
usual one for $\nabla $) does not depend on a notion of model data. Here we introduce, for
the first time in the ``oid'' context, a notion of compatible model data, and of model
curvature, in which the model {\em is} specified ahead of time, as in the standard
approach. With these basic ingredients we can imitate the standard theory, with some
further novelties discussed below,

\subsection{Weyl gauges and semi-direct products}\label{intro_prolong}
The general considerations leading to the Lie groupoid splittings alluded to in
\ref{firstfirst} can be previewed here by considering the special Lie {\em algebra} case
of our Theorem \ref{weyl}:
\begin{theorem}
  Let
  $\cdots \supset {\mathfrak a}^{-1} \supset {\mathfrak a}^0 \supset {\mathfrak a}^1
  \supset {\mathfrak a}^2 \supset \cdots$ be a filtration\footnote{See \ref{filtered} for
    precisely what we mean here.} of some finite-dimensional Lie algebra ${\mathfrak a} $,
  and assume the graded Lie algebra $\graded({\mathfrak a}^0)$, associated with
  ${\mathfrak a}^0$, admits a grading element $E$, necessarily lying in
  $\graded({\mathfrak a})_0$.  Moreover, assume that $E $ has a lift
  $\epsilon \in {\mathfrak a}^0$ such that
  \begin{equation}
    [{\mathfrak a}, \epsilon] \subset {\mathfrak a}^1.\label{invariance}
  \end{equation}
  Then ${\mathfrak a}^j\subset {\mathfrak a}$ is an ideal for all $j \ge 0$ and
  ${\mathfrak a}$ is isomorphic to the semi-direct product
  ${\mathfrak a}/{\mathfrak a}^1 \oplus_{\adjoint}\graded({\mathfrak a}^1)$, the
  isomorphism depending on the choice of $\epsilon $.
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
Recall here that an element $E$ of a graded Lie algebra
${\mathfrak g}=\oplus_j {\mathfrak g}_j $ is a {\df grading element} if $[E , X] = j X$,
whenever $X \in {\mathfrak g}_j$. Note that the theorem requires only a grading element of
$\graded({\mathfrak a}^0)$ and not $\graded({\mathfrak a})$. When the additional
``invariance'' condition \eqref{invariance} is satisfied, we call $\epsilon $ a {\df Weyl
  gauge}.

If ${\mathfrak a} $ is filtered and the condition about ideals is also satisfied,
necessary for the existence of a Weyl gauge, we say that ${\mathfrak a} $ is {\df firmly
  filtered}. This is a strong condition, but the right one for our general setting, as we
shall see already for homogeneous spaces $G/H$ and other examples in Section
\ref{introduction}. The theorem above has the following corollary:
\begin{aside}
  A filtered Lie algebra ${\mathfrak a}$ admits a Weyl gauge if and only if it is firmly
  filtered and $\graded({\mathfrak a}^0)$ admits an ${\mathfrak a}$-invariant grading
  element $E$.
\end{aside}

The above theorem does {\em not} apply to the canonical filtrations associated with the
parabolic subalgebras of a reductive (or semisimple) Lie algebra (see
\ref{canonical_filtrations_}), as these cannot be firmly filtered. The point is, rather,
that Weyl gauges have generalizations to filtered Lie algebroids and Lie groupoids (even
intransitive ones), including the action algebroids/groupoids of generalized flag
manifolds. For us, a parabolic geometry is a firmly filtered, transitive Lie groupoid,
together with suitably compatible model data (Definition \ref{parabolic_defined}). This
model data identifies each tangent space $T_mM$ (non-canonically) with a Lie algebra
quotient ${\mathfrak g}/{\mathfrak p} $, where $\mathfrak g$ is reductive and
${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is parabolic. These assumptions on
${\mathfrak g}/{\mathfrak p} $ guarantee the existence of Weyl gauges, by a generalization
of the italicized statement above. In the above super-simplification of the general
scenario, ${\mathfrak a}/{\mathfrak a}^1$, together with its representation
$\graded({\mathfrak a}^1)$, play the role of the corresponding Levi structure.

% An immediate application of our results in the general setting (specifically, our Theorems
% \ref{weyl2} and \ref{all}) is the following:
% \begin{aside}
%   Every action groupoid ${\mathcal G} = G \times G/H$ associated with a generalized flag
%   manifold $G/H$ is a semi-direct product ${\mathcal H} \times \exp(N)$, where
%   ${\mathcal H}$ is a certain transitive Lie groupoid with reductive vertex group (the
%   reductive Levi quotient of the parabolic subgroup $H\subset G$) and $N$ a nilpotent Lie
%   algebra bundle (modeled on the nilradical of the Lie algebra of $H$).
% \end{aside}
% Our results may be viewed as a generalization of this one to the ``curved'', possibly
% intransitive case.  In the case of the conformal sphere, the result is that
% $\SO(n + 1, 1) \times S^n$ is the semi-direct product $\CO[TS^n]\times T^*\!S^n$, where
%  Conformal automorphisms are determined by their jets to second order, and we have
% the following interpretation: $\CO[TS^n]$ encodes is the zeroeth and first order parts,
% while $T^*S^n$ encodes the second order part.

The name ``Weyl gauge'' comes from the Weyl structures/connections in the standard theory
of parabolic geometry \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}, which generalize Weyl's own connections in
conformal geometry.  It may be there exists a correspondence between the new semi-direct
product description and the constructions in \cite{Cap_Slovak_09} but this is not obvious
to us. In any case, Weyl gauges ground the entire theory developed here (and make sense
before introducing any assumptions about model data, such as semisimplicity) while in
\cite{Cap_Slovak_09} prolongation is first developed without Weyl structures.  Here the
semi-direct product structure leads to perhaps the most explicit description of parabolic
geometries to date --- in fact, of all transitive filtered Cartan geometries, a mild
generalization (Theorem \ref{abstract_levi_}).

\subsection{Infinitesimal geometry} An obvious advantage of the Lie groupoid approach is a
direct relationship between global and infinitesimal representations of the geometry,
namely a natural generalization of the relationship between Lie groups and Lie
algebras. Indeed, our development generally begins at the infinitesimal level, where
constructions are easier to understand (and how we generally discovered them) and we only
globalize them later. There is even the possibility, not pursued here, of completely
reducing the global theory to the infinitesimal one, using the Lie groupoid versions of
Lie I and Lie II \cite{Crainic_Fernandes_11}, provided one is happy to ignore certain
topological distinctions between geometries blurred by restricting to the case of
source-simply-connected Lie groupoids.

In the principal bundle approach, it is conventional to view something called the the
  adjoint tractor bundle ${\mathcal A}$, as the corresponding infinitesimal object. For
example, the model curvature of a parabolic geometry is there understood as an
${\mathcal A}$-valued two-form. This seems natural because tractor bundles are associated
bundles. In the Lie groupoid point-of-view the analogue of associated bundles are Lie
groupoid representations, but there is no canonical representation of a Lie groupoid on
its Lie algebroid. Stated differently, the Atiyah Lie algebroid of a principal bundle is
not an associated bundle; it only becomes one (is identifiable with ${\mathcal A}$) {\em
  after} choosing a Cartan connection. This essential difference between the two
approaches has a related manifestation. As we recall in Section \ref{cartan_section}, a
Cartan connection $D$ on a Lie groupoid immediately infinitesimalizes to a Koszul
connection $\nabla $ on the corresponding Lie algebroid, without model data playing any
role, but it seems this connection does not appear in \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}, at least not
explicitly; rather the focus is on the so-called tractor connection on ${\mathcal A}$,
which depends on its associated bundle structure. While the tractor connection has an
analogue in the present approach, the infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla $ and the
adjoint tractor connection are different, except in the flat case. See
\eqrefs{tractors}{tractor1}.

\subsection{Model data}\label{role}
Important ingredients of the present approach are the ``oid'' versions of classical Cartan
connections, $D$ and $\nabla $ mentioned above. In \cite{Blaom_12} we outlined an
ambitious program for constructing such Cartan connections for general geometric
structures of finite type, without the need to prescribe a flat model {\em a priori},
using a direct PDE prolongation procedure. While some simple structures are amenable to
this approach, the program has largely been unsuccessful. In the case of parabolic
geometries, the specification of model data {\em a priori} appears to be essential: It
provides a hook into the rich structure theory of reductive and semisimple Lie algebras,
simplifying the normalization process, which seems intractable in the earlier PDE
approach.

The notion of model data in the Lie groupoid case is different from the classical notion
because it makes sense without reference to the Cartan connection $D$ (which corresponds
to the parallelism in Ehresmann's definition). Model data identifies each tangent space
$T_mM$ (non-canonically) with a Lie algebra quotient ${\mathfrak g}/{\mathfrak h}$, and if
${\mathfrak h} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is a {\em separating} Lie subalgebra (true for all
parabolic subalgebras when ${\mathfrak g} $ is reductive) then {\em any compatible Cartan
  connection is completely determined by the model data}. So, in a sense, the model data
``is'' also the connection in such cases, but unlike the connection $D$, it is a purely
infinitesimal object! That said, given model data, the question of existence of a
compatible Cartan connection $D$, which we require, is a subtle one. The notion of a
separating Lie algebra is recalled in \ref{separating}.

% \subsection{Extensions} While not pursued here at all, the present work lays the
% foundations for the possible extension of parabolic geometry to {\em intransitive}
% geometries of finite type. Already the problem of existence of Cartan connections on
% intransitive Lie groupoids is a subtle problem, but one in which some progress is being
% made \cite{Trentinaglia_18,Trentinaglia_23}.

\subsection{Outline}
Section \ref{introduction} introduces filtered Lie algebroids and the firmly filtered
special case, and gives examples pertinent to the application to parabolic
geometry. Section \ref{cartan_geometries} generalizes the classical notion of a Cartan
geometry to the intransitive case, by formulating the notion using Lie groupoid
language. In \ref{separating} we recall the notion of separating Lie algebras, and show
that Cartan geometries with ``separating'' model data (which include parabolic geometries)
have some nice properties. Section \ref{parabolic_summary} formulates the Lie groupoid
abstraction of parabolic geometries, and their reductions, Levi structures, and states the
main theorems on existence, normalization, uniqueness, and equivalence, whose proofs are
laid out in the remaining sections.

Section \ref{semi-direct} generalizes the notion of Weyl gauges and the the semi-direct
product structure theorem stated in \ref{intro_prolong} above. Ambiguities in the choice of
gauge lead to the notion of Weyl gauge transformations, relevant to the uniqueness
theorem for parabolic geometry, but these transformations are introduced in Section
\ref{parabolic_summary} under the guise of Lie groupoid conjugations, which are more
concrete. So Section \ref{semi-direct} concludes with a proof that these amount to same
thing.

Section \ref{cartan_section}, which is logically independent of the preceding section, is
mostly a summary of our aforementioned work on Cartan connections on Lie groupoids and
algebroids, in the absence of model data. Model data in the ``oid'' context is introduced
in Section \ref{modeldata}. A crucial technical result established in Section
\ref{modeldata} is a method for constructing model data compatible with a given Cartan
connection (Proposition \ref{constructing}).

Section \ref{filtered_cartan} applies the semi-direct product theorems of Section
\ref{semi-direct} to give a characterization of transitive filtered Cartan geometries, a
certain generalization of parabolic geometries: All such geometries are obtained by
prolonging a Levi structure and making use of the following additional data: a splitting
of the underlying filtered manifold, a splitting of the anchor of the Levi structure's Lie
algebroid, and a Rho tensor, which will be familiar from the standard theory.

Section \ref{deform} introduces $\psi$-deformations, which are a natural collection
of transformations of a Lie groupoid or Lie algebroid which we can use to relate different
choices of model data, via push-forward. Unlike Weyl gauge transformations, these are
necessarily {\em not} morphisms. They are defined by objects $\psi$ which we view as
sections of the kernel of $J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $, where
$J^1 {\mathcal G} $ is the groupoid of one-jets of the underlying Lie groupoid
${\mathcal G} $ of the parabolic geometry, an interpretation that helped us to see how a
compatible Cartan connection should transform to ensure compatibility with the
$\psi$-deformed model data. This section concludes by establishing a relationship between
$\psi$-deformations and Weyl gauge transformations that we need for uniqueness results.

Section \ref{modelcurvature} derives various properties of curvature, such as invariance
under Lie algebroid automorphisms, and Bianchi's identity, and lays out the algebraic
machinery of Kostant \cite{Kostant_61} regarding the adjoint representation of a parabolic
subalgebra's reductive Levi subalgebra on the graded reductive Lie algebra that contains
it. Actually, Kostant's work applies in the semisimple case, but what we need from it
readily generalizes to reductive Lie algebras. This is the crucial algebraic
ingredient for normalizing model curvature, which can be viewed as a $2$-chain in a
related complex of vector bundles. Subsection \ref{homology} onwards is mostly a reworking
of parts of \cite[\S3.1]{Cap_Slovak_09}. The main point is Proposition \ref{curv_deform},
expressing the change in model curvature upon $\psi $-deforming the model data, in terms
of a cochain complex differential. We conclude with a brief discussion of tractor bundles,
so as to relate our Cartan connections on Lie algebroids with adjoint tractor connections.

In Section \ref{parabolic_proofs} we provide the remaining details needed to prove the
results stated in Section \ref{introduction}.

\subsection*{Audience} We assume readers are familiar with Lie groupoid/algebroid basics
--- say, what is briskly summarized in the first two lectures of
\cite{Crainic_Fernandes_11}. Other sources include
\cite{CannasdaSilva_Weinstein_99,Moerdijk_Mrcun_03,Dufour_Nguyen_05} and the book
\cite{Mackenzie_05}. In addition, parts of \cite{Blaom_16b} describing the generalization
of the adjoint action to Lie groupoids, may be helpful.

We circumvent a lot of Lie algebra structure theory, and obtain our generalizations to the
reductive case, by adopting the very nice point of view of parabolic subalgebras in
\cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}. Essential parts of this description are outlined in
Appendix \ref{lie_algebra_facts}.

\subsection*{Blanket assumptions}
All manifolds are smooth, which means $C^\infty$ or real-analytic, and have connected
components sharing a common dimension. With the exception of Lie groupoids, manifolds are
Hausdorff. All manifolds are second countable. In particular, all transitive
Lie groupoids are automatically locally trivial (source maps are both surjective and
submersive) \cite[Proposition A.3]{Trentinaglia_15} and so are gauge groupoids (groupoids
defined by a principal bundle).

The kernel of the anchor of a Lie algebroid, which is variously known as the isotropy,
vertex bundle, or adjoint bundle, is here called the {\df anchor kernel}.

\subsection*{Acknowledgements} The author is indebted to Jan Slov\'ak and Andreas \v{C}ap
for clarifying several arguments in \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}. He thanks David Calderbank for
many helpful discussions, for walking the author through \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16} in
detail, and for invaluable encouragement. The proof of Proposition \ref{separating_}
is largely due to Professor Calderbank.

\refreshpage
\section{Filtered Lie algebroids}\label{introduction}
In this section we introduce filtered Lie algebroids and the important special case of
{\em firmly} filtered Lie algebroids, and give examples which include observations
foreshadowing aspects of parabolic geometry to be presented in Section
\ref{parabolic_summary}.

\subsection{Definition}\label{filtered}
Here a filtration $$\cdots A^{-1}\supset A^0 \supset A^1 \supset A^2 \supset\cdots $$ of
some vector space or vector bundle $A$ will always be understood to satisfy
$\cap_j A^j =0$ and $\cup_j A^j = A$. All filtrations are descending.  If $A$ is a vector
bundle, then we call the filtration a {\df Lie algebroid filtration} if $A$ is a Lie
algebroid and:

\begin{conditions}
\item[\bf F1.] The induced filtration
  $\cdots \Gamma(A^{-1})\supset \Gamma(A^0) \supset \Gamma(A^1) \supset \Gamma(A^2)
  \supset\cdots $ of the section space $\Gamma(A)$ is a filtration of Lie algebras,
  meaning $[X, Y] \in \Gamma(A^{j+k})$ for $X \in \Gamma(A^j)$ and $Y \in \Gamma(A^k)$.
\item[\bf F2.] The kernel of the anchor contains $A^0$.\label{fa2}
\end{conditions}

A filtered Lie algebroid $A$ is {\df firmly filtered} if $A^0$ and the filtration of $A^0$
are invariant under the canonical representation of $A$ on its anchor kernel, i.e., if
$\Gamma(A^j) \subset \Gamma(A)$ is an ideal for all $j \ge 0$.

\begin{proposition} Let $A$ be a filtered Lie algebroid. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item The associated graded vector bundle $\graded(A)$ is a bundle of graded Lie
    algebras (a smooth field of graded Lie brackets). \label{b1}
  \item If $A$ is firmly filtered, then $\graded(A)$ is, fibre-wise, the Lie algebra
    direct sum of its negative and non-negative parts, i.e., $\oplus_{i<0}\graded(A)_i$
    and $\oplus_{i\ge 0}\graded(A)_i$ are fibre-wise ideals of $\graded(A)$. \label{rt2}
    \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}
%
\begin{remark}
  If the fibres of $\graded(A)$ are pairwise isomorphic Lie algebras, then $\graded(A)$ is
  a bona fide Lie algebra bundle, by, e.g., \cite[Theorem 6.4.5]{Mackenzie_05}; this will
  be the case in all the examples discussed here.\footnote{If $A$ is firmly filtered,
  transitive, and {\em integrable}, then $\graded(A^0)$ must be a Lie algebra bundle,
  because the canonical representation of $A$ on $A^0$ will drop to a representation of
  $A$ on $\graded(A^0)$, and the latter integrates to a representation of the transitive
  (and so locally trivial) integrating Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ having
  simply-connected source fibres.}
\end{remark}

\begin{proof}
  Let $X \in \Gamma(A^j) $ and $Y \in \Gamma(A^k)$ be given. To prove \eqref{b1}, it
  suffices to show that
  $$ [fX, Y] \equiv f[X, Y] \mod \Gamma(A^{j+k+1})$$
  for any smooth function $f$ on the base manifold. If $\#$ denotes
  the anchor, then the necessary and sufficient condition is
  that
  $$df(\#Y)X \in \Gamma(A^{j + k + 1}).$$
  If $k \ge 0$ then this is holds by F2. If instead $k \le -1 $, then $j + k + 1 \le j$,
  implying $A^j \subset A^{j + k + 1}$ and the condition is still satisfied.

  Suppose $A$ is firmly filtered. Then it is clear that $\oplus_{i<0}\graded(A)_i$ and
  $\oplus_{i\ge 0}\graded(A)_i$ are fibre-wise subalgebras of $\graded(A)$. To prove
  \eqref{rt2}, it suffices to show that sections of one of these commute with sections of
  the other. To this end, suppose $X \in \Gamma(A^k)$ and $Y \in \Gamma(A^j)$, with $k<0$
  and $j\ge 0$. Then $j + k + 1 \le j$, which implies $A^j \subset A^{j+k+1}$. Since $A$
  is firmly filtered, $\Gamma(A^j) \subset \Gamma(A)$ is an ideal, so that
  $[X, Y] \in \Gamma(A^j) \subset \Gamma(A^{j + k + 1})$. It follows that
  $[\graded_j(X), \graded_k(Y)]=0$.
\end{proof}


\begin{notation}[Bounds on degree]
  Suppose $A$ is a {\em graded} vector space or vector bundle. Then, we write $k \le A$ if
  $A_i=0$ for all $i < k$ and $A \le k$ if $A_i=0$ for all $i > k$. For example, if
  $A = A_{-2}\oplus A_{-1} \oplus A_0$, then we write $-2 \le A \le 0$. We write
  $|A| = k $ if $-k \le A \le k$ and $A_{-k} \ne 0 \ne A_k$.

  If $A$ is merely {\em filtered}, then we write $A \le k$ if $\graded(A) \le k$, etc. For
  example, for filtered $A$, we write $|A| = 1$ if
  $\graded(A) = \graded(A)_{-1} \oplus \graded(A)_0 \oplus \graded(A)_1$ with
  $\graded(A)_{\pm 1} \ne 0$.
\end{notation}

The remainder of the section is devoted to examples of filtered Lie algebroids. All but
the first class of examples are always firm.

\subsection{ Filtered Lie algebras and Lie algebra bundles} Evidently, every filtered
Lie algebra, and more generally every filtered Lie algebra bundle, is a filtered Lie
algebroid.

\subsection{ Filtered manifolds} For any Lie algebroid filtration of a tangent bundle
$TM$, we have $TM \le -1$, by Axiom F2.  These filtrations are trivially firm. A smooth
manifold $M$ whose tangent bundle is so structured is called a {\df filtered manifold}, a
notion introduced by Morimoto \cite{Morimoto_93}, and appearing prominently in
\cite{Cap_Slovak_09}.  The anchor $\# \colon A \rightarrow M$ of any {\em transitive}
filtered Lie algebroid $A$ pushes the filtration down to a filtration of $TM$. That is,
the base of any transitive filtered Lie algebroid is a filtered manifold.\footnote{One can
  drop the requirement of transitivity, but only at the expense of dropping our
  requirement $\cap_jA_j=0$ in the definition of a filtration.} \label{yt}

\subsection{Trivial filtrations}\label{trivial} Suppose $A$ is any Lie algebroid whose anchor kernel has
constant rank (e.g., is transitive). Choosing $A^0$ to be this kernel, and defining
$A^j=A$ for $j<0$ and $A^j = 0$ for $j>0$, we get a firm Lie algebroid filtration with
$-1 \le A \le 0 $ that we call the {\df trivial filtration} of $A$.

\subsection{ Filtered Klein geometries}\label{eg2}
For a more interesting class of examples, let $G$ be a Lie group with Lie algebra
${\mathfrak g}$ and $P \subset G$ a closed subgroup. Furthermore, suppose that
${\mathfrak g} $ admits a $P$-invariant filtration such that ${\mathfrak g}^0$ is the Lie
algebra of $P$. In particular, as recalled in \ref{canonical_filtrations_}, a canonical
filtration of this kind exists whenever $G/P$ is a generalized flag manifold.  Write
$M =G/P$ and let $A={\mathfrak g} \times M$ be the action algebroid. Then we obtain a
filtration of $A$, as a vector bundle, by defining
$A^j(m) = \Adjoint_g({\mathfrak g}^j)\times \{m\}$, where $g \in G$ is any element
satisfying $m = g \cdot m_0$ and $m_0$ is the left coset of $P $ containing the identity
of $G$. It is not hard to see (Proposition \ref{homogeneous}) that $A$ is a firmly
filtered Lie algebroid (irrespective of whether ${\mathfrak g}_j \subset {\mathfrak g} $
is an ideal for any $j$!).


Significantly, $\graded(A)$, which will be a graded Lie algebra bundle, is {\em not}
modeled on $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ but modeled on the ordinary direct sum of the graded
Lie algebras $\oplus_{i<0}\graded({\mathfrak g})_i$, re-equipped with {\em negative}
bracket, and $\oplus_{i\ge 0}\graded({\mathfrak g})_i$. So $\graded(A)$ has {\em two}
different, although related, Lie algebra bundle structures: the one inherited from the
filtered Lie algebroid, and a ``flat'' one inherited from ${\mathfrak g} $. On $A^0$ they
coincide.

\subsection{ Projective structures.}\label{projective} Let $A \subset J^2(TM)$ be the
abstract homogeneous PDE you solve to find the infinitesimal isometries of some projective
structure on $M$. Then $A$ is a subalgebroid mapped surjectively onto $J^1(TM)$ by the
projection $J^2(TM) \rightarrow J^1(TM)$, whose kernel intersects $A$ in the image $A^1$
of the natural injection $j \colon T^*\!M \rightarrow S^2(T^*\!M)\otimes TM$ defined by
$$j(\alpha)(V_1, V_2) = \alpha(V_1)V_2 + \alpha(V_2)V_1.$$ See \cite[\S5.9]{Blaom_12}. If we define
$A^0$ to be the anchor kernel of $A$, and $A^{-1}=A$, we get a firm Lie algebroid
filtration of $A$ with $|A|=1$. The corresponding graded Lie algebra bundle is
$$\graded(A) = TM \oplus \gl(TM) \oplus T^*\!M,$$
where $TM$ and $T^*\!M $ are commutative, $\gl(TM) \oplus T^*\!M $ is the Lie algebra
bundle semi-direct product, and $TM$ and $\gl(TM) \oplus T^*\!M $ are ideals, by
Proposition \eqrefs{filtered}{rt2}.

While not immediately obvious, $\graded(A)$ has a second, natural, {\em semisimple,} Lie
algebra bundle structure, modeled on $\speciallinear(n + 1, {\mathbb R})$,
$n= \dimension(M)$, and compatible with the grading. The bracket for this structure, which
we will denote $T$, extends the existing bracket on $\graded(A^0)=\gl(TM) \oplus T^*\!M $
with the new relations
\begin{equation}
  T(C, V) = C V, \quad
    T(V, \alpha) = j(\alpha)V, \label{projective_bracket}
\end{equation}
for sections $C$, $V$ and $\alpha $ of $\gl(TM)$, $TM$ and $T^*\!M $ respectively.
Significantly, this bracket coincides with the flat one referenced in \ref{eg2}, when $M$
is the projective sphere, viewed as a homogeneous
$\operatorname{SL}(n + 1, {\mathbb R})$-space.

The fact that the Lie algebra $\speciallinear(n + 1, {\mathbb R})$ for the full symmetry
of the homogeneous model persists in some form in the ``curved'' case is somewhat surprising but
crucial, and is shared by every concrete geometric structure underlying a parabolic
geometry.

\subsection{Conformal structures}\label{conformal}
Let $B \subset J^1(TM)$ be the abstract homogeneous PDE you solve to find the
infinitesimal isometries of a conformal structure on $M$ \cite[Section
12]{Blaom_12}.\footnote{Alternatively, we may identify $B$ with the Lie algebroid of the
  Lie groupoid of all relative conformal frames.} Then $B$ is a transitive subalgebroid
whose anchor kernel $\co(TM)\subset \gl(TM)$ consists of the infinitesimal conformal
automorphisms of tangent spaces. Now $B$ acts on $TM$ via restricted adjoint action, and
so acts on $T^*\!M $. Regarding $T^*\!M $ as a commutative Lie algebra bundle, we
construct, in the category of Lie algebroids, the semi-direct product
$A = B \oplus_{\adjoint} T^*\!M $ (what we shall come to call the {\df prolongation} of
$B$). Then, if we put $A^1 = T^*\!M $, $A^0 = \co(TM) \oplus T^*\!M$ (the anchor kernel of
$A$), and $A^{-1}=A$, then $A$ becomes a firmly filtered Lie algebroid with $|A| = 1$. The
corresponding graded Lie algebra bundle is
$$\graded(A)=TM \oplus \co(TM) \oplus T^*\!M,$$ where $\co(TM) \oplus T^*\!M $ is the
semi-direct product, and $TM$ and $\co(TM) \oplus T^*\!M $ are ideals.

Again, there is also a semisimple Lie algebra bundle structure on $\graded(A)$ compatible
with the grading, in this case modeled on $\so(n + 1, 1)$, and coinciding with the flat
one of \ref{eg2} when $M$ is the conformal sphere, viewed as a homogeneous
$\operatorname{SO}(n + 1, 1)$-space. After redefining the injection $j$ above,
\begin{equation*}
  j(\alpha)(V_1, V_2) = \alpha(V_1) V_2 + \alpha(V_2)V_1 -
  \sigma(V_1,V_2)\sigma^{-1}(\alpha),
\end{equation*}
now a map
$j \colon T^*\!M\rightarrow (S^2(T^*\!M)\otimes TM) \cap (T^*\!M \otimes \co(TM)) $, the
semisimple bracket $T$ extends the existing bracket on
$\graded(A^0)=\co(TM) \oplus T^*\!M$, with the same additional relations
\eqref{projective_bracket}.  Here $\sigma $ is any metric in the conformal class.

\vspace{\baselineskip} The semisimple Lie algebra bundles $TM \oplus \gl(TM)\oplus T^*\!M$
and $TM \oplus \co(TM)\oplus T^*\!M$ above are examples of infinitesimal Levi structures,
defined in \ref{levi_}.

\refreshpage
\section{Cartan geometries \`a la Lie groupoids}\label{cartan_geometries}
In this section we define Cartan geometries using the language of Lie groupoids,
recovering Ehresmann's principal bundle notion as a special case. Our definitions rest on:
(i) the notion of a Cartan connection on a Lie groupoid, reviewed in more detail in
Section \ref{cartan_section}; and (ii) the new notion of compatible model data on the
corresponding Lie algebroid. Mild, verifiable, sufficient conditions for the Cartan
connection to be {\em uniquely} determined by compatible model data are then established in the
transitive case (Proposition \ref{separating}). Model curvature is defined, and related
to the curvature of the Lie algebroid connection.

\subsection{Definitions}\label{preview}
Suppose that $G$ is a Lie group acting smoothly on a manifold $M$, possibly in a
non-transitive fashion.  We are interested in ``deformed'' versions of the action groupoid
${\mathcal G} := G \times M$, and its Lie algebroid $A := {\mathfrak g} \times M$, and to
that end we identify two structures: (i) The canonical horizontal $n$-plane field $D$ on
${\mathcal G} $, tangent to the foliation $G \times \{m\}$, $m \in M$ --- an example of a
Lie groupoid Cartan connection; and (ii) The trivial Lie algebra bundle structure on the
infinitesimalization $A = {\mathfrak g} \times M$ of ${\mathcal G} $ --- an example of
model data.

If ${\mathcal G} $ is arbitrary Lie groupoid, then a horizontal $n$-plane field $D$ on
${\mathcal G} $ is a {\df Cartan connection} if it is suitably compatible with the Lie
groupoid structure. It's
infinitesimalization may be regarded as a Koszul connection $\nabla $ on $A$, and will be
suitably compatible with the Lie algebroid structure, making it an {\df (infinitesimal)
  Cartan connection} on $A$. The curvature of $\nabla $ is an obstruction to
symmetry: When $\nabla $ is flat, $A$ is a (possibly twisted) action algebroid and, under
certain technical conditions, ${\mathcal G} $ is a (possibly twisted) action groupoid. For
details and variations on these results, see Section \ref{cartan_section}.

Associated with any Cartan connection $D$ is a representation $\bar D$ of ${\mathcal G} $
on $A$, extending the canonical action $\Adjoint$ of ${\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel
of $A$. Conversely, in the transitive case, every such extension is $\bar D$ for some
uniquely determined Cartan connection $D$ (Proposition \ref{cartan}). One may view
$\bar D$ as a kind of proxy for the adjoint representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on its Lie
algebroid (which does not generally exist, unless ${\mathcal G} $ is a Lie {\em group}).

Similarly, there is associated with any infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$, a
representation $\bar\nabla $ of $A $ on itself, extending the canonical representation of
$A $ on the anchor kernel. In the transitive case, every such extension is $\bar \nabla$
for some uniquely determined Cartan connection $\nabla$ (Proposition \ref{hsd}). If
$(A, \nabla)$ is the infinitesimalization of some $({\mathcal G}, D)$ as above, then
$\bar\nabla$ is the infinitesimalization of the preceding global representation $\bar
D$. In any case, it is related to $\nabla $ by a simple formula,
\begin{equation}
  \label{horse}
  \bar\nabla_XY = \nabla_{\#Y}X +[X, Y],
\end{equation}
useful in computations. Here $\#$ denotes the anchor. One may similarly view $\bar\nabla $
as a proxy for the infinitesimal adjoint representation of $A$ on itself.

A smooth field $\tau $ of Lie brackets on a Lie algebroid $A$ is {\df model data} if
$\tau(X,Y) = [X, Y]$ whenever $X$ and $Y$ both lie in the kernel of the anchor of $A$. In
particular, if $A$ is transitive, any Lie algebra bundle structure on $A$ extending the
canonical Lie algebra bundle structure of the anchor kernel will be model data, and
without further comment, in transitive cases model data will always be assumed to be of
this type. We say that $\tau $ {\df has Lie algebra model} ${\mathfrak g} $ if every fibre
of $A$, with the Lie algebra structure defined by restricting $\tau $, is isomorphic to
${\mathfrak g} $.

An {\df infinitesimal Cartan geometry} on $M$ will be a Lie algebroid $A$ over $M$,
equipped with an infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla $ and model data $\tau $ that are
{\df compatible} in the sense that both these conditions hold:
\begin{conditions}
  \item[{\bf c1.}] $\tau $ is $\bar\nabla$-invariant.
  \item[{\bf c2.}] $\bar\nabla_ZX = \tau(Z, X)$ whenever $\#Z=0$, $X \in A$.
\end{conditions}
Here $\# \colon A \rightarrow TM $ denotes the anchor. A {\df Cartan geometry} will be a
Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ over $M$, equipped with a Cartan connection $D$ and model
data $\tau$ on its Lie algebroid $A$ that are {\df compatible}, in the sense that
\begin{conditions}
      \item[{\bf C1.}] $\tau $ is $\bar D$-invariant,
\end{conditions}
and c2 holds, where $\nabla $ is the infinitesimalization of $D$.

Evidently, the infinitesimalization $(A, \nabla, \tau)$ of any Cartan geometry
$({\mathcal G}, D, \tau)$ is an infinitesimal Cartan geometry. Also, it is readily
verified that action groupoids are Cartan geometries and infinitesimal action algebroids
are infinitesimal Cartan geometries. We refer to these geometries as the {\df flat
  models}.

If only c1 (or C1) is known to hold, then $\tau $ is {\df weakly compatible} with $\nabla$
(resp., $D$). For emphasis, we sometimes use ``strongly compatible'' as a synonym of
``compatible''.

\subsection{Compatibility}
We now elaborate on condition c2, whose relevance is less obvious than c1 and C1. Notice
first that when a Lie algebroid $A$ is equipped with model data $\tau $ and an
infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$, then $A$ acts on itself in {\em two} distinct
ways: first, we have the the Lie algebroid representation $\bar\nabla$; second we have the
representation $X \mapsto \adjoint^\tau_X = \tau(X,\,\cdot\,)$ of $A$ on itself as a
bundle of Lie algebras. Condition c2 is simply the assertion that every element of the
anchor kernel of $A$ acts the same way in both these cases. (Elements of the kernel act on
{\em elements} of $A$, not merely on sections.) As already noted, c2 holds in the flat models,
which is some justification for including it. However, also:
\begin{conditions}
\item Both C1 and c2 hold for classical Cartan geometries (Proposition
  \ref{cartan_geometries_defined}).
\item In the transitive case, we need c2 for model curvature to be a two-form on $M$
  (see \ref{model_curvature_general}).
\item For so-called {\em separating} model data, C1 already implies c2 (Proposition
  \eqrefs{separating}{nuf1}) and this will apply in many interesting examples, including
  parabolic geometries (Proposition \ref{separating_}).
\end{conditions}
This last observation notwithstanding, we have the following counterexample:
\begin{example}[Weak compatibility without strong compatibility]
  Let $M$ be a copy of ${\mathbb R} $ and $G$ the mutliplicative group $(0, \infty)$. Then
  we have the trivial, transitive Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} := M \times G \times M$
  \cite[Example 1.1.7]{Mackenzie_05}, with Lie algebroid $A := TM \times {\mathfrak g} $,
  ${\mathfrak g} = {\mathbb R} $. If $\frac{\partial}{\partial m}$ denotes the constant
  vector field on $M$, then, by the transitivity, a Cartan connection $D$ on
  ${\mathcal G} $ is defined implicitly by
  $\bar D_{(m_2, g, m_1)}(\frac{\partial}{\partial m} (m_1), \xi) =
  (\frac{\partial}{\partial m} (m_2), \xi)$. Viewing a section of $A$ as a pair $(u, X)$
  of functions, $u \colon M \rightarrow {\mathbb R}$, and
  $X \colon M \rightarrow {\mathfrak g}$, we have
  $\bar\nabla_{(u, X)}(v, Y) = (u \frac{\partial v}{\partial m}, u\frac{\partial
    Y}{\partial m})$, and define $\bar D$-invariant model data $\tau $ by
  $\tau((u, X), (v, Y)) = (0, uY - vX)$. For any ${\mathfrak g}$-valued function $X$ on
  $M$, $(0, X)$ is a section of the anchor kernel.  But
  $\bar\nabla_{(0, X)}(u, Y) = \tau((0, X), (v, Y)) $ only if $vX = 0$, which shows
  that c2 fails.
\end{example}

\subsection{Classical Cartan geometries}\label{cartan_geometries_defined}
We next show that our Cartan geometries include classical Cartan geometries as
a special case (a fact we will not use elsewhere).

Let $G$ be a Lie group with Lie subgroup $H$ and let $\pi \colon P \rightarrow M$ be a
left principal $H$-bundle. Let $\omega $ be a ${\mathfrak g}$-valued one-form on $P$
satisfying: (i) $\omega \colon {T_mM} \rightarrow {\mathfrak g}$ is an isorphism at every
$m \in M$ ($\omega $ is a parallism); (ii) For each $X \in {\mathfrak g} $,
$\omega^{-1}(X)$ is the corresponding infinitesimal generator for the action of $H$ on
$P$; and (iii) $\omega(v \cdot h) = \Adjoint_{h^{-1}} \omega(v)$, for all $v \in TM$,
$h \in H$ ($\omega$ is $H$-equivariant). Then $\omega $ a Cartan connection in the sense
of Ehresmann \cite{Ehresmann_51}.

Take ${\mathcal G} $ to be gauge groupoid $(P \times P)/H$, which has $A = TP /H$ as
Lie algebroid. On account of transitivity, and the $H$-equivariance of $\omega$, a Cartan
connection $D$ on ${\mathcal G} $ is defined implicitly by
$\bar D_{[p_2, p_1]_H}[v_1]_H = [(\omega(p_2)^{-1}\circ\omega(p_1))(v_1)]_H$, for
$v_1 \in T_{p_1}P$ and $p_1, p_2 \in P $. Here $[\,\cdot\,]_H$ denotes equivalence
modulo $H$.\footnote{For a construction that avoids reference to $\bar D$, see
  \cite{Blaom_16b}, which already establishes a one-to-one correspondence between Cartan
  connections on transitive Lie groupoids and classical Cartan connections {\em stripped
    of model data}.}

The $H$-equivariance of $\omega $ shows that model data $\tau $ on $A$ is well-defined by
\begin{equation}
  \label{defmodel}
  \tau([u]_H, [v]_H) = \omega(p^{-1})[\omega(p)(u), \omega(p)(v)]_{\mathfrak g}; \qquad u, v \in T_p P.
\end{equation}
It follows immediately from the definition that $\tau $ is weakly compatible with $D$
(condition C1).

The following is just a basic fact about Ehresmann-Cartan connections:
\begin{lemma}
  For any $H$-invariant vector field $U$ on $P$, and vertical vector field $V $ on
  $P $, one has
  ${\mathcal L}_V(\omega(U)) = [\omega(V), \omega(U)]_{\mathfrak g}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
  Since ${\mathcal L}_{fV}(\omega(fU)) = f {\mathcal L}_V(\omega(U))$, for any smooth
  function $f$, it suffices to suppose $V$ is an infinitesimal generator for the action of
  $H$. The result now follows easily from the $H$-equivariance of $\omega $.
\end{proof}
%
\begin{proposition}
  In the scenario just described for classical Cartan geometries, the model data $\tau $
  is compatible with $D$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}[Proof of proposition]
  It remains to prove compatibility condition c2. For a section $X$ of $A = T P /H$, let
  $\hat X$ denote the corresponding $H$-invariant vector field on $P$, so that
  $[\hat X(p)]_H=X(\pi(p))$. Then we may express \eqref{defmodel} as
  $\omega(\widehat{\tau(Z, X)}) = [\omega(\hat Z), \omega(\hat X)]_{\mathfrak g}$, for
  $X, Z \in \Gamma(A)$. On the other hand, it is not too difficult to compute the
  infinitesimalization $\bar\nabla $ of $\bar D$: it is characterized by the identity
  $\omega(\widehat{\bar\nabla_ZX}) = {\mathcal L}_{\hat Z}\omega(\hat X)$. Supposing
  $Z \in \Gamma(A)$ lies in the anchor kernel, $\hat Z$ will be
  vertical, and the lemma delivers
  ${\mathcal L}_{\hat Z}(\omega(\hat X))=[\omega(\hat Z), \omega(\hat X)]_{\mathfrak
    g}$. Combining all these observations shows that
  $\omega(\widehat{\bar\nabla_ZX}) = \omega(\widehat{\tau(Z, X)})$, which establishes c2.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Separating model data}\label{separating}
We call a subalgebra ${\mathfrak h} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ {\df separating} if it is
separating for the automorphism group $\automorphism({\mathfrak g})$, which is to say,
that if any two automorphisms agree on ${\mathfrak h} $, then they must agree on all of
${\mathfrak g}$. Equivalently, the requirement is that the only automorphism restricting
to the identity on ${\mathfrak h} $ is the identity.

Similarly, we say that ${\mathfrak h} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is {\df infinitesimally
  separating} if the only derivation vanishing on ${\mathfrak h} $ is the trivial
derivation. Any separating subalgebra is automatically infinitesimally
separating. Parabolic subalgebras of reductive Lie algebras are always separating
(Proposition \ref{separating_}).

\begin{example}[Model data for Riemannian geometry]
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be the semidirect product
  ${\mathfrak g} = \so(n) \times {\mathbb R}^n$. Then $\so(n) \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is
  separating.
\end{example}

Model data $\tau $ on $A$ will be {\df separating} if at every point $m \in M$, the anchor
kernel of $A$ sitting over $m$ is a separating subalgebra of $A|_m$, for the Lie bracket
defined by $\tau $.

\begin{proposition}
  Let $\tau $ be separating model data on a Lie algebroid $A$. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item If $\tau$ is weakly compatible with an infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$
    then it is strongly compatible. Similarly, if $D$ is a Cartan connection on a Lie
    groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ integrating $A$, then weak compatibility of $\tau$ with $D$
    implies strong compatibility. \label{nuf1}
  \item If $A$ is transitive, there can exist at most one infinitesimal or global Cartan
    connection compatible with $\tau $.\label{nuf2}
  \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
  \eqref{nuf1}: If $A$ is integrated by a Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ and $\tau$ is
  weakly compatible with $D$, then it is weakly compatible with its infinitesimalization
  $\nabla $. So to prove \eqref{nuf1}, it suffices to consider the infinitesimal case. Fix
  some $Z$ lying in the kernel ${\mathfrak h}$ of $\# \colon A|_m \rightarrow T_mM$. Then
  we may view $\bar\nabla_Z$ as a linear map $A|_m \rightarrow A|_m$. In fact, with
  respect to the bracket $\tau $, this is a Lie algebra derivation, because $\tau $ is
  $\bar\nabla $-invariant, assuming weak compatibility. But $\adjoint_Z^\tau$ is also a
  derviation of $A|_m$, and on ${\mathfrak h} $ these two derivations coincide, by
  \eqrefs{preview}{horse} and the definition of model data. Since $\tau $ is separating,
  ${\mathfrak h} \subset A|_m$ is a separating subalgebra, with respect to the bracket
  defined by $\tau $, and so infinitesimally separating. It follows that the derivations
  agree on $A|_m$, which proves compatibility condition c2, and hence strong compatbility.

  \eqref{nuf2}: Suppose that $\nabla^1$ and $\nabla^2$ are Cartan connections weakly
  compatible with model data $\tau$ on $A$, and write
  $\delta_XY = \bar\nabla^1_XY - \bar\nabla^2_XY$. For each $X \in A|_m$, $m \in M$, we
  may regard $\delta_X$ as a linear map $A|_m \rightarrow A|_m$ and, because $\tau $ is
  both $\bar\nabla^1$ and $\bar\nabla^2$-invariant (weak compatibility) it follows that
  $\delta_X$ is even a Lie algebra derivation, with respect to the bracket $\tau $. Since
  $\bar\nabla^1$ and $\bar\nabla^2$ both extend the canonical representation of $A$ on its
  anchor kernel, $\delta_X$ vanishes on ${\mathfrak h} \subset A|_m$ defined as above. By
  the same argument as before, $\bar\nabla^1$ and $\bar\nabla^2$ must coincide. In the
  transitive case, an infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$ is determined uniquely by
  the corresponding representation $\bar\nabla$, so $\nabla^1 = \nabla^2$.

  Turning to the global case, suppose $ D^1$ and $D^2$ are Cartan connections weakly
  compatible with model data $\tau $ and write
  $\Delta_gY = \bar D^1_{g^{-1}} \bar D^2_g Y$. For each arrow $g \in {\mathcal G} $
  beginning at some $m \in M$, we may regard $\Delta_g$ as a vector space automorphism
  $A|_m \rightarrow A|_m$ and, because $\tau $ is both $\bar D^1$ and $\bar D^2$-invariant
  (weak compatibility) it follows that $\Delta_g$ is a Lie algebra automorphism, with
  respect to the bracket $\tau$. Since both $\bar D^1$ and $\bar D^2$ both extend the
  canonical representation of $\mathcal G$ on the anchor kernel, $\Delta_gZ = Z$ for all
  $Z$ in ${\mathfrak h} \subset A|_m$ defined as above. Since $\tau $ is separating,
  $\Delta_g$ must be the identity on $A|_m$, proving $\bar D^1 = \bar D^2$. In the
  transitive case, a Cartan connection $D$ is determined uniquely by the corresponding
  representation $\bar D$, so $D^1 = D^2$.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Model curvature}\label{model_curvature_general}
Consider an infinitesimal Cartan geometry $(A, \nabla)$, such as the
infinitesimalization of some global Cartan geometry. While the curvature of the
infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$ is an obstruction to symmetry, and makes sense
without the prescription of any model data, {\df model curvature} is more amenable to
normalization, as we shall see concretely in the case of parabolic geometry.

In the general case the model curvature is a section of $\wedge^2{\mathcal D}^*\otimes A$,
where ${\mathcal D} \subset TM$ is the image of the anchor. However, for simplicity, we
only define it here in the case that $A$ is {\em transitive}: it is then the section
$\kappa$ of $\wedge^2 T^*\!M \otimes A$ defined by
\begin{equation*}
  \kappa(\#X, \#Y) = \torsion \bar\nabla (X, Y) - \tau(X, Y); \qquad X, Y \in \Gamma(A).
\end{equation*}
Here $\#$ is the anchor and
$$\torsion \bar\nabla(X, Y) = \bar\nabla_XY - \bar\nabla_XY - [X, Y] = \nabla_{\#Y} X -
\nabla_{\# X} Y + [X, Y].$$ That $\kappa $ is well defined follows from
\eqrefs{preview}{horse} and compatability condition c2.

Basic properties of curvature, such as a
transformation rule and the Bianchi identity, are established in Section
\ref{modelcurvature}; see also Theorem \ref{transform_}.

The model curvature is the Lie algebroid analogue of ``curvature'', as that term is
generally understood in the Cartan geometry literature.

\begin{proposition}
  The model curvature $\kappa $ of a transitive infinitesimal Cartan geometry
  $(A, \nabla)$ determines the curvature of $\nabla $ according to
  \begin{equation*}
    \curvature \nabla (U, V)X = (\bar\nabla_X \kappa)(U, V).
  \end{equation*}
  In particular, if $\kappa =0$, then $\curvature \nabla =0$ and
  $\torsion \bar\nabla =\tau $.
\end{proposition}
\noindent The consequences of $\curvature \nabla =0$ will be briefly reviewed in
\ref{curvature}.

According to the proposition, $\kappa $ is also an obstruction to symmetry, but of the
kind prescribed {\em a priori} by the choice of the Lie algebra model for $\tau $. As
Theorem \ref{curvature} will show, the model curvature measures the difference between a
``deformed'' Lie bracket $\torsion \bar\nabla$, defined by the Cartan connection, and a
bona fide Lie bracket, prescribed by the model data $\tau$. This nice interpretation is
obscured in the standard principal bundle approach.

\begin{proof}[Proof of proposition]
  For any Cartan connection $\nabla $ on a transitive Lie algebroid, one has the easily
  derived identity,
  \begin{equation}
    \label{solidarity}
    \curvature\nabla(\#X, \#Y)Z =  \bar\nabla_Z\torsion \bar\nabla (X, Y).
  \end{equation}
  See, for example, \cite[\S5.4]{Blaom_06}. From the definition of $\kappa $,
  \begin{equation}
    \label{poland}
    (\bar\nabla_Z\kappa)(\#X, \#Y) = (\bar\nabla_Z\torsion \bar\nabla)(X, Y) -
    (\bar\nabla_Z \tau)(X, Y).
  \end{equation}
  The stated identity follows from \eqref{solidarity}, \eqref{poland} and the
  compatability condition c1.
\end{proof}

\section{Parabolic geometries. Part I}\label{parabolic_summary}
In this section we define parabolic geometries,
certain reductions thereof, called Levi structures, and outline our main results about
them. Proofs appear after various restatements given in Section \ref{parabolic_proofs}.

\subsection{Parabolic subalgebras and parabolic filtrations}\label{canonical_filtrations_}
Following \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}, we call a Lie subalgebra
${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ {\df parabolic} if it contains the normalizer of a
maximial nil subalgebra. Facts about parabolic subalgebras stated below without proof are
detailed in Appendix \ref{lie_algebra_facts}. In this section, ${\mathfrak g} $ is always
a finite-dimensional Lie algebra over a field of characteristic zero, such as
${\mathbb R} $ or ${\mathbb C} $.

\begin{theorem}[Theorem \ref{parabolic_characterizations}] Suppose ${\mathfrak g} $ is
  reductive and fix a non-degenerate trace-form, which always exists. Then
  ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is parabolic if and only if its polar
  ${\mathfrak p}^\perp$, which does not depend on the choice of form, is nilpotent and
  lies in the semisimple part $[{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$ of ${\mathfrak g}$. In that
  case ${\mathfrak p} $ is self-normalizing, ${\mathfrak p}^\perp $ is an ideal of
  ${\mathfrak p} $, and ${\mathfrak p}/{\mathfrak p}^\perp $ is reductive.
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
See \eqref{qq4} below and Theorem \ref{parabolic_characterizations} for other
characterizations of parabolic subalgebras.
\begin{remark}
  In fact, ${\mathfrak p}^\perp$ will be the so-called nilpotent radical of
  ${\mathfrak p}$ (see \ref{nilpotency}), and ${\mathfrak p}/{\mathfrak p}^\perp$ is
  known as the {\df reductive Levi quotient} of ${\mathfrak p}$. We won't need this here,
  but it explains the name ``Levi structure'' introduced later.
\end{remark}

To connect parabolic subalgebras with filtrations and gradings, we recall a general method
for constructing Lie algebra filtrations from more primitive data. For a subalgebra
${\mathfrak n} \subset {\mathfrak g} $, the map
${\mathfrak s} \mapsto \adjoint_{\mathfrak n}({\mathfrak s}):=[{\mathfrak n}, {\mathfrak
  s}]$ on subspaces ${\mathfrak s} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ has a right-inverse,
${\mathfrak s} \mapsto \{X \in {\mathfrak g} \suchthat [{\mathfrak n}, X] \subset
{\mathfrak s}\}$. This motivates the construction in the following lemma:

\begin{lemma}[Proposition 2.4 of \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}]
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be an arbitrary Lie algebra and
  ${\mathfrak g}^0, {\mathfrak g}^1 \subset {\mathfrak g}$ subalgebras. Define two
  sequences of subspaces
  $$ \cdots {\mathfrak g}^{-3} \supset {\mathfrak g}^{-2} \supset {\mathfrak g}^{-1}
  \supset {\mathfrak g}^0\quad\text{and}\quad {\mathfrak g}^1 \supset {\mathfrak
    g}^2\supset {\mathfrak g}^3 \supset \cdots$$ by inductively defining
  ${\mathfrak g}^j = [{\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g}^{j-1}]$ for $j\ge 2$ and
  ${\mathfrak g}^j = \{X \in {\mathfrak g}\suchthat [{\mathfrak g}^1, X] \subset
  {\mathfrak g}^{j+1}\}$ for $j \le -1$.
  % defining
  % ${\mathfrak g}^{-j} := \adjoint^{-j}_{{\mathfrak g}^1}({\mathfrak g}^0)$ for
  % $-j \le -1$, and ${\mathfrak g}^j := \adjoint^{j-1}_{{\mathfrak g}^1}({\mathfrak g}^1)$,
  % for $j \ge 2$.
  We do not require ${\mathfrak g}^0\supset {\mathfrak g}^1$. However, if
  ${\mathfrak g}^1 \subset N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak g}^0)$, then
  $[{\mathfrak g}^j, {\mathfrak g}^k] \subset {\mathfrak g}^{j+k}$ for all
  $j,k \in {\mathbb Z}$.
\end{lemma}
\noindent%
In particular, if ${\mathfrak g}^1$ is an ideal of ${\mathfrak g}^0$, then the proposition
applies and we call the sequence
$\cdots {\mathfrak g}^{-3} \supset {\mathfrak g}^{-2} \supset {\mathfrak g}^{-1} \supset
{\mathfrak g}^0\supset{\mathfrak g}^1 \supset {\mathfrak g}^2\supset {\mathfrak g}^3
\supset \cdots$ the {\df pre-filtration} associated with
$({\mathfrak g}^0, {\mathfrak g}^1)$.

\begin{proposition}[Proposition \ref{can}, Corollary \ref{can}, Proposition \ref{in_terms_of_gradings}, Proposition \ref{separating_}]
  Fix a non-degenerate trace-form on a reductive Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g}$. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{qq1} For any parabolic subalgebra ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $,
    the pre-filtration of ${\mathfrak g} $ associated with
    $({\mathfrak p},{\mathfrak p}^\perp) $, which is independent of the choice of form, is
    a bone fide Lie algebra filtration (it ascends to $0$ and descends to ${\mathfrak g}$)
    with $|{\mathfrak g}| = m $ for some $m\ge 0$ (see Notation \ref{filtered}). This is
    the {\df canonical filtration} associated with ${\mathfrak p}$.
  \item\label{qq1b} The associated graded $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ admits a unique grading
    element $E \in [\graded({\mathfrak g}), \graded({\mathfrak g})]$. In particular,
    ${\mathfrak g} $ and $\graded({\mathfrak g}) $ are isomorphic Lie algebras (by
    arguments recalled in \ref{splittings}).
  \item Every parabolic subalgebra of ${\mathfrak g}$ is separating and contains
    $Z({\mathfrak g})$.\label{cando}
  \item\label{qq4} A subalgebra ${\mathfrak p} $ is parabolic if and only if there exists
    a grading of ${\mathfrak g} $ such that $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$ and
    ${\mathfrak p} = \oplus_{j\ge 0} {\mathfrak g}_j$. In that case the corresponding
    filtration, defined by ${\mathfrak g}^j:=\oplus_{i\ge j}{\mathfrak g}_i$, is the
    canonical filtration associated with ${\mathfrak p} $ if and only if ${\mathfrak g}_1$
    generates ${\mathfrak g}^1$, or equivalently, if
    ${\mathfrak g}^1 \supset {\mathfrak g}^2 \supset {\mathfrak g}^3 \supset \cdots $ is
    the lower central series of ${\mathfrak g}^1$.
  \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}
\noindent%
The condition $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$ is necessary: Define a grading of
${\mathfrak g} =\gl({\mathbb C}, 2)$ by declaring ${\mathfrak g}$ to have, as the
non-trivial components, the one-dimensional subspaces ${\mathfrak g}_{-2}$,
${\mathfrak g}_{-1}$, ${\mathfrak g}_0$ and ${\mathfrak g}_1$, spanned respectively by
\begin{equation*}
 \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0\\0 & 1\end{bmatrix}, \quad
 \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0\\-1 & 0\end{bmatrix}, \quad
 \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0\\0 & -1\end{bmatrix} \quad\text{and}\quad
 \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 1\\0 & 0\end{bmatrix}.
\end{equation*}
Then ${\mathfrak g}_0 \oplus {\mathfrak g}_1$ cannot be parabolic, because it does not
contain the centre of $\gl({\mathbb C}, 2)$, violating \eqref{cando}.  In a filtered
reductive (even semisimple) Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$, the subalgebra ${\mathfrak g}^0$
need not be parabolic: Take ${\mathfrak g}$ to be $\speciallinear(2, {\mathbb C})$,
${\mathfrak g}^{-1}={\mathfrak g} $, ${\mathfrak g}^1=0$, and ${\mathfrak g}^0$ to be the
one-dimensional subalgebra of diagonal matrices.

\begin{definition}
  An arbitrary filtration of a reductive Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g} $ will be called {\df
    parabolic} if ${\mathfrak p}:={\mathfrak g}^0$ is parabolic, and the filtration is the
  canonical filtration associated with ${\mathfrak p} $.
\end{definition}

\subsection{Filtered Lie groupoids}\label{filtered_groupoids}
We will call a Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ over $M$ {\df filtered} if its associated Lie
algebroid $A$ is filtered. Now ${\mathcal G} $ acts on elements of the anchor kernel of
$A$, and so on elements of $A^0$; if $A$ is firmly filtered then this action of
$\mathcal G$, denoted $\Adjoint$, will {\em infinitesimally} preserve $A^0$ and its
filtration, but need not do so globally. However, if it does, then $\mathcal G$ is said to
be {\df firmly filtered}.

\subsection{Generalized flag manifolds}\label{parabolic_}
Suppose that $G$ is a reductive Lie group and $P \subset G$ a parabolic subgroup. By
definition, this means $P$ has a parabolic Lie algebra
${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ and that the associated canonical filtration of
${\mathfrak g} $ is $P$-invariant under $\Adjoint$ (it is automatically
${\mathfrak p} $-invariant under $\adjoint$). The quotient $M=G/P$ is known as a {\df
  generalized flag manifold}. The projective and conformal spheres are
examples. Generalized flag manifolds constitute the flat models of parabolic geometries.

The $P$-invariance of the canonical filtration leads to a firm filtering of the action
algebroid $A := {\mathfrak g} \times M$, as described in \ref{eg2}. It is easy to see that
the action groupoid ${\mathcal G} := G \times M $ is also firmly filtered.

\subsection{Parabolic geometries defined}\label{parabolic_defined}
Suppose $A$ is a filtered Lie algebroid. Then model data $\tau$ on $A$ is {\df compatible}
with the filtration if this is also a filtration with respect to $\tau$, i.e.,
$\tau(X, Y) \in A^{j+k}$ whenever, $X \in A^j$ and $Y \in A^k$ have a common base
point. An (infinitesimal) Cartan connection $D$ (resp., $\nabla)$ is {\df compatible} with
the filtration if the filtration is $\bar D$-invariant (resp., $\bar\nabla$-invariant).

A {\df parabolic geometry} is a transitive, firmly filtered, Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $
over some smooth manifold $M$, with associated Lie algebroid denoted $A$, and
filtration-compatible model data $\tau $ on $A$, such that:
\begin{conditions}
    \item[{\bf P1.}] The Lie algebra model for $\tau $ is reductive.\label{parabolic2}
    \item[{\bf P2.}] $A^0$ is the kernel of the anchor of $A$. \label{parabolic0}
    \item[{\bf P3.}] With respect to $\tau $, the filtration of $A$ is fibre-wise
      parabolic, in the sense of Definition
      \ref{canonical_filtrations_}. \label{parabolic1}
    \item[{\bf P4.}] There exists a Cartan connection $D$ on ${\mathcal G} $
      compatible with $\tau $.\label{parabolic4}
\end{conditions}
\noindent%
In particular, a parabolic geometry is a Cartan geometry in the sense of \ref{preview}.
\begin{proposition}%
  \mbox{}
  \begin{conditions}
  \item Assuming $M$ is connected, it suffices to check P3 holds over
    a single point $m \in M$.\label{soot1}
  \item $D$ is necessarily compatible with the filtration of $A$.\label{soot3}
  \item It suffices there exists a Cartan connection $D$ that is {\em weakly}
    compatible with $D$.\label{soot2}
  \item $D$ is unique. \label{soot4}
  \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  For \eqref{soot1}, see \ref{parabolic}, and for \eqref{soot3}, see Corollary A,
  subsection \ref{all}. By P1, P2, and P3, and Proposition
  \eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{cando}, the model data $\tau $ is separating. So
  \eqref{soot2} and \eqref{soot4} follow from Proposition \ref{separating}.
\end{proof}

An {\df infinitesimal parabolic geometry} consists of a transitive, firmly filtered Lie
algebroid $A$, and filtration-compatible model data $\tau $, satisfying the same
conditions given above, but with the Cartan connection $D$ in P4 replaced with an
infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$. An analogue of the proposition holds except that
for \eqref{soot1} we need to assume $A$ is integrable. Evidently, every parabolic geometry
has a corresponding infinitesimalization.

Parabolic geometries are generalized by {\df filtered Cartan geometries}, introduced in
Section \ref{filtered_cartan}.

\subsection{Regularity}\label{regularity_}
Let $A$ be any {\em transitive} filtered Lie algebroid. In that case the anchor
$\# \colon A \rightarrow TM$ induces an isomorphism
$\oplus_{i<0}\graded(A)_i \cong \graded(TM)$, and model data $\tau $ compatible with the
filtration is said to be {\df regular} if the induced bracket on $\graded(TM)$ is minus
one times the canonical bracket (the one induced by the Jacobi-Lie bracket on vector
fields).\footnote{The minus sign is connected to the convention adopted here,
  and often in the Lie groupoid literature, that brackets on the Lie algebras of Lie groups are
  defined by {\em right}-invariant vector fields; most of the parabolic geometry literature, including \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}, adopts the opposite convention.} It is not too hard to show that the
canonical model data associated with any filtered Klein geometry, and in particular the
canonical model data associated with a generalized flag manifold, is regular (Proposition
\ref{homogeneous}).

A parabolic geometry is {\df regular} if the associated model data $\tau $ is regular.

\subsection{Reduction}\label{reduction_}
The concrete manifestations of parabolic geometries (their ``underlying geometric
structures'') are typically obtained by a applying a reduction procedure.  Let
$({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a parabolic geometry and $D$ the compatible Cartan connection.

\begin{proposition}[Proposition \ref{subgroups}]
  In a parabolic geometry $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ let
  ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ denote the source-connected subgroupoid
  integrating $A^1$.  Then ${\mathcal G}^1 $ is a bundle of simply-connected nilpotent Lie
  groups; it is also a normal subgroupoid of ${\mathcal G}$, so that
  ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ is a Lie groupoid, also transitive.
\end{proposition}

\begin{remark}
  It follows from Remark \ref{canonical_filtrations_} that that $A^0/A^1$ is fibre-wise
  reductive, so that ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ has reductive vertex groups.
\end{remark}

Since we suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is firmly filtered, we have a grading-preserving
representation, $\Adjoint$, of ${\mathcal G} $ on $\graded(A^0)$.  Moreover, it is easy to
see that ${\mathcal G}^1$ acts trivially here (Proposition \eqrefs{weyl2}{xerox1}).  So
$\Adjoint$ descends to a representation of ${\mathcal H} := {\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$
on $\graded(A^0)$. This canonical representation extends to a representation of
${\mathcal H}$ on $\graded(A)$, which we denote by $R^D$, induced by the
filtration-preserving representation $\bar D$ of ${\mathcal G} $ on $A$.

The observations of the preceding paragraph carry over to the canonical, infinitesimal
representation, $\adjoint$, of $A$ on $A^0$, delivering a canonical representation of
$B := A/A^1$ on $\graded(A^0)$ and an extension $R^\nabla $ of this representation to one
of $B$ on $\graded(A)$, which are simply infinitesimalizations of the global ones:
$R^\nabla =dR^D$.

If we write $T := \graded_0(\tau)$, then $(\graded(A), T)$ is a graded Lie algebra bundle
with the same reductive Lie algebra model as $(A, \tau )$, by Proposition
\eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{qq1b}, which shows, moreover, that
$\graded(A^1)=\oplus_{i\ge 1}\graded(A)_i$ must be orthogonal to the parabolic
$\graded(A^0)=\oplus_{i\ge 0}\graded(A)_i$, for any non-degenerate trace-form for $T$. By
transitivity, we have $\oplus_{i\le -1}\graded(A)_i \cong \graded(TM)$, so we obtain a
representation of ${\mathcal H} $ on $\graded(TM)$. On the other hand, we can use a
non-degenerate trace-form for $T$ to identify $\graded(A^1)$ with
$(\oplus_{i\le -1}\graded(A)_i)^*\cong \graded(TM)^*\cong\graded(T^*\!M)$. Under this
identification, the representation $R^D$ of ${\mathcal H} $ on $\graded(T^*\!M)$ will be
the dual of the one on $\graded(TM)$, if we suppose that the trace-form is
$R^D$-invariant, which (with the help of transitivity) can always be arranged, on account
of the $R^D$-invariance of $T$, which follows from the $\bar D$-invariance of
$\tau$. Finally, we notice that $A^0/A^1 = B^0$, where $B^0$ is the anchor kernel of $B$.

In summary, we have
\begin{equation}
  \graded(A) \cong \graded(TM) \oplus B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M),\label{soldering}
\end{equation}
supporting the reductive Lie algebra bundle structure $T$ (c.f.~Examples \ref{projective}
and \ref{conformal}) and with ${\mathcal H}$ acting on each component.

\begin{remark}
  By our preceding Remark, $B^0$ is a fibre-wise reductive Levi subalgebra of the
  parabolic $B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M)$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Levi structures}\label{levi_}
A {\df Levi structure} consists of a filtered, transitive, Lie groupoid ${\mathcal H}$,
whose Lie algebroid $B$ has anchor kernel $B^0$ and satisfies $B \le 0$ (see Notation
\ref{filtered}), together with a reductive Lie algebra bundle structure $T$ on
  \begin{equation}
    \graded(TM) \oplus B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M)\label{fug_}
  \end{equation}
  and a representation $R$ of ${\mathcal H}$ on the same, such that:
  \begin{conditions}
      \item $R$ preserves the obvious grading of \eqref{fug_}.
      \item $R$ extends the canonical representation of ${\mathcal H} $ on the anchor
        $B^0$ of its infinitesimalization $B$, while its restrictions to representations
        on $\graded(TM)$ and $\graded(T^*\!M)$ are dual.
      \item $T$ is $R$-invariant. \label{tai}
      \item\label{levi3_} $T$ extends the canonical Lie algebra bundle structure on $B^0$,
        i.e., $T(C, C') = [C, C']$, while $T $ and $R$ are compatible, in the sense that
        $T(C, X) = dR_C X$ and $T(C, Z) = -(dR_C)^*Z$. Here $C, C' \in \Gamma(B^0)$,
        $X \in \Gamma(\graded(TM))$, and $Z\in \Gamma(\graded(T^*\!M))$.

      \item The grading of \eqref{fug_} is compatible with $T$, and, under
        iterations of the bracket $T$, $\graded(T^*\!M)$ is fibre-wise generated by
        $\graded(T^*\!M)_1$.\label{levi6}
      \item The natural pairing of $\graded(TM)$ and $\graded(T^*\!M)$ coincides with that
        induced by some non-degenerate $R$-invariant trace-form form for
        $T$.\label{killing_duality}
      \item\label{hust} With respect to $T$, the fibre-wise centre of \eqref{fug_} lies in
        $B^0$.
\end{conditions}
\noindent Notice that \eqref{hust} and Proposition \eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{qq4}
ensure that $B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M) $ is fibre-wise $T$-parabolic; \eqref{levi6}
ensures that the filtration defined by the obvious grading of \eqref{fug_} is the
associated canonical filtration associated with $B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M) $. (By Remark
\ref{canonical_filtrations_}, $B^0$ is a fibre-wise reductive Levi subalgebra, and in
particular, the vertex groups of ${\mathcal H} $ are necessarily reductive.)  By
Proposition \eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{qq1b} there will be a unique fibre-wise
grading element $E \in \Gamma(B^0)$; the uniqueness and \eqref{tai} ensure that $E$ is
${\mathcal H}$-invariant.

It is not required that $T$ restrict to the canonical Lie algebra bundle structure on
$\graded(TM)$ (here $TM$ is getting the filtration pushed down by the anchor of
$B$). However, if this restriction is the {\em negative} of the canonical one, then the
geometry is {\df regular}.

\begin{proposition}
  Let ${\mathcal {\mathcal H}}$, $T$ and $B$ assume the meanings of \ref{reduction_}, and
