  let $R:= R^D$ be the grading-preserving representation of ${\mathcal H}$ on
  $\graded(TM) \oplus B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M)$ described there. Then
  $({\mathcal H}, T, R)$ is a Levi structure called the {\df Levi reduction} of
  $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  Our observations under \ref{reduction_} establish everything that is not obvious, with
  the exception of property \eqref{levi3_}, which follows from the strong
  compatibility of $\tau $ and $D$.
\end{proof}

If $B$ in the definition of a Levi structure is an arbitrary Lie algebroid (not
necessarily integrated by some Lie groupoid ${\mathcal H} $) and $R$ now denotes some {\em
  infinitesimal} action of $B$ on \eqref{fug_}, and we replace $dR$ in \eqref{levi3_} with
$R$, then the preceding conditions define an {\df infinitesimal Levi structure} and, as we
will see later, the immediately preceding proposition has an infinitesimal analogue. We
have already encountered simple examples of an infinitesimal Levi structures, namely for
projective and conformal geometry. It is easy to see these examples
globalize.\footnote{However, these two examples prove ultimately to be of different
  character. The uniqueness Theorem \ref{uniqueness} stated later applies in the conformal
  case but not in the projective case. This is reflected in the fact that the Levi
  structure for projective geometry is the same {\em for every choice of projective
    connection}.} For example we have:
\begin{example}[Conformal structures]
  Define $B \subset J^1 (TM) $ and $T$ as in \ref{conformal} and filter $B$ trivially (see
  \ref{trivial}). If we let ${\mathcal H} \subset J^1 (M \times M)$ denote the Lie
  groupoid of one-jets of local diffeomorphisms preserving the conformal structure to
  first order, which has $B$ as its Lie algebroid, and let ${\mathcal H} $ act on
  $\graded(TM)=TM$ by restricted adjoint action, and extend this to a grading-preserving
  action $R$ on \eqref{fug_} in the obvious way, then $({\mathcal H}, T, R) $ becomes a
  Levi structure.
\end{example}
\noindent%
In the next example the base manifold $M$ is non-trivially filtered:
\begin{example}[$n$-plane fields with growth vector $(n, n(n +1)/2)$] Consider, on a
  smooth manifold of dimension $n(n + 1)/2$, an $n$-plane field ${\mathcal D} \subset TM$
  that is maximally non-integrable. That is, at every $m \in M$, local sections of
  ${\mathcal D} $ and their iterated Jacobi-Lie brackets, generate $T_mM$. Unlike contact
  structures, such an $n$-plane field is a geometric structure of finite type, admitting a
  classical normalized Cartan connection, as Bryant already showed
  when $n=3$ \cite{bryant_79,bryant_2006}.

  The quotient $TM/{\mathcal D}$ and $\wedge^2 {\mathcal D}$ have the same rank, and, by
  the assumption of maximal non-integrability, the morphism
  $V_1\wedge V_2 \mapsto [V_1, V_2] \modulo {\mathcal D} \colon \wedge^2 {\mathcal D}
  \rightarrow TM/{\mathcal D}$ is surjective, and whence an isomorphism.  Taking
  $T^{-1}M = {\mathcal D} $ and $T^{-2}M=TM$, we make $TM$ into a filtered Lie algebroid
  with $ -2 \le TM \le -1$ and, on account of the isomorphism, have the identification
  $\graded(TM) \cong \wedge^2 {\mathcal D} \oplus {\mathcal D} $ with grading-compatible
  bracket characterized by
  \begin{equation}
  [X_1, X_2] = X_1\wedge X_2;\qquad  X_1, X_2 \in \Gamma({\mathcal D}).\label{brillig}
  \end{equation}

  To construct a Levi structure from this data, equip
  ${\mathcal D} \oplus ({\mathbb R} \times M) \oplus {\mathcal D}^*$ with a real quadratic
  form $(\,\cdot\,,\,\cdot\,)$, of signature $(n+1, n)$, by declaring ${\mathcal D} $ and
  ${\mathcal D}^*$ to be isotropic,
  ${\mathcal D} \perp ({\mathbb R} \times M) \perp {\mathcal D}^*$, $(X, Z) = X(Z)$, and
  $(f_1, f_2) = f_1f_2$; here $X$ lies in ${\mathcal D} $, $Z$ in ${\mathcal D}^*$ and
  $f_1, f_2$ are smooth real functions. Next, define a linear  vector bundle isomorphism
  $\rho$, from
  \begin{equation}
    \label{lab}
     \wedge^2 {\mathcal D} \oplus {\mathcal D} \oplus \gl({\mathcal D}) \oplus {\mathcal
      D}^* \wedge^2 {\mathcal D}^* \cong \graded(TM) \oplus \gl({\mathcal D}) \oplus \graded
      (T^*\!M)
  \end{equation}
  onto $\so({\mathcal D} \oplus ({\mathbb R} \times M) \oplus {\mathcal D}^*)$, the
  $\so(n + 1, n)$-bundle of fibre-wise isometries of $(\,\cdot\,,\,\cdot\,)$, by declaring
  \begin{align*}
     \rho(C)(Y, f, W) &= \big(CY, 0, -C^*W\big)\\
     \rho(X)(Y, f, W) &= \big(fX, -W(X), 0\big)\\
     \rho(X_1\wedge X_2)(Y, f, W) &= \big(W(X_2)X_1-W(X_1)X_1, 0, 0\big)\\
     \rho(Z)(Y, f, W) &= \big(0, Z(Y), -fZ\big)\\
     \rho(Z_1\wedge Z_2)(Y, f, W) &= \big(0, 0, Z_1(Y)Z_2-Z_2(Y)Z_1\big).
  \end{align*}
  Here $C$ is a section of $\gl({\mathcal D})$, $X, X_1, X_2, Y$ are sections of
  ${\mathcal D} $ and $Z, Z_1, Z_2, W$ are sections of ${\mathcal D}^*$.  In
   matrix notion we can write this as
  \begin{equation*}
    \rho(X_1\wedge X_2, X, C, Z, Z_1\wedge Z_2)=
    \begin{bmatrix}
      C & X & -X_1\wedge X_2\\ Z & 0 & -X^{**}\\ Z_1\wedge Z_2 & -Z^{**} & -C^*.
    \end{bmatrix},
  \end{equation*}
  where on the right, $X_1\wedge X_2$, $Z_1\wedge Z_2$, $X^{**}$ and $Z^{**}$ are
  interpreted, in the obvious way, as sections of
  $\homomorphism({\mathcal D}^*, {\mathcal D})$,
  $\homomorphism({\mathcal D}, {\mathcal D}^*)$,
  $\homomorphism({\mathcal D}^*, ({\mathbb R} \times M))$ and
  $\homomorphism(({\mathbb R} \times M), {\mathcal D}^*)$ respectively.  We equip
  \eqref{lab} with the obvious grading and make $\rho $ into a representation of
  \eqref{lab} on ${\mathcal D} \otimes ({\mathbb R} \times M) \otimes {\mathcal D}^*$ by
  introducing the grading-compatible bracket $T$ on \eqref{lab} generated by the relations
  \begin{gather*}
    T(X_1, X_2) = - X_1\wedge X_2, \qquad  T(C, X) = R_CX := CX,\\
    T(C, X_1 \wedge X_2) = R_CX_1, \wedge X_2 + X_1 \wedge R_CX_2 = R_C(X_1 \wedge X_2), \\
    T(Z_1, Z_2) = Z_1 \wedge Z_2, \qquad  T(C, Z) = R_CZ := -C^*Z,\\
    T(C, Z_1 \wedge Z_2) = R_CZ_1 \wedge Z_2 + Z_1 \wedge R_C Z_2 = R_C(Z_1 \wedge Z_2),\\
    T(X, Z) = Z \otimes X,\\
    T(X_1 \wedge X_2, Z_1 \wedge Z_2) = Z_1(X_1)Z_2 \otimes X_2 + Z_2(X_2)Z_1\otimes X_1\\ -Z_2(X_1)Z_1 \otimes X_2 - Z_1(X_2)Z_2 \otimes X_1,\\
    T(X_1 \wedge X_2, Z) = Z(X_1)X_2 - Z(X_2)X_1, \enspace
    T(Z_1 \wedge Z_2, X) = Z_2(X)Z_1 - Z_1(X)Z_2.
  \end{gather*}
  This makes \eqref{lab} into a $\so(n + 1, n)$-bundle. In passing, we note that
  $\gl({\mathcal D}) \oplus {\mathcal D}^* \oplus \wedge^2 {\mathcal D}^*$ is the
  stabilizer of the maximally isotropic subbundle
  ${\mathcal D}^* \subset {\mathcal D} \oplus ({\mathbb R} \times M) \oplus {\mathcal D}^*
  $.

  Since grading components of index $i$ and $j$ are necessarily $B_\rho$-orthogonal if
  $i + j \ne 0$ (Proposition \eqrefs{par}{ira1}) the following sufficiently describes the
  trace-form $B_\rho $
  \begin{align*}
    B_\rho(C_1, C_2) &= 2\trace(C_1C_2)\\
    B_\rho(Z, X) &= 2Z(X)\\
    B_\rho(Z_1 \wedge Z_2, X_1\wedge X_2) &= 2Z_1(X_1)Z_2(X_2) - 2Z_1(X_2)Z_2(X_1),
  \end{align*}
  as is readily computed. This shows that $B_\rho$ is non-degenerate. To satsify
  \eqref{killing_duality}, we instead adopt the trace-form $B_{\rho/\sqrt{2}}=B_\rho/2$.

  The frame groupoid ${\mathcal H} $ of ${\mathcal D} $ acts on each summand in
  \eqref{lab} in an obvious way, and hence on \eqref{lab} in a grading-preserving way ---
  we denote this representation by $R$. The filtration of $TM$ pulls back, via anchor, to
  a trivially firm filtration of the Lie algebroid $B$ of ${\mathcal H} $, with the
  additional understanding that $B^0$ is the anchor kernel, and $B^j=0 $ for positive
  $j$. The triple $({\mathcal H}, R, T)$ is now easily seen to be Levi structure. Note we
  have made choices in the definition of $\rho$ that ensure the sign in the first relation
  for $T$ is opposite to that in \eqref{brillig}, to ensure regularity.
\end{example}

Unfortunately for us, the analogue of a {\em global} Levi structure is called an
``infinitesimal flag structure'' in \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}. Moreover, from the present point
of view, ``infinitesimal flag structure'' seems a name more apt for the action algebroids
described in \ref{eg2}, specialized to the parabolic case. To avoid confusion, we
eschew the term altogether.

\subsection{Prolonging a Levi structure to a parabolic geometry}\label{existence}
Our proof of the following basic existence theorem is new:
\begin{theorem}[Theorem \ref{existence_}]
  Every Levi structure $({\mathcal H}, T, R)$ is, up to isomorphism, the reduction of some
  parabolic geometry $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$. If the Levi structure is regular, then the
  parabolic geometry can be chosen to be regular.
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
Morphisms of Levi structures are defined in \ref{functor}.

The model data $\tau $ is not uniquely defined by the Levi structure, but determined up to
choices of a suitable tensor
${\mathsf P} \in \Gamma(\graded(T^*\!M)\otimes \graded(T^*\!M))$ called the {\df Rho
  tensor}, splitting of the anchor $\# \colon B \rightarrow TM $, and splitting of the
filtration of $TM$. Here $B$ is the Lie algebroid of ${\mathcal H}$. Suitable
normalization of this data is addressed in \ref{normalization} below.

The key idea in the theorem's proof is to observe that every parabolic geometry
$({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ has a semi-direct product structure, which follows from the
guaranteed existence of Weyl gauges, a notion introduced in Section
\ref{semi-direct}. Specifically, the representation $R^D$ of ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$
on $\graded(T^*\!M)$, defined by the $\tau $-compatible Cartan connection $D$, integrates
to an action of ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ on $\exp(\graded(T^*\!M))$, the Lie group
bundle with simply-connected nilpotent model that integrates the nilpotent Lie algebra
bundle $\graded(T^*\!M)$; and one obtains, after fixing a gauge, an isomorphism
\begin{equation*}
    {\mathcal G} \cong {\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1 \times_{R^D} \exp(\graded(T^*\!M)),
\end{equation*}
where $\times_{R^D}$ indicates semi-direct product. This follows from Theorem \ref{weyl2},
given later.

To recover the underlying Lie groupoid $\mathcal G$ associated with some parabolic
geometry having $({\mathcal H}, T, R)$ as reduction, we accordingly define
${\mathcal G} = {\mathcal H} \times_R \exp(\graded(T^*\!M)) $, which we call the {\df
  prolongation} of the Levi structure. The more technical task is to reconstruct
suitable model data $\tau $ such that $\graded_0(\tau) = T$. In fact, we state and prove a
more general result, Theorem \ref{abstract_levi_}, characterizing arbitrary transitive
filtered Cartan geometries. This result includes an explicit description of $\tau $
and a compatible Cartan connection $D$, in terms of the aforementioned Rho tensor
${\mathsf P}$ and splittings.

\subsection{Normalization of curvature}\label{normalization}
As we have observed, while subject to the constraint $\graded_0(\tau) = T$, there is
considerable freedom in the choice of model data $\tau $ appearing in the existence
Theorem \ref{existence}. We want to choose $\tau $ so as to ``normalize'' the model
curvature $\kappa$.  This curvature, defined in \ref{model_curvature_general}, is a
section of $\wedge^2 T^*\!M \otimes A$. However, from the point of view of normalization,
there is a more convenient representation.  Since the filtration of $A$ is parabolic
(Axiom P3), we have $A^1 = (A^0)^\perp$, where $\perp$ is with respect to any
non-degenerate trace-form for $\tau $. The $\bar D$-invariance of $\tau $ means we can
always find a $\bar D$-invariant such trace-form, and such forms are completely determined
by their restriction to a single fibre of $A$. Fixing such a form, we use it to identify
$(A/A^0)^*$ with $A^1$. But, by transitivity, $A/A^0\cong TM $, because $A^0$ is the
anchor kernel (Axiom P2), and we conclude $T^*\!M \cong A^1$. In this way, we may view
$\kappa$ as a section of $\wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A$. This representation is ambiguous only
up to the choice of a non-degenerate trace-form on some fixed fibre $(A_{m_0}, \tau)$.

As the bracket $\tau $ makes the vector bundle $A$ into a representation of the Lie
algebra bundle $A^1$, $\wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A $ appears in a natural chain complex
\begin{equation}
  \label{chain1_} 0 \leftpartial{\tau} A \leftpartial{\tau} A^1 \otimes A
\leftpartial{\tau} \wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A \leftpartial{\tau} \cdots.
\end{equation} Over each point $m \in M$, this is the classical chain complex associated
with the representation $\adjoint^\tau$ of the Lie algebra $A^1|_m$ on $A|_m$.
Following \cite{Cap_Slovak_09},
we will refer to $\partial^\tau $ as the {\df Kostant codifferential}.
Basic facts about such complexes immediately carry
over to the case of Lie algebra bundle representations and we use such facts without
further comment. By definition (c.f.,
\cite[p.~262]{Cap_Slovak_09}),
%
\begin{multline}
  \label{form1}
  \partial^\tau(Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n \otimes X) =
  \sum_{i=0}^n(-1)^{i+1} Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge \widehat{Y_i} \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n
  \otimes \tau(Y_i,X)\\ +\sum_{i<j}(-1)^{i+j}\tau(Y_i,Y_j) \wedge Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge
  \widehat{Y_i} \wedge \cdots \widehat{Y_j} \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n \otimes X.
\end{multline}
%
For example,
$\partial^\tau(Y_0\wedge Y_1 \otimes X) = Y_0 \otimes \tau(Y_1, X) - Y_1 \otimes \tau(Y_0,
X) -\tau(Y_0, Y_1)\otimes X$. Incidentally, appealing to compatibility Axiom c2 (see
\ref{preview}), we can express $\partial^\tau $ in terms of the representation $\bar\nabla$ instead,
\begin{multline*} \partial^\tau(Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n \otimes X) =
\sum_{i=0}^n(-1)^{i+1} Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge \widehat{Y_i} \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n
\otimes \bar\nabla_{Y_i}X\\ +\sum_{i<j}(-1)^{i+j}[Y_i,Y_j] \wedge Y_0 \wedge \cdots \wedge
\widehat{Y_i} \wedge \cdots \widehat{Y_j} \wedge \cdots \wedge Y_n \otimes X,\label{form1}
\end{multline*}
but emphasize $\partial^\tau $ is not a differential operator, only an algebraic one.

We say model data $\tau $ in a parabolic geometry is {\df normal} if
$\partial^\tau \kappa =0$.  Our proof of the following is an adaption of that of
\cite[Propositions 3.1.10, 3.1.13]{Cap_Slovak_09}:

\begin{theorem}[Theorem \ref{normalization_}]
  Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a regular parabolic
  geometry. Then there exists {\bfseries normal}, model data $\tau'$ such that
  $({\mathcal G} , \tau')$ is a regular parabolic geometry with the same Levi reduction.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Harmonic curvature}\label{harmonic}
In a regular parabolic geometry with normal model data $\tau $, the homology class
$\kappa_{\mathrm H} := [\kappa] \in H_2(A^1, A)$ is called the {\df harmonic
  curvature}. While it seems the harmonic curvature contains less information, it
  is nevertheless a complete obstruction to flatness, as the corollary below shows.

It is not too hard to see that the filtration of $A$ induces a filtration of
$H_k(A^1, A)$. However, according to \eqref{poker2} below, something much stronger and
useful is true:

\begin{theorem}[Proposition \ref{homology}, Proposition \ref{top_curvature}]
  Let $\kappa $ be the model curvature of a regular and normal parabolic geometry
  $({\mathcal G} , \tau)$, and let $k \ge 0$ be any integer. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
     \item In a natural representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on $H_k(A^1, A)$ defined by the
    compatible connection $D$, the subgroupoid ${\mathcal G}^1$ acts trivially, i.e.,
    $H_k(A^1, A)$ is a representation of ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$.\label{poker1}
  \item $H_k(A^1, A)$ admits a canonical {\bfseries grading} compatible with it's natural filtration,
    allowing harmonic curvature to be unambiguously split into grading components,
    $\kappa_{\mathrm H} = \sum_{i\ge 1}(\kappa_{\mathrm H})_i$.\label{poker2}
  \item If $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l \ge 1$ and $(\kappa_{\mathrm H})_l = 0$,
    then $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$.\label{poker3}
  \end{conditions}
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
One says {\df $\kappa$ is homogeneous of degree $l$} if $\kappa$ can be written as a
finite sum of terms of the form $X\wedge Y \otimes Z$ where $X$, $Y$, $Z$ lie in $A^j$,
$A^k$, $A^m$, respectively, with $j+k+m \le l$; equivalently, if we view $\kappa $ as a
section of $\wedge^2 T^*\!M \otimes A$, if $\kappa(X, Y)$ lies in $A^{j+k+l}$ whenever $X$
and $Y$ lie in $A^j$ and $A^k$ respectively. See also \cite[\S3.1.1,
p.~235]{Cap_Slovak_09}.

We obtain the following by applying \eqref{poker3} inductively:
\begin{corollary}[c.f.~Theorem 3.1.12, p.~265, \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}]
  In a normal, regular parabolic geometry, $\kappa_{\mathrm H} =0$ implies $\kappa =0$
  (and hence also $\curvature \nabla =0$).
\end{corollary}

The harmonic curvature $\kappa_{\mathrm H}$ is a section of $H_2(A^1, A)$, which is a
representation of a transitive groupoid, and can accordingly be understood as an
associated bundle; it's splitting into grading components can be understood by studying a
single fibre, which will be a representation of the (reductive) vertex group of
${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$. More generally, each fibre of $H_j(A^1, A)$, $j \ge 0$, is
of the form $H_j({\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g})$, where ${\mathfrak g} $ is the reductive
Lie algebra model for the Lie algebra bundle structure $\tau $, which gets a canonical
filtration, because $A$ is parabolically filtered; the subalgebra ${\mathfrak g}^0$ will
be parabolic, its orthogonal (with respect to any non-degenerate trace-form) is
${\mathfrak g}^1$. Fixing a lift $\epsilon $ of the unique grading element
$E \in \graded({\mathfrak g})$, we can write ${\mathfrak g}$ as a graded Lie algebra,
${\mathfrak g}_{-k} \oplus \cdots \oplus {\mathfrak g}_k$, $k\ge 0$ (Proposition
\eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{qq1b}). This allows us to introduce the {\em co}homology
$H^j({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})$, where
${\mathfrak g}_- = {\mathfrak g}_{-k}\oplus \cdots \oplus {\mathfrak g}_{-1}$. (This
space, in turn, is the model for the bundle of cohomologies for a cochain complex
$\wedge^\bullet \graded(T^*\!M)\otimes \graded(A)$ that will enter in the proof of the
uniqueness result described next.) According to (a mild generalization) of Kostant's
version of Hodge theory in this setting \cite{Kostant_61} (see also Proposition
\ref{hodge}), there is an identification
$H_j({\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g}) \cong H^j({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})$, which
does not depend on the choice of $\epsilon $, and the grading of the space on the right
gives a filtration-compatible grading of the space on the left, corresponding exactly to
the grading in \eqref{poker2} (but for which we will give a direct argument).  In the
semisimple case, Kostant's theorem, restated in \cite[Theorem 3.3.5,
p.~351]{Cap_Slovak_09}, computes the cohomology $H^j({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})_l$,
for each $l \in {\mathbb Z} $, for complex ${\mathfrak g} $; some additional arguments
extend the result to the real case. We refer the reader to \cite{Cap_Slovak_09} for
extensive details. It seems one ought to be able to generalize these results to the
reductive case, but we have not attempted to do that here.

\subsection{Uniqueness up to Weyl gauge transformations}\label{uniqueness}
The inner automorphisms of an arbitrary Lie groupoid are those given by conjugation by
bisections. When ${\mathcal G} $ is firmly filtered, a special role is played by those
inner automorphisms generated by bisections of ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G}$, the
bundle of simply-connected nilpotent Lie groups integrating the totally intransitive Lie
subalgebroid $A^1 \subset A$, where a bisection is the same thing as a section. We call
such automorphisms {\df Weyl gauge transformations}, for reasons explained in
\ref{gauge_transformations}. By Proposition \ref{reduction_}, there is, for each such
transformation, some $Z \in \Gamma(A^1)$ such that each arrow $g \in {\mathcal G} $,
beginning at some $m \in M$, is mapped to $\exp(Z(gm))g\exp(-Z(m))$. This conjugation has
derivative
$$X \mapsto e^{-\adjoint_Z}X:=X - [Z, X] + \frac{1}{2!}[Z, [Z, X]] - \cdots\enspace,$$
and morphisms of a filtered Lie algebroid $A$ of this form will be called {\df
  infinitesimal Weyl transformations}.\footnote{The minus sign enters here because of our
  convention that right-invariant vector fields define the Lie bracket on the Lie algebra
  of a Lie group.} Both global and infinitesimal Weyl gauge transformations cover the
identity of the base $M$, and $e^{-\adjoint_Z}$ is filtration-preserving, by F1. Evidently,
in a global parabolic geometry, every infinitesimal Weyl transformation is integrated by
a unique global Weyl gauge transformation.

\begin{example}
  Let $A$ be a filtered Lie algebroid with $|A| = 1$. Then the infinitesimal Weyl gauge
  transformations have the form $X \mapsto X + [Z, X]$, for some $Z \in \Gamma(A^1)$.
\end{example}

\begin{proposition}[Corollary of Proposition \ref{transform}]%
  If $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ is a (regular) parabolic geometry, and $\tau'$ the
  push-forward of $\tau $ under a Weyl gauge transformation, then $({\mathcal G}, \tau')$
  is another (regular) parabolic geometry with the same Levi reduction.
\end{proposition}

According to the next result, normal model data is uniquely determined, up to Weyl gauge
transformations, provided a Lie cohomological condition is satisfied. Our proof of the
following is an adaption of that of \cite[Proposition 3.1.14]{Cap_Slovak_09}:

\begin{theorem}[Theorem \ref{un}] Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a regular parabolic
  geometry and let ${\mathfrak g} $ be Lie algebra model for $\tau $, identified with a
  graded reductive Lie algebra, as detailed at the end of \ref{harmonic}. Assume
  $H^1({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1=0$.  Then if $({\mathcal G}, \tau')$ is second
  parabolic geometry with $\graded_0(\tau')=\graded_0(\tau)$, then if both $\tau $ and
  $\tau'$ are normal, then $\tau' = q_*\tau$ for some infinitesimal Weyl gauge
  transformation $q$.
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
Here
$H^1({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1=\oplus_{l \ge 1}H^1({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak
  g})_l$.

\subsection{The equivalence problem}\label{equivalence}
The parabolic geometries over $M$ form a category if we define a morphism of parabolic
geometries $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ and $(\tilde {\mathcal G} , \tilde \tau)$ over $M$ to be
a Lie groupoid morphism $Q \colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow \tilde {\mathcal G} $ covering
the identity, whose infinitesimalization $q=dQ \colon A \rightarrow \tilde A$ is
filtration-preserving and pushes $\tau $ to $\tilde \tau$. One can similarly make the
collection of Levi structures over $M$ into a category; for details see \ref{functor}.  As
for parabolic geometries, we can similarly identify a graded reductive Lie algebra model
${\mathfrak g} $ for the Lie algebra bundle structure $T$ of a Levi geometry
$({\mathcal H}, R, T)$. The Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g} $ we associate with a Levi
reduction can be taken to be as the same Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g} $ we use for the
parabolic geometry that we are reducing. In a parabolic geometry the condition
\begin{equation}
  \label{coarse}
H^1({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1=0
\end{equation}
holds if and only if it holds for every geometry in the same isomorphism class, and the
same is true for Levi structures.

Combining Theorems \ref{existence}, \ref{normalization} and \ref{un}, and appealing to the
fact that prolongation is a functor (Theorem \ref{functor}), we establish the following
solution to the equivalence problem for certain Levi structures:

\begin{theorem}[Theorem \ref{equivalence_}]
  The map that sends a regular normal parabolic geometry over $M$
  to its corresponding Levi reduction, induces a bijection between: (i) isomorphism classes
  of regular normal parabolic geometries over $M$ satisfying \eqref{coarse}; and (ii)
  isomorphism classes of regular Levi structures over $M$ satisfying \eqref{coarse}.
\end{theorem}

\refreshpage
\section{Weyl gauges and semi-direct product structure}\label{semi-direct}
We now return to the general study of filtered Lie algebroids introduced in Section
\ref{introduction}, our main goal being the generalization of Theorem \ref{intro_prolong}
to Lie algebroids and Lie groupoids. The idea of the proof of Theorem \ref{intro_prolong}
is to show that the stabilizer $B \subset {\mathfrak a}$ of $\epsilon $ is a complement
for ${\mathfrak a}^1 $ in ${\mathfrak a} $, and that $B$ preserves the grading of
${\mathfrak a^1} $ determined by $\epsilon $, under the adjoint representation. We
suppress details, as we will provide them for the generalizations.

\vspace{0.5\baselineskip} The groupoids and algebroids in this section are not required to
be transitive and the only class of Lie groups or algebras playing any role are nilpotent ones.

\subsection{Splittings of a filtered Lie algebra}
\label{splittings}
If ${\mathfrak g}$ is a finite-dimensional filtered Lie algebra, then the existence of a
grading element $E \in \graded({\mathfrak g})$ has strong consequences for the Lie
algebra. For, choosing any $\epsilon \in {\mathfrak g}^0$ such that
$\graded_0(\epsilon) = E$, one can show, using induction and $\cap_j {\mathfrak g}^j=0$,
that the maps
$$ X \mapsto  [\epsilon, X] - jX \colon {\mathfrak g}^j \rightarrow {\mathfrak g}^{j+1}$$
are well-defined projections, whose kernels constitute a grading of ${\mathfrak g} $ for
which $\epsilon$ is a grading element; this in turn gives rise to an isomorphism
$\Phi_\epsilon\colon \graded({\mathfrak g}) \rightarrow {\mathfrak g}$ which must in fact be a
Lie algebra isomorphism. When we alter the choice of $\epsilon$, the isomorphism $\Phi_\epsilon$ changes
in a way made precise in \eqref{splittings2} below.

We call $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded({\mathfrak g}) \rightarrow {\mathfrak g} $ an {\em
  $E$-splitting} of the filtration. With $E$ and $\epsilon$ fixed as above, there is evidently a
one-to-one correspondence between $E$-splittings and the affine space
$\epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1$, because $\Phi_{\epsilon'}(E) = \epsilon'$ for all $\epsilon' \in \epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1$.

Now ${\mathfrak g}^1$ is a nilpotent Lie algebra, integrated by a simply connected
nilpotent Lie group, which we denote by $\exp({\mathfrak g}^1)$; nilpotency ensures the
exponential map is a diffeomorphism. Part \eqref{splittings1} of the lemma below shows
that $\exp({\mathfrak g}^1)$ acts freely and transitively on the set of $E$-splittings by
constructing a concrete realization $G^1$. A proof of this known result appears in
Appendix \ref{appendix_splittings}.
%
\begin{lemma}
  Let $\epsilon \in {\mathfrak g}^0 $ be a lift of some grading element
  $E \in \graded({\mathfrak g})$. Let $G^0$ be {\em any} Lie group integrating
  ${\mathfrak g}^0 $, and let $G^1 \subset G^0$ be the connected Lie subgroup with Lie
  algebra ${\mathfrak g}^1$. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item Under the adjoint action, $G^1$ acts freely and transitively on the affine space
    $\epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1\subset {\mathfrak g}^0$, and consequently on $E$-splittings of
    ${\mathfrak g} $. In particular, $G^1$ is simply connected (i.e.,
    $G^1 \cong \exp({\mathfrak g}^1)$) so that the infinitesimal adjoint action of
    ${\mathfrak g}^1$ on ${\mathfrak g} $ has a unique globalization
    $\Adjoint\colon G^1 \rightarrow \automorphism({\mathfrak g})$ extending the adjoint
    action just mentioned.\label{splittings1}
  % \item For any $g \in G^1$, $\Adjoint_g - \identity$ sends ${\mathfrak g}^j$ to
  %   ${\mathfrak g}^{j+1}$, for any $j \in {\mathbb Z}$, i.e., is homogeneous of degree
  %   one.\label{splittings30}
  % \item The only linear map $\varphi \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow {\mathfrak g} $ that
  %   commutes with $\adjoint_e$ and such that $\varphi - \identity$ is homogeneous of degree
  %   one is the identity.\label{splittings40}
  \item For any $\epsilon_1, \epsilon_2 \in \epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1$, we have
    $\Phi_{\epsilon_2} = \Adjoint_g\circ \Phi_{\epsilon_1}$, where $g \in G^1$ is the unique element for
    which $\epsilon_2 = \Adjoint_g \epsilon_1$. \label{splittings2}
  \end{conditions}
\end{lemma}

\subsection{Weyl gauges for filtered Lie algebroids}\label{weyl}
A {\df Weyl gauge} for a filtered Lie algebroid $A$ will be any section $\epsilon$ of
$A^0$ such that $E = \graded_0(\epsilon)$ is a fibre-wise grading section of the bundle of
Lie algebras $\graded(A^0)$, and
\begin{equation}
  [X, \epsilon] \in A^1 \enspace\text{for all $X \in A$.}\label{w2}
\end{equation}
If $A$ is firmly filtered, then the canonical action of $A$ on its anchor kernel
determines an action $\adjoint$ of $A$ on $\graded(A^0)$.  Equation \eqref{w2} then holds
if and only if $E$ is $\adjoint$-invariant. That is, in the firm case, Weyl gauges exist
if and only if an $\adjoint$-invariant fibre-wise grading section of $\graded(A^0)$
exists, and any lift to $A^0$ of such a section is a Weyl gauge. In fact, according to the
corollary stated below all Weyl gauges arise in this way.

Note that we do not require that $E$ be a fibre-wise grading section of
$\graded(A)$, only of $\graded(A^0)$ (but see also \ref{model_data_gradings}).

\begin{theorem}
  Let $A$ be a filtered Lie algebroid admitting a Weyl gauge $\epsilon$, and let $B \subset A$
  denote the isotropy of $\epsilon$ under the canonical representation of $A$ on its anchor
  kernel, i.e., the kernel of the map
  $X \mapsto [X, \epsilon] \colon A \rightarrow A^1$. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item $A$ is firmly filtered, and in particular, $A^1 \subset A$ is an ideal (i.e.,
    $\Gamma(A^1) \subset \Gamma(A)$ is an ideal).\label{pq1}
  \item $A$ is the internal semi-direct product of $B$ and $A^1$.\label{pq2}
  \item The grading $A^0=\oplus_{i\ge0}{\mathfrak a}_i$ associated with $\epsilon$ is
    invariant under the canonical representation of $B$. In particular, the splitting
    $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded(A^1)\rightarrow A^1$ intertwines the representation of
    $A/A^1\cong B$ on $\graded(A^1)$ with the representation of $B$ on $A^1$, so that $A$
    is the external semi-direct product of $A/A^1$ and $\graded(A^1)$.\label{pq3}
  \item Under the identification $A \cong A/A^1 \oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1)$ of
    \eqref{pq3}, the Weyl gauge $\epsilon$ takes the form $\epsilon=(E,0)$, for some section $E$ of
    $A^0/A^1 \subset A/A^1$ that commutes under Lie algebroid bracket with all
    sections of $A/A^1$; and $\adjoint_EY = jY$ for any $Y \in \graded(A^0)_j$.\label{pq4}
  \end{conditions}
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
Note that the identification $A \cong A/A^1 \oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1)$ transfers to
$A/A^1 \oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1)$ the following filtration:
$(A/A^1 \oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1))^i= (A^i/A^0)
\oplus_{\adjoint}(\oplus_{j\ge\max\{1,i\}}\graded(A)_j)$.

\begin{example}[Projective geometry] In example \ref{projective} the Lie algebroid
  $A \subset J^1 (TM)$ acts on $TM$ in the usual way, $\adjoint_{J^1 V}W = [V,W]$, which
  gives us associated representations of $A$ on $\gl(TM)$ and $T^*\!M $. On each component
  of $\graded(A^0)=\gl(TM)\oplus T^*\!M$ the infinitesimal adjoint action of $A$ is given
  by these representations, which shows that
  $E = -\identity \in \gl(TM) \subset \graded(A^0)$ is $\adjoint$-invariant. Consequently,
  any lift $\epsilon $ of $-\identity$ through $A^0 \rightarrow A^0/A^1 = \gl(TM)$ is a
  Weyl gauge for $A$. According to the theorem, each such gauge defines an identification
  of $A$ with the semi-direct product $J^1 (TM) \oplus_{\adjoint}T^*\!M $.
\end{example}

\begin{proof}[Proof of theorem]
  The isotropy $B \subset A$ is a subalgebroid, by Jacobi's identity. We first prove
  \begin{equation}
     A = B \oplus A^1, \label{y7}
  \end{equation}
  as vector bundles. It suffices to show that the restriction $r$ of
  $X \mapsto [X, \epsilon]$ to a map $A^1 \rightarrow A^1$ is an isomorphism. However, by
  F1, this restriction is filtration-preserving, and the corresponding map
  $\graded(A^1) \rightarrow \graded(A^1)$ sends $\xi \in \graded(A^1)_j$ to $j \xi$,
  because $\graded_0(\epsilon)$ is a fibre-wise grading section of $\graded(A^0)$. As this
  is obviously an isomorphism, $r$ must be too.

  Next we prove the grading $A^0=\oplus_{i\ge0}{\mathfrak a}_i$ associated with $\epsilon$ is
  invariant under the canonical representation of $B$. To that end, suppose
  $Y \in \Gamma({\mathfrak a}_j)$ for some $j\ge 0$ and let $X \in \Gamma(B)$ be
  arbitrary. The Jacobi identity for the bracket on $A$ gives us
  \begin{equation*}
    [X, [\epsilon, Y]] = [[X, \epsilon], Y] + [\epsilon, [X, Y]].
  \end{equation*}
  On the other hand, applying $\adjoint_X$ to both sides of the
  identity $[\epsilon, Y] = jY$, we have
  \begin{equation*}
     [X, [\epsilon, Y]] = j\adjoint_XY.
  \end{equation*}
  Combining these two results, we obtain
  \begin{equation*}
    [[X, \epsilon], Y] + [\epsilon, \adjoint_XY] = j \adjoint_XY.
  \end{equation*}
  But $[X, \epsilon] = 0 $, because $X \in \Gamma(B)$. We conclude that
  $\adjoint_XY \in \Gamma({\mathfrak a}_j)$.

  Having proven, in particular, that $B$ preserves the filtration of $A^0$, to establish
  \eqref{pq1} it suffices, in view of \eqref{y7}, to show that the filtration of $A^0$ is
  invariant under the canonical action of itself, which follows immediately from F1.

  Claim \eqref{pq2} follows from \eqref{pq1} and \eqref{y7}. The first part of \eqref{pq3}
  has already been addressed, and the rest now follows easily. Part \eqref{pq4} is now also
  clear.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}\label{ew1}
  A filtered Lie algebroid $A$ admits a Weyl gauge if and only if it is firmly
  filtered and $\graded(A^0)$ admits an $\adjoint$-invariant fibre-wise grading section $E$.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
  Given a firmly filtered Lie algebroid $A$ with $\adjoint$-invariant fibre-wise grading
  section $E$ of $\graded(A^0)$, any lift of $E$ to a section $\epsilon \in \Gamma(A^0)$
  is immediately seen to be a Weyl gauge. The converse statement follows from the theorem.
\end{proof}

If $A$ is a filtered Lie algebroid, then $A^1$, which lies in the anchor kernel of $A$, is
a bundle of nilpotent Lie algebras, and is accordingly integrated by a bundle of
simply-connected nilpotent Lie groups, denoted $\exp(A^1)$.

\begin{remark}
  By simple-connectivity, the adjoint action of $A^1$ on $A^0$ integrates to an action
  $\Adjoint$ of $\exp(A^1)$ on $A^0$. We have the identity
  $\Adjoint_{\exp(Z)}X=e^{-\adjoint_Z}X$, for any $Z \in \Gamma(A^1)$ and
  $X \in \Gamma(A^0)$, but the right-hand side makes sense for any $X \in \Gamma(A)$.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}
  Let $A$ be a filtered Lie algebroid admitting a Weyl gauge, and ${\mathcal G} $ any Lie
  groupoid integrating $A$. Then the source-connected Lie subgroupoid
  ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ integrating $A^1$ is a bundle of simply-connected
  nilpotent Lie groups isomorphic to $\exp(A^1)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
  We prove the first part of the conclusion, as the second part follows from the first, by
  Lie I for Lie groupoids. By Axiom F2, $A^0$ and $A^1$ lie in the kernel of the anchor of
  $A$, and so both are bundles of Lie algebras (totally intransitive). The
  source-connectedness of ${\mathcal G}^1$ now implies that ${\mathcal G}^1 $ is totally
  intransitive (its source and target fibres coincide) so that these fibres are connected
  Lie groups. These Lie groups are subgroups of the fibres of the source-connected
  subgroupoid ${\mathcal G}^0 \subset {\mathcal G}$ integrating $A^0$ (also a bundle of
  Lie groups). With a Weyl gauge $\epsilon$ in hand, we may apply Lemma
  \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings1} fibre-wise to show that these groups are in fact
  simply-connected and nilpotent.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Filtered Lie groupoids and Weyl gauges}\label{weyl2}
Let ${\mathcal G} $ be a filtered Lie groupoid over $M$. The filtration need not be firm;
see \ref{filtered_groupoids} for definitions. We call $\epsilon  \in \Gamma(A)$ a {\df Weyl gauge}
for ${\mathcal G} $ if it is a Weyl gauge for $A$, and if for every arrow
$g \in {\mathcal G}$ with source $m \in M$ we have
\begin{equation}\label{tui}
   \Adjoint_g \epsilon(m) - \epsilon(gm) \in A^1(gm),
\end{equation}
where $gm$ denotes the target of $g$. If ${\mathcal G} $ is firmly filtered, then this
condition holds if and only if $E= \graded_0(\epsilon)$ is ${\mathcal G}$-invariant under
$\Adjoint$, in which case the requirement \eqrefs{weyl}{w2} is automatic.

\begin{proposition}
  Let ${\mathcal G} $ be a filtered Lie groupoid with Lie algebroid $A$ and
  ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ the source-connected Lie subgroupoid integrating
  $A^1$.
  \begin{conditions}
    \item Under $\Adjoint$, ${\mathcal G}^1$ acts trivially on $\graded(A^0)$.\label{xerox1}
    \item If ${\mathcal G} $ admits a Weyl gauge then ${\mathcal G}^1$ is a bundle of
      simply-connected nilpotent Lie groups isomorphic to $\exp(A^1)$.\label{xerox2}
  \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  Part \eqref{xerox1} holds because $A^1$ already acts trivially on $\graded(A^0)$ by F1,
  and ${\mathcal G}^1$ has connected source-fibres. Part \eqref{xerox2} is an immediate
  corollary of Proposition \ref{weyl}.
\end{proof}

To navigate a mild obstruction to obtaining semi-direct products in the groupoid
case, we will say the filtration of $\mathcal G$ is {\df weakly firm} if $A^1$ is
${\mathcal G} $-invariant under $\Adjoint$. Evidently a firm filtration of
${\mathcal G} $ is weakly firm. (This notion will not be important for our later applications to parabolic geometry.)
\begin{remark}
  Suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is filtered and admits a Weyl gauge $\epsilon$. Then if $\mathcal G$ has
  connected source fibres, then we claim it is weakly firm. Indeed, $\epsilon$ will be a
  Weyl gauge for the Lie algebroid $A$ of ${\mathcal G} $, so that $A^1 \subset A$ must be an ideal, by
  Theorem \eqrefs{weyl}{pq1}. Our claim follows now from the connectedness assumption.
\end{remark}

Suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is firmly filtered, so that ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1 $ acts on
$\graded(A^0)$, by \eqref{xerox1}. This action, also denoted $\Adjoint$, preserves the Lie
algebra bundle bracket. Now, let $\exp(\graded(A^1))$ denote the totally intransitive Lie
groupoid with simply-connected fibres that integrates $\graded(A^1)$, a Lie algebra bundle
with nilpotent Lie algebra model. Then the action just mentioned integrates to an action
of ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ on $\exp(\graded(A^1))$, also denoted $\Adjoint$,
preserving the totally intransitive Lie groupoid structure, i.e., it maps each (nilpotent)
Lie group fibre isomorphically onto another Lie group fibre. We can accordingly define
the semi-direct product ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1 \times_{\Adjoint}\exp(\graded(A^1))$,
and have the following globalization of Theorem \ref{weyl}.

\begin{theorem}
  Let ${\mathcal G} $ be a weakly firm, filtered Lie groupoid with Lie algebroid
  $A$. Define ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ as in the proposition above, and
  suppose ${\mathcal G} $ admits a Weyl gauge $\epsilon$.  Then, if
  ${\mathcal H} \subset {\mathcal G} $ denotes the isotropy subgroupoid of $\epsilon$ under the
  canonical representation of ${\mathcal G} $, then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ is normal,\label{pr1}
  \item ${\mathcal G}={\mathcal G}^1{\mathcal H} $ is the internal semi-direct product of ${\mathcal H} $ and
    ${\mathcal G}^1$,\label{pr2}
  \item The grading $A^0=\oplus_{i\ge0}{\mathfrak a}_i$ associated with $\epsilon$ is
    invariant under the canonical representation of ${\mathcal H}$. \label{pr3}
  \item ${\mathcal G} $ is firmly filtered.\label{pr4}
  \item As a Lie groupoid, ${\mathcal G} $ is identifiable with the external semi-direct
    product ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1
    \times_{\Adjoint}\exp(\graded(A^1))$. Specifically, let
    $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded(A^1) \rightarrow A^1$ denote the splitting associated with
    $\epsilon$, and let $\tilde \Phi_\epsilon \colon \exp(\graded(A^1)) \rightarrow {\mathcal G}^1$ be
    the morphism of totally intransitive Lie groupoids that integrates $\Phi_e$.  For
    $g \in {\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$, define $g^{\mathcal H} \in {\mathcal H} $ by the
    requirement $g^{\mathcal H} {\mathcal G}^1 = g$.  Then the map
    $$(g, k) \mapsto \tilde \Phi_\epsilon(k) g^{\mathcal H} \colon {\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1
    \times_{\Adjoint}\exp(\graded(A^1)) \rightarrow {\mathcal G}$$ is a Lie groupoid
    isomorphism.\label{pr5}
  \end{conditions}
\end{theorem}
\begin{proof}
  Since we assume ${\mathcal G} $ is weakly firm, ${A^1 \subset A}$ is invariant under the
  canonical representation $\Adjoint$ of ${\mathcal G} $ on ${\mathcal G}^1$. But, by the
  proposition, the exponential map for ${\mathcal G}^1$ is a diffeomorphism, and we will
  have $g\exp(Z)g^{-1} = \exp(\Adjoint_gZ)$ for any arrow $g \in {\mathcal G} $ beginning
  at the base point of each $Z \in A^1$. This proves \eqref{pr1} because the filtration is
  weakly firm.

  To prove \eqref{pr2} we now show each arrow $g \in {\mathcal G}$, beginning at some
  $m \in M$, can be written uniquely as $g = kh$, with $k \in {\mathcal G}^1$ and
  $h \in {\mathcal G} $. Indeed, from \eqref{tui} we have
  $\Adjoint_g\epsilon(m) \in \epsilon(gm) + A^1|_{gm}$. But, according to Lemma
  \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings1}, the Lie group ${\mathcal G}^1_{gm}$ (the source=target
  fibre of ${\mathcal G}^1$ at $gm \in M$) acts freely and transitively on the affine
  space $\epsilon(gm) + A^1|_{gm}$. Therefore there exists a unique $k \in {\mathcal G}^1$ such
  that $\Adjoint_g\epsilon(m) = \Adjoint_k \epsilon(gm)$. But this means
  $\Adjoint_{k^{-1}g}\epsilon(m) = \epsilon(gm) = \epsilon(k^{-1}gm)$, i.e., $k^{-1}g \in {\mathcal H} $.

  To prove \eqref{pr3}, suppose $Y \in \Gamma({\mathfrak a}_j)$ for some $j\ge 0$ and let
  $h \in {\mathcal H} $ be an arbitrary arrow beginning at some $m \in M$. Since the
  bracket on $A^0$ is ${\mathcal G}$-invariant under $\Adjoint$, the grading identity
  $[\epsilon, Y](m) = jY(m)$ gives us $[\Adjoint_h\epsilon(m), \Adjoint_hY(m)] = j\Adjoint_hY(m)$. From
  the definition of ${\mathcal H} $, we conclude that
  $[\epsilon(hm), \Adjoint_hY(m)] = j\Adjoint_hY(m)$, establishing that
  $\Adjoint_hY(m) \in {\mathfrak a}_j$, as required.

  In view of the fact that ${\mathcal G} = {\mathcal G}^1 {\mathcal H} $, and having
  established \eqref{pr3}, it suffices, in proving \eqref{pr4}, to show that the
  filtration of $A^0$ is invariant under the action of ${\mathcal G}^1$. But $A^0$ is
  already invariant under the action of $A^1$ by F1. Since ${\mathcal G}^1$ is
  source-connected, the filtration of $A^0$ is therefore invariant under the action of
  ${\mathcal G}^1$.

  Part \eqref{pr5} is a rather straightforward consequence of the other parts, whose
  proof is omitted.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}\label{ew2}
  A weakly firm, filtered Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ with Lie algebroid $A$ admits a
  Weyl gauge if and only if it is firmly filtered and $\graded(A^0)$ admits an
  $\Adjoint$-invariant fibre-wise grading section $E$.
\end{corollary}
\begin{proof}
  Given an $\Adjoint$-invariant fibre-wise grading section $E$ of $\graded(A^0)$, any lift
  to a section of $A^0$ is a Weyl gauge for $\mathcal G$, as is immediately verified. The
  converse statement follows from the theorem.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Weyl gauge transformations}\label{gauge_transformations}
Let $A$ be a firmly filtered Lie algebroid over $M$ and $\epsilon$ a Weyl gauge
lifting some grading section $E$ of $\graded(A^0)$. Then, applying Theorem \ref{weyl}, we
obtain an isomorphism
$\phi_\epsilon \colon (A/A^1)\oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1) \xrightarrow{\sim} A$. A new
Weyl gauge $\epsilon'$, lifting $E$, determines a second isomorphism
$\phi_{\epsilon'} \colon (A/A^1)\oplus_{\adjoint}\graded(A^1) \xrightarrow{\sim} A$, so
that $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1} \colon A \rightarrow A$ is a
filtration-preserving Lie algebroid automorphism covering the identity on $M$, and mapping
the gauge $\epsilon$ to $\epsilon'$. We may construct automorphisms of a firmly filtered
Lie groupoid in a similar way, using Theorem \ref{weyl2}.

\begin{theorem}
  The automorphisms of firmly filtered Lie algebroids and Lie groupoids described above
  are precisely the Weyl gauge transformations (conjugations) defined in \ref{uniqueness}.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}[Proof of the {\bfseries infinitesimal case}]
  Let $\epsilon $, $\epsilon'$, $\phi_\epsilon $ and $\phi_{\epsilon'}$ have the meanings
  above. Then it is not hard to see, from the statement of Theorem \ref{weyl}, that
  $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1} $ is the unique Lie algebroid isomorphism
  $\varphi$ covering the identity such that:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item \label{gold1} $\varphi(B_\epsilon) = B_{\epsilon'}$, where $B_\epsilon$ denotes
    the isotropy of $\epsilon $ under the canonical representation of $A$ on its anchor
    kernel.
  \item \label{gold2} $\varphi(X) \equiv X \modulo A^1$, for all $X \in \Gamma(A)$.
  \item \label{gold3} $\varphi(Y) = \Phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \Phi_\epsilon^{-1}(Y)$ for all
    $Y\in \Gamma(A^1)$, where $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded(A^1) \rightarrow A^1$ denotes
    the splitting determined by $\epsilon $.
  \end{conditions}
  Now, a fibre-wise application of Lemma \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings1} show that there
  exists $Z \in \Gamma(A^1)$ such that
  $\epsilon' = \Adjoint_{\exp(Z)}\epsilon = e^{-\adjoint_Z}\epsilon $. See also Remark
  \ref{weyl}. To show $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1}$ is a Weyl gauge
  transformation, it suffices to show that the Weyl gauge transformation
  $\varphi:=e^{-\adjoint_Z}$ satisfies the above conditions. But \eqref{gold1} follows
  immediately, by construction, because $e^{-\adjoint_Z}$ is a Lie algebroid morphism;
  \eqref{gold2} follows from the expansion
  $e^{-\adjoint_Z}X:=X -[Z, X] + \frac{1}{2!}[Z, [Z, X]] - \cdots$ and the fact that
  $\Gamma(A^1) \subset \Gamma(A)$ is an ideal ($A$ is firmly filtered); while
  \eqref{gold3} follows from the fibre-wise application of Lemma
  \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings2}.

  It remains to show {\em every} Weyl gauge transformation $e^{-\adjoint_Z}$ is of the
  form $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1}$ for some $\epsilon $ and
  $\epsilon'$. But the above argument already shows this, if we choose $\epsilon $ to be
  any Weyl gauge and $\epsilon' := e^{-\adjoint_Z}(\epsilon)$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of the {\bfseries global case}]
  Let ${\mathcal G} $ be a firmly filtered Lie groupoid with Lie algebroid $A$, and let
  $\epsilon$ and $\epsilon'$ have the meanings above. Applying Theorem \ref{weyl2}, we
  obtain Lie groupoid isomorphisms
  $\phi_\epsilon, \phi_{\epsilon'} \colon {\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1
  \times_{\Adjoint}\exp(\graded(A^1)) \rightarrow {\mathcal G}$. From the statement of the
  theorem, it is not too hard too see that $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1} $ is
  the unique Lie groupoid isomorphism $\varphi$ covering the identity such that:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item \label{silver1} $\varphi({\mathcal H}_\epsilon) = {\mathcal H}_{\epsilon'}$, where
    ${\mathcal H}_\epsilon$ denotes the isotropy of $\epsilon $ under the canonical
    representation of ${\mathcal G}$ on the anchor kernel of $A$.
  \item \label{silver2} $\varphi(g){\mathcal G}^1 \equiv g {\mathcal G}^1$, for all
    $g \in {\mathcal G} $.
  \item \label{silver3} $d\varphi(Y) = \Phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \Phi_\epsilon^{-1}(Y)$ for all
    $Y\in \Gamma(A^1)$, where $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded(A^1) \rightarrow A^1$ denotes
    the splitting determined by $\epsilon $.
  \end{conditions}
  In convincing herself of this, the reader will keep in mind that
  ${\mathcal G}^1\cong\exponential(A^1)$ and $\exponential(\graded(A^1))$ are
  source simply-connected, so that a groupoid morphism having one of these as domain is
  determined completely by its infinitesimalization.

  Now choose $Z \in \Gamma(A^1)$ as in the infinitesimal argument, so that
  $\epsilon'=\Adjoint_{\exp(Z)}\epsilon=e^{-\adjoint_Z}\epsilon $. To show that
  $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1}$ is a Weyl gauge transformation, it suffices
  to show that the Weyl gauge transformation $\varphi :=K_{\exp(Z)}$ satisfies the above
  conditions, where $K_{\exp(Z)}\colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G}$ denotes
  conjugation by the (bi)section $\exp(Z)$. To this end, suppose $h \in {\mathcal G} $ is
  an arrow beginning at some $m \in M$ and that $h \in {\mathcal H}_\epsilon$. Then
  $\Adjoint_h \epsilon(m) = \epsilon(hm)$. Applying $\Adjoint_{\exp(Z)}$ to both sides, we
  conclude that $\Adjoint_{K_{\exp(Z)}(h)}\epsilon'(m) = \epsilon'(m)$, showing
  $K_{\exp(Z)}(h) \in {\mathcal H}_{\epsilon'}$. We can reverse the roles of $\epsilon $
  and $\epsilon' $ and repeat the argument, and conclude that
  $K_{\exp(Z)}({\mathcal H}_\epsilon) = {\mathcal H}_{\epsilon'}$, i.e., \eqref{silver1}
  holds. The normality of ${\mathcal G}^1$ (Theorem \eqrefs{weyl2}{pr1}) means
  $g {\mathcal G}^1 = {\mathcal G}^1g$, for any $g \in {\mathcal G}$. If
  $g \in {\mathcal G}$ is an arbitrary arrow, beginning at some $m \in M$, we compute,
  appealing to normality twice,
    \begin{multline*}
      K_{\exp(Z)}(g){\mathcal G}^1 =
    {\mathcal G}^1 \exp(Z(gm))g\exp(-Z(m))\\ =
    {\mathcal G}^1 g\exp(-Z(m)) =
    g\exp(-Z(m)){\mathcal G}^1 =
    g{\mathcal G}^1,
  \end{multline*}
  which proves \eqref{silver2}. Since $dK_{\exp(Z)}=e^{-\adjoint_Z}$, \eqref{silver3}
  already follows from the argument given in the infinitesimal case.\footnote{The source
    of the minus sign is our convention of using right-invariant vector fields to define
    Lie brackets, so that $dK_{\exp(Z)}X=\Adjoint_{J^1\exp(Z)}X=e^{-\adjoint_Z}X$,
    $X \in \Gamma(A)$.}

  To show every Weyl gauge transformation $K_{\exp(Z)}$ is of the form
  $\phi_{\epsilon'}\circ \phi_\epsilon^{-1}$ for some $\epsilon $ and $\epsilon'$, take
  $\epsilon $ to be any Weyl gauge, $\epsilon' := e^{-\adjoint_Z}(\epsilon)$, and repeat
  the preceding argument.
\end{proof}

% According to the theorem below, gauge transformations are closed under
% composition.

% It is not hard to see that a gauge transformation $\varphi$ must satisfy:
% \begin{align}
%    \varphi(X) &\equiv X \modulo A^1,\quad  X \in \Gamma(A)\label{gauge1}\\
%    \varphi(Y) &\equiv Y \modulo A^{j+1},\quad Y \in \Gamma(A^j),\, j > 0.\label{gauge2}
% \end{align}
% In particular, the second identity follows from a general fact about splittings of a
% filtered vector bundle $V$: If $\Phi_1, \Phi_2 \colon \graded(V) \rightarrow V$ are
% splittings, then $\Phi_2 \circ \Phi_1^{-1} - \identity$ is homogeneous of degree one.

% The group of all filtration-preserving Lie algebroid isomorphisms
% $\varphi \colon A \rightarrow A$ satisfying \eqref{gauge1} and \eqref{gauge2} will be
% denoted $\GGauge(A)$, a definition that makes sense for an arbitrary filtered Lie
% algebroid $A$. Note that \eqref{gauge1} implies $\varphi$ must cover the identity, because
% F1 then forces $\#\varphi(X) = \# X$, and we suppose the base $M$ is connected.

% \begin{theorem}
%   Let $A$ be a filtered Lie algebroid over $M$. Then for each pair of Weyl gauges
%   $\epsilon_1, \epsilon_2$ with $\graded_0(\epsilon_1)=\graded_0(\epsilon_2)$ there exists unique
%   $\varphi \in \GGauge(A)$ such that $\varphi(\epsilon_1) = \epsilon_2$. In particular, if $A$ admits
%   a Weyl gauge, then $\GGauge(A)$ is precisely the set of gauge transformations of $A$.
% \end{theorem}


% In particular, $\GGauge(A)$ acts freely and transitively on the set of all Weyl gauges for
% $A$ that lift some fixed fibre-wise grading section $E$ of $\graded(A^0)$, whenever this
% exists.  Applying Lemma \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings1} fibre-wise shows that the group
% of sections $\Gamma({\exp(A^1)})$ of the bundle of nilpotent Lie groups $\exp(A^1)$ also
% acts freely and transitively via $\Adjoint$ on the set of Weyl gauges, lifting some
% section $E$. While each choice of Weyl gauge $\epsilon$ accordingly determines a bijection
% $\GGauge(A)\cong \Gamma({\exp(A^1)})$, there is no canonical isomorphism between the two
% groups in general. The example below is a notable exception.

% \begin{proof}[Proof of theorem]
%   From our preparatory remarks above, we already know that some $\varphi \in \GGauge(A)$
%   with $\varphi(\epsilon_1)=\varphi(\epsilon_2)$ exists, namely some gauge transformation. To prove the
%   first statement of the theorem it remains to establish uniqueness.

%   Suppose $\varphi' \in \GGauge(A)$ also maps $\epsilon_1$ to $\epsilon_2$. Then
%   $\varphi^{-1} \circ \varphi'$ will be an element of $\GGauge(A)$ mapping $\epsilon_1$ to
%   $\epsilon_1$. So, to establish uniqueness, it suffices to suppose $\varphi(\epsilon_1) = \epsilon_1$ and
%   prove that $\varphi $ must be the identity.

%   By Theorem \ref{weyl}, we may suppose, without loss of generality, that $A$ is the
%   semi-direct product $B\oplus_r (\oplus_j {\mathfrak a}_j)$ of a filtered Lie algebroid
%   $B$, with $B\le 0$, with a filtered Lie algebra bundle
%   $A^1=\oplus_{j=1}^k{\mathfrak a}_j$, on which $B$ is acting by $r$, a representation
%   that preserves the grading. Moreover, we may suppose there exists a section $E$ of $B^0$
%   such that $\epsilon_1 = (E, 0)$, and such that $[E, X]=0$ for all $X \in \Gamma(B)$. When we
%   think of $E$ as a section of the Lie algebra bundle
%   $B^0 \oplus (\oplus_{j>0}{\mathfrak a}_j)$, we have $\adjoint_EY=r_EY$ for
%   $Y \in \Gamma(\oplus_{j>0}{\mathfrak a}_j)$, where $\adjoint$ is the adjoint
%   representation of this Lie algebra bundle.

%   Requirement \eqref{gauge1} means that $\varphi(X, Y) = (X, \theta(X) + \lambda(Y))$, for
%   some vector bundle morphisms $\theta \colon B \rightarrow A^1$ and
%   $\lambda \colon A^1 \rightarrow A^1$ covering the identity on $M$. Since
%   $\varphi(\epsilon_1)=\epsilon_1$, we conclude that $\theta(E) = 0$.

%   Since $\varphi $ is a Lie algebroid morphism, we must have
%   $\varphi([(E,0), (0,Y)]) = [\varphi(E,0), \varphi(0,Y)]$ for all $Y \in A^1$. Applying
%   the definition of the semi-direct product bracket, and using $\theta(E)=0$, we can write
%   this as $\lambda(\adjoint_EY) = \adjoint_E \lambda(Y)$. Since requirement \eqref{gauge2}
%   implies that is $\lambda - \identity$ is homogeneous of degree one, we may apply Lemma
%   \eqrefs{splittings}{splittings40} fibre-wise to conclude that $\lambda(Y) = Y$, so
%   that $\varphi$ is the identity.

%   Turning to the second claim of the theorem, suppose $\varphi \in \GGauge(A)$, let
%   $\epsilon_1$ be a Weyl gauge, and put $\epsilon_2 := \varphi(\epsilon_1)$.
%   Corollary \ref{ew1} shows that $A$ is firmly filtered, so that a section
%   $\epsilon \in \Gamma(A^0)$ is a Weyl gauge if and only if $\graded_0(\epsilon)$ is an
%   $\adjoint$-invariant grading section of $\graded(A^0)$. But \eqref{gauge1} ensures that
%   $\graded_0(\epsilon_1) = \graded_0(\epsilon_2)$, so $\epsilon_2$ is Weyl gauge, because
%   $\epsilon_1$ is. By the first part of the theorem, $\varphi$ must coincide with the
%   gauge transformation mapping $\epsilon_1$ to $\epsilon_2$.
% \end{proof}

\refreshpage
\section{Cartan connections on Lie groupoids and algebroids}\label{cartan_section}

Cartan connections on Lie groupoids, introduced in \cite[Appendix A]{Blaom_12}, generalize
to the intransitive case the classical Cartan connections introduced by Ehresmann
\cite{Ehresmann_51} and in widespread use, when the latter notion is stripped of model
data. The corresponding infinitesimal notion, Cartan connections on Lie algebroids, were
introduced some time earlier, in \cite{Blaom_06}. The relationship with the classical
notion is detailed in \cite{Blaom_16b}.

The meaning of curvature for these connections is different from the classical
one. Curvature is still an obstruction to symmetry, but the kind of symmetry is not
preordained by the specification of a flat model {\em a priori}.

With the exception of Proposition \ref{cartan}, which we presume is new, this section is a
summary of definitions and results presented in the cited works and elsewhere. It is
logically independent of Sections \ref{introduction} and  \ref{semi-direct}.

\subsection{Cartan connections on a Lie groupoid}\label{cartan}%
A {\df Cartan connection} on a Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ over $M$ is an $n$-plane field
$D \subset T {\mathcal G}$ arising from some right inverse
${\mathcal G} \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G} $ for the canonical projection
$J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $, which is necessarily uniquely determined by
$D$. Here $J^1 {\mathcal G} $ denotes the Lie groupoid of one-jets of bisections of
${\mathcal G} $. Of course, we are understanding the right inverse to be a Lie groupoid
morphism. This is equivalent to requiring that $D \subset T {\mathcal G}$ be
multiplicatively closed, in the sense of \cite{Salazar_13,Crainic_etal_15}, but this
interpretation will play no role here.

The usual adjoint action of a Lie group on its Lie algebra is generalized by an action of
$J^1 {\mathcal G} $ on the Lie algebroid $A$ of ${\mathcal G} $, denoted $\Adjoint$
\cite{Blaom_16b}. Composing with the right inverse
${\mathcal G} \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G} $ of
$J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $ associated with $D$, we obtain a
representation $\bar D$ of ${\mathcal G}$ on $A$. This representation extends the
canonical one of ${\mathcal G} $ on elements of the anchor kernel of $A$ (which we have
also been denoting by $\Adjoint$ in Section \ref{semi-direct}).

The prototype of a Cartan connection is the horizontal $n$-plane field $D$ on
an action groupoid $G \times M$, for a smooth action of some Lie group $ G $ on
$M$, which need not be transitive. In this case
\begin{equation}
  \bar D_{(g, m)}(\xi, m) = (\Adjoint_g \xi, g\cdot m).\label{adjoint}
\end{equation}

Every Cartan connection $D$ on a {\em transitive} Lie groupoid can be recovered from the
representation $\bar D$:

\begin{proposition}
  Suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is a Lie groupoid with a transitive Lie algebroid $A$. Then for
  every representation $\rho $ of ${\mathcal G} $ on $A$, extending the canonical
  representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel, there exists a unique Cartan
  connection $D$ on ${\mathcal G} $ such that $\bar D = \rho $.
\end{proposition}
\noindent%
The infinitesimal version of this result, Proposition \ref{hsd} below, already appears in
\cite{Blaom_06}, and has an easy proof. The proposition above is proven in Appendix
\ref{rut}.


\subsection{Infinitesimal Cartan connections}\lab{hsd}%
Infinitesimalizing a Cartan connection, viewed as a Lie groupoid
morphism ${\mathcal G} \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G}$, we obtain a splitting $s
\colon A \rightarrow J^1 A $ of the exact
sequence
\begin{equation*}
  0\longrightarrow T^*\!M\otimes A\longrightarrow J^1A\longrightarrow A\longrightarrow 0
\end{equation*}
in the category of Lie algebroids. Here $J^1 A $ denotes the Lie algebroid of one-jets of
sections of the Lie algebroid $A $ of ${\mathcal G}$. In the category of vector bundles, splittings
$s$ of the above sequence are in one-to-one correspondence with Koszul connections
$\nabla$ on $A$; this correspondence is given by\footnote{According to our sign
  conventions, the inclusion $T^*\!M \otimes A \rightarrow J^1 A $ induces the following
  map on sections: $df \otimes X \mapsto f J^1 X - J^1 (f X)$.}
\begin{equation}
  sX = J^1 X + \nabla X; \qquad  X \in \Gamma(A). \mathlab{cspan}
\end{equation}
When $s \colon A \rightarrow J^1 A $ is a morphism of Lie algebroids, as is the case here,
then $\nabla $ is called a {\df Cartan connection} on $A$ \cite{Blaom_06}, or an {\df
  infinitesimal Cartan Connection} when we want to distinguish it from the corresponding
global notion. Evidently, the notion makes sense for arbitrary Lie algebroids, not only
those integrated by Lie groupoids. According to \cite{Blaom_06}, $\nabla $ is a Cartan
connection on $A$ if and only if
\begin{equation*}
  \nabla_V[X, Y] = [\nabla_VX,
        Y]+[X,\nabla_VY]-\nabla_{\bar\nabla_XV}Y+\nabla_{\bar\nabla_YV}X,
\end{equation*}
for all vector fields $V$ on $M$ and sections $X, Y$ of $A$. Here $\bar\nabla$ denotes the
associated $A$-connection on $TM$ defined by
\begin{equation}
 \bar\nabla_XV= \#\nabla_VX +[\#X, V],
\end{equation}
an $A$-representation when $\bar\nabla $ is Cartan. Here $\# \colon A \rightarrow TM$
denotes anchor.

Given a Cartan connection $\nabla $ on some Lie algebroid $A$, we can compose the
corresponding splitting $s \colon A \rightarrow J^1 A$ with the adjoint action of $J^1 A$
on $ A$, $\adjoint_{J^1X}Y = [X, Y]$, to obtain a self-representation $\bar \nabla $ of
$A$. Explicitly, we have

\begin{equation}
 \bar\nabla_XY = \nabla_{\#Y}X +[X, Y].\label{zero}
\end{equation}
The anchor $\# \colon A \rightarrow TM$ becomes $\bar\nabla$-equivariant. A $TM$-connection $\nabla $ is actually Cartan if and only if
\begin{equation}
  (\nabla_V\torsion \bar\nabla)(X,Y) =
 \curvature \nabla (\#X, V)Y -  \curvature \nabla (\#Y, V) X,
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation*}
  \torsion \bar\nabla (X, Y) = \bar\nabla_XY - \bar\nabla_YX - [X, Y] =
  \nabla_{\#Y}X - \nabla_{\#X}Y + [X, Y].
\end{equation*}

The representation $\bar\nabla$ of $A$ on itself coincides with the canonical
representation of $A$ on sections lying in the anchor kernel (denoted $\adjoint$ in
Section \ref{semi-direct}). In the transitive case, $\nabla $ can be recovered from
$\bar\nabla $:

\begin{proposition}[\mbox{\cite[Proposition 6.1]{Blaom_06}}]
  Suppose $A $ is a transitive Lie algebroid. Then for every representation $\rho $ of
  $A $ on itself, extending the canonical representation of $A$ on sections lying in the
  anchor kernel, there exists a unique Cartan connection $\nabla $ on $A$ such that
  $\bar \nabla = \rho $.
\end{proposition}

\subsection{Curvature as obstruction to symmetry}\label{curvature}
The {\df curvature} of $\nabla $ is defined as usual as per Koszul connections. This
notion of curvature is not to be confused with curvature as this is generally understood
in the Cartan geometry literature, what we have called {\df model curvature}, whose Lie
algebroid analogue was defined in \ref{normalization}. For further context, see
\cite{Blaom_16b}.  The prototype of an infinitesimal Cartan connection is the trivial flat
connection $\nabla $ on an action algebroid ${\mathfrak g} \times M$, for some
infinitesimal action of a Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ on $M$. If we identify
${\mathfrak g} $ with the constant ($\nabla$-parallel) sections of
${\mathfrak g} \times M$, then its bracket is
$\torsion\bar\nabla(\,\cdot\,,\,\cdot\,)$. Indeed, let
$\tau \in \Gamma(\wedge^2 A^* \otimes A)$ denote the trivial extension of the bracket on
${\mathfrak g} $ to a fibre-wise bracket on $A = {\mathfrak g} \times M$, and recall that
the Lie algebroid bracket on ${\mathfrak g} \times M$ is given by
\begin{equation}
  [X,Y]=\nabla_{\#X}Y-\nabla_{\#Y}X +\tau(X,Y). \label{one}
\end{equation}
Then we can write
\begin{equation}
    [X,Y]=\bar\nabla_XY-\bar\nabla_YX - \tau(X,Y),\label{two}
\end{equation}
from which $\tau = \torsion \bar\nabla $ immediately follows.

\begin{theorem}[\cite{Blaom_06}]
  If $M$ is simply connected, then every Lie algebroid $A$ equipped with a flat Cartan
  connection $\nabla$ is naturally isomorphic to an action algebroid
  ${\mathfrak g} \times M$ (possibly intransitive) where ${\mathfrak g}\subset \Gamma(TM) $
  is the Lie algebra of $\nabla $-parallel sections, equipped with bracket
  $\torsion \bar\nabla$.
\end{theorem}
\noindent More informally, we have:
\begin{aside}
  A Lie algebroid equipped with a Cartan connection is an infinitesimal symmetry deformed
  by curvature; the torsion of the connection is the deformed symmetry Lie bracket.
\end{aside}

If $\curvature \nabla =0$ but $M $ is not simply connected, then $A$ is a Lie algebra
action with monodromy twist \cite{Blaom_16b}. Lie groupoids with Cartan connections,
whose infinitesimalizations are flat, define pseudogroups of symmetries of $M$, and under
an additional completeness assumption, twisted Lie group actions \cite{Blaom_16,
  Blaom_16b}. In the transitive case, one can recover a locally homogeneous structure,
assuming ``geometric closure'', if not global homogeneity up to cover \cite{Blaom_13}.

\begin{example}[Riemannian geometry]
  Associated with every Riemannian metric is a canonical Cartan connection $\nabla $ on
  the isotropy subalgebroid\footnote{This isotropy is the abstract homogeneous PDE you
    solve to find Killing fields (whether or not they exist).} $A \subset J^1 (TM)$ of the
  metric \cite{Blaom_06,Blaom_12}, which is an infinitesimalization of a global connection
  $D$ on the isotropy subgroupoid ${\mathcal G} \subset J^1(M \times M)$ of the metric
  \cite{Blaom_16b}. It's significance is that the anchor induces a map
  $\Gamma(A) \rightarrow \Gamma(TM)$ establishing a one-to-one correspondence between
  $\nabla $-parallel sections and infinitesimal isometries (Killing fields).

  Under a natural identification of $A$ with $ TM \oplus \so(TM)$, the Lie bracket is
  given by
\begin{equation*}
  \left[
  \begin{pmatrix}
    V_1\\ C_1
  \end{pmatrix},
  \begin{pmatrix}
    V_2\\ C_2
  \end{pmatrix}
\right] =
\begin{pmatrix}
  [V_1,V_2]\\
  [C_1, C_2] + \nabla^{TM}_{V_1} C_2 - \nabla^{TM}_{V_2}
  C_1 + \curvature \nabla^{TM}(V_1,V_2)
\end{pmatrix}\enspace,
\end{equation*}
where $\nabla^{TM}$ is the Levi-Cevita connection. The Cartan connection is given by
\begin{equation*}
  \nabla_U
  \begin{pmatrix}
    V \\ C
  \end{pmatrix}
  =
  \begin{pmatrix}
    \nabla^M_UV + C U \\
    \nabla^M_UC + \curvature \nabla^M (U,V)
  \end{pmatrix}\,,
\end{equation*}
which has torsion
\begin{equation}
  \torsion \bar\nabla \left(
    \begin{pmatrix}
        V_1 \\ C_1
      \end{pmatrix},
      \begin{pmatrix}
        V_2 \\ C_2
      \end{pmatrix}
  \right)=
  \begin{pmatrix}
    C_1 V_2 -C_2 V_1 \\
    [C_1,C_2] - \curvature \nabla^{TM} (V_1,V_2)
  \end{pmatrix}\,.  \label{torsion}
\end{equation}
The curvature of $\nabla $ is readily computed, vanishing precisely when $M$ has constant
sectional curvature $s$. From the formula for the torsion, we conclude that
${\mathfrak g} $ in the preceding theorem is the symmetry Lie algebra of the Riemannian
sphere, plane or hyperbolic space, according to whether $s$ is positive, zero, or
negative.
\end{example}

\subsection{Cartan connections compatible with a filtration}\label{compat}
Recall that an infinitesimal Cartan connection $\nabla$ on a filtered Lie algebroid $A$ is
{\df compatible} with the filtration if the filtration is invariant under the associated
representation $\bar\nabla $. Similarly, a Cartan connection $D$ on a filtered Lie
groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ with Lie algebroid $A$ is {\df compatible} with the filtration
provided the filtration of $A$ is invariant under the associated representation $\bar D$,
which in turn implies compatibility of the corresponding infinitesimal Cartan connection
$\bar\nabla$.

In addition to the examples given in \ref{filtered}, the following result justifies our
focus on {\em firmly} filtered Lie algebroids.
\begin{proposition}
  If a transitive filtered Lie groupoid (Lie algebroid) admits a compatible
  (infinitesimal) Cartan connection, then it is firmly filtered.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  Suppose $\nabla $ is a Cartan connection on a transitive filtered Lie algebroid
  $A$. Then $\bar\nabla $ extends the canonical representation $\adjoint$ of $A$ on its
  anchor kernel, which contains $A^0$. So $\adjoint_X Y := [X, Y] = \bar\nabla_XY$ for
  $X \in A$ and $Y \in A^0$. If $\nabla $ is compatible with the filtration, then $A^0$
  and the filtration of $A^0$ must therefore be $\adjoint$-invariant. The groupoid case
  follows immediately from the infinitesimal one.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Filtered Klein geometries}\label{homogeneous}
We now prove some claims made in \ref{eg2} regarding the homogeneous space $M=G/P$
described there. Let ${\mathcal G} = G \times M$ be the action groupoid, equipped with its
canonical Cartan connection $D$, so that ${\mathcal G}$ acts on
$A = {\mathfrak g} \times M$ according to \eqrefs{cartan}{adjoint}. Since the filtration
of ${\mathfrak g} $ is $P$-invariant, the filtration of $A$ is $\bar D$-invariant.

Since $A$ is transitive and $A^0$ is the anchor kernel, we have, as vector bundles,
\begin{equation*}
  \graded(A) \cong \graded(TM)\oplus \graded(A^0).
\end{equation*}
As in \ref{curvature}, we denote the fibre-wise Lie bracket on $A={\mathfrak g} \times M$
coming from ${\mathfrak g} $ by $\tau $, which coincides with the torsion of the
infinitesimalization $\nabla $ of $D$, namely the canonical flat Koszul connection on the
trivial bundle ${\mathfrak g} \times M$. We note in passing that $\tau$ is evidently
$\bar D$-invariant, and so $\bar\nabla$-invariant. When we want to emphasize we are using
the bracket $T=\graded_0(\tau)$ when viewing $\graded(A)$, $\graded(A^0)$ or $\graded(TM)$
as Lie algebra bundles, we shall denote them by $\graded^T(A)$, $\graded^T(A^0)$ and
$\graded^T(TM)$.

\begin{proposition}
  The vector bundle filtration of $A = {\mathfrak g} \times M$ defined in \ref{eg2} is a
  firm Lie algebroid filtration, and ${\mathcal G} = G \times M$ is a firmly filtered Lie
  groupoid. Furthermore, equipping $\graded(A)$ with the corresponding Lie algebra bundle
  structure (Proposition \ref{filtered}), we have
  \begin{equation*}
    \label{decomposition}
    \graded(A) = -\graded^T(TM)\oplus \graded^T(A^0)\quad
    \text{(Lie algebra bundle direct sum)}
  \end{equation*}
  where -$\graded^T(TM)$ is $\graded^T(TM)$ equipped with
  negative bracket, and $T=\graded_0(\tau)$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
  Axiom F2 holds by construction. Let $X\in \Gamma(A^j)$ and $Y\in \Gamma(A^k)$ be
  given. Then we claim:
  \begin{align}
  \bar\nabla_X Y &= [X, Y] \quad\text{if $k \ge 0$}\label{gamma}\\
  \bar\nabla_X Y &= \tau(X, Y) \quad\text{if $j \ge 0$}\label{delta}\\
  [X, Y] &= \tau(X, Y) \quad\text{if $j \ge 0$ and $k \ge 0$} \label{alpha}\\
  [X, Y] &\equiv -\tau(X, Y) \mod A^{j + k + 1} \quad\text{if $j < 0$ and $k < 0$}\label{beta}\\
  [X, Y] &\in \Gamma(A^{j + k + 1}) \quad\text{if $j < 0$ and $k \ge 0$}.\label{blue}
  \end{align}
  Equation \eqref{gamma} follows from \eqrefs{hsd}{zero} and F2. If $j\ge 0$, then
  Equation \eqrefs{curvature}{one} reads $[X, Y] = - \nabla_{\#Y}X +\tau(X, Y)$, because
  $\#A^j=0$ in that case. This and \eqrefs{hsd}{zero} delivers \eqref{delta}.  Claim
  \eqref{alpha} follows immediately from the preceding two.

  If $j < 0$, then $j + k + 1 \le k$, and so
  $A^k \subset A^{j + k+ 1}$. Similarly, if $k < 0$, then
  $A^j \subset A^{j + k + 1}$. Since the filtration is
  $\bar\nabla$-invariant, \eqref{beta} now follows from \eqrefs{curvature}{two}.

  As already mentioned, if $j <0 $, then $A^k \subset A^{j+k+1}$. On
  the other hand, if additionally $k \ge 0$, then
  $[X, Y] = \bar\nabla_XY$, by \eqref{gamma}. So \eqref{blue} is a
  consequence of the $\bar\nabla $-invariance of $A^k$.

  To establish Axiom F1 for filtered Lie algebroids, we need to show
  $[X, Y] \in \Gamma(A^{j+k})$. Equations \eqref{alpha} takes care of the case $j\ge 0$
  and $k \ge 0$. Equation \eqref{beta} takes care of the case $j < 0$ and $k < 0$ because
  $A^{j+k+1} \subset A^{j+k}$ is always true. The remaining case is $j < 0$ and $k \ge 0$
  (and the case $j \ge 0$ and $k < 0$, which will follow by symmetry). But as
  $A^{j + k + 1} \subset A^{j + k}$, this already follows from \eqref{blue}.

  The last statement in of the theorem follows easily from \eqref{alpha}, \eqref{beta},
  and \eqref{blue}.

  Since the filtration of $A$ is $\bar D$-invariant, $D$ is compatible with the
  filtration, and so firmness follows from Proposition \ref{compat}.
\end{proof}

\refreshpage
\section{Model data}\label{modeldata}
Model data, defined in \ref{preview}, generalizes the trivial Lie algebra bundle
structure of an action algebroid ${\mathfrak g} \times M$.

\subsection{Constructing Cartan connections compatible with model data}\label{constructing}
In Section \ref{filtered_cartan} we shall need the following important result, reducing,
in the {\em transitive} case, the problem of constructing Cartan connections compatible
with given model data, to the problem of constructing representations of a suitable
subalgebroid/subgroupoid.

\begin{proposition}
  {\em \bfseries Infinitesimal case.} Let $A$ be a transitive Lie algebroid that can be written as
  vector bundle sum $A = B \oplus A'$, where $B \subset A$ is a subalgebroid and
  $A' \subset A$ an ideal lying in the kernel of the anchor
  $\# \colon A \rightarrow TM$. Let $\tau $ be model data on $A$ and suppose
  there exists a representation $\rho^B$ of $B$ on $A$, with respect to which $\tau $ is
  invariant, and such that $\rho^B$ extends the canonical representation of $B \subset A $
  on the anchor kernel of A:
  \begin{equation}
    \rho^B_X Z = [X, Z]\enspace \text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $Z \in \Gamma(A)$,
      $X \in \Gamma(B)$}.\label{ext1}
  \end{equation}
  Then there exists a unique Cartan connection $\nabla $ on $A$, necessarily weakly
  compatible with $\tau $, such that
  \begin{equation}
    \bar\nabla_{X + Y}W = \rho^B_XW + \tau(Y, W);\qquad X
    \in \Gamma(B),\,Y \in \Gamma(A'),\,W \in \Gamma(A).\label{ext2}
  \end{equation}
  If $\tau $ is compatible with a filtration of $A$, and $\rho^B$ preserves the
  filtration, then $\nabla $ is also compatible with the filtration.
  If, additionally,
  \begin{equation}
    \rho^B_{Z}W = \tau(Z, W)\enspace
    \text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $Z \in \Gamma(B)$, $W \in \Gamma(A)$,}\label{ext3}
  \end{equation}
  then $\tau $ is strongly compatible with $\nabla $.

  {\em \bfseries Global case.} Suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is a Lie groupoid, with Lie algebroid $A$,
  that can be written ${\mathcal G} = {\mathcal G}' {\mathcal H} $ with
  ${\mathcal G}'\cap {\mathcal H} = M$ (groupoid of identity elements), where
  ${\mathcal G}' \subset {\mathcal G} $ is a totally intransitive, normal, Lie subgroupoid
  with simply-connected fibres. Let $\tau $ be model data on $A$ and suppose there is a
  representation $\rho^{\mathcal H}$ of ${\mathcal H} $ on $A$, with respect to which
  $\tau $ is invariant, and such that $\rho^{\mathcal H}$ extends the canonical
  representation of ${\mathcal H} \subset {\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel of A:
  \begin{equation}
    \label{pony4}
    \rho^{\mathcal H}_hZ = \Adjoint_h Z  \enspace
      \text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $h \in {\mathcal H}$}.
  \end{equation}
  Then, there exists a unique Cartan connection $D $ on ${\mathcal G}$, necessarily
  weakly compatible with $\tau $, such that
  \begin{equation}
    \bar D_{kh}W = \Adjoint^\tau_k \rho^{\mathcal H}_hW,\label{ext17}
  \end{equation}
  for each arrow $h \in H$ beginning at some $m \in M$, $k \in {\mathcal G}^1|_{hm}$ and
  $W \in A|_m$. Here $\Adjoint^\tau$ is unique representation of ${\mathcal G}'$ on $A$
  integrating the infinitesimal representation $\adjoint^\tau$ of the Lie algebroid $A'$
  of ${\mathcal G} '$ on $A$ defined by $\adjoint^\tau_YW=\tau(Y, W)$.  If $\tau $ is
  compatible with a filtration of $A$, and $\rho^B$ preserves the filtration, then $D$ is
  also compatible with the filtration.
\end{proposition}
\noindent%
Note that $\Adjoint^\tau$ is well-defined on account of the hypothesis of
simple-connectivity.
\begin{proof}{\em \bfseries Infinitesimal case.} Under the hypotheses of the theorem, define an
  $A$-connection $\rho $ on $A$ using the right-hand side of \eqref{ext2}:
  \begin{equation*}
   \rho_{X + Y}W = \rho^B_XW + \tau(Y, W);\qquad
    X \in \Gamma(B),\,Y \in \Gamma(A'),\,W \in \Gamma(A).
  \end{equation*}
  By Proposition \ref{hsd}, to show there exists a Cartan connection $\nabla $ satisfying
  \eqref{ext2}, it suffices to show that $\rho $ is actually a representation, and,
  secondly, that $\rho $ extends the canonical representation of $A$ on sections of its
  anchor kernel:
  \begin{equation}
    \label{gobbly}
    \rho_WZ = [W, Z]\enspace\text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $W \in \Gamma(A)$.}
  \end{equation}

  If $W \in \Gamma(B)$, then \eqref{gobbly} holds, by hypothesis \eqref{ext1}. If
  $W \in \Gamma(A')$ and $\#Z=0$, then $\rho_WZ=\tau(W,Z)=[W, Z]$, because both $W$ and
  $Z$ lie in the anchor kernel and $\tau $ is model data. Since $A = B \oplus A'$, this
  proves \eqref{gobbly}.

  Since the restriction of $X \mapsto \rho_X $ to $B$ is $\rho^B$, a representation, and
  the restriction of $Y \mapsto \rho_Y $ to $A'$ is $Y \mapsto \tau(Y, \,\cdot\,)$, also a
  representation, to show $\rho $ is itself a representation, it suffices to prove that

  \begin{equation*}
    \rho_X \rho_Y W - \rho_Y \rho_X W - \rho_{[X,Y]}W = 0, \qquad W \in \Gamma(A),
  \end{equation*}
  whenever $X \in \Gamma(B)$ and $Y \in \Gamma(A')$. But in that case,
  $[X, Y] \in \Gamma(A')$, because $A' \subset A$ is an ideal, and we compute
  \begin{align*}
    \rho_X \rho_Y W - \rho_Y \rho_X W - \rho_{[X,Y]}W
       &= \rho^B_X(\tau(Y, W)) - \tau(Y, \rho^B_XW) - \tau([X,Y], W)\\
                     &= \rho^B_X(\tau(Y, W)) - \tau(Y, \rho^B_XW) - \tau(\rho^B_XY, W),
                       \quad\text{by \eqref{ext1}}  \\
                     &= 0,
                       \quad\text{by the $\rho^B$-invariance of $\tau $.}
  \end{align*}
  Proof of the remaining claims in infinitesimal case is straightforward and omitted.

  {\em \bfseries Global case.} Define a map $(g, W) \mapsto \rho_gW \in A$, from the
  submanifold of all $(g, W) \in {\mathcal G} \times A$ such that $g$ is an arrow
  beginning at the base point of $W$, using the right-hand side of \eqref{ext17}:
  \begin{equation*}
    \rho_{kh}W=\Adjoint^\tau_k \rho^{\mathcal H}_hW;\qquad k \in {\mathcal G}',\,h \in {\mathcal H}.
  \end{equation*}
  To show that there exists a unique Cartan connection $D$ on $A$ satisfying
  \eqref{ext17}, it suffices to show that $\rho$ is a representation, and that $\rho $
  extends the canonical representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel of $A$:
  \begin{equation}
    \rho_gZ = \Adjoint_gZ\enspace\text{whenever $\#Z=0$}\label{ext18}
  \end{equation}
  and $g \in {\mathcal G} $ is an arrow beginning at the base point of $Z$.

  By the hypothesis \eqref{pony4}, \eqref{ext18} already holds for $g \in {\mathcal
    H}$. Since ${\mathcal G} ={\mathcal G}' {\mathcal H} $, to prove \eqref{ext18} in
  general, it suffices to prove
  \begin{equation}
    \Adjoint^\tau_k Z= \Adjoint_kZ\enspace\text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $k \in {\mathcal G}'$} .
  \end{equation}
  In fact, since ${\mathcal G}'$ has connected fibres, is suffices to prove the
  infinitesimalization,
  \begin{equation*}
          \adjoint^\tau_YZ = [Y, Z]\enspace\text{whenever $\#Z=0$, $Y \in A'$.}
  \end{equation*}
  But this true because both $Y$ and $Z$ here lie in the anchor kernel,
  $\adjoint^\tau_YZ:=\tau(Y,Z)$, and $\tau $ is model data.

  Since $\rho^{\mathcal H} $ and $\rho^{{\mathcal G}^1}$ are already representations, to
  show $\rho $ itself is a representation, it suffices to show, for any arrow
  $h \in {\mathcal H} $ beginning at some $m \in M$, that
  \begin{equation*}
    \rho^{\mathcal H}_h \rho^{{\mathcal G}^1}_k \rho^{\mathcal H}_{h^{-1}}W = \rho^{{\mathcal G}^1}_{hkh^{-1}}W,
  \end{equation*}
  where $k \in {\mathcal G}^1|_m$ and $W \in A|_{hm}$ are arbitrary. In fact, since
  ${\mathcal G}^1$ has connected fibres, it is enough to show the two Lie group
  homomorphisms ${\mathcal G}^1|_m \rightarrow \automorphism(A|_m)$ below have the same
  derivative:
  \begin{align*}
