\end{proof}

\subsection{Model curvature under $\psi$-deformations}\label{curv_deform}
Under the same blanket assumptions as \ref{homology}, consider the {\em cochain} complex
\begin{equation}
  \label{chain5a} 0 \rightpartial{}  \graded(A)
  \rightpartial{} \graded(A)_{-}^* \otimes \graded(A)
  \rightpartial{} \wedge^2 \graded(A)_{-}^* \otimes \graded(A) \rightpartial{} \cdots,
\end{equation}
where $\graded(A)_{-}:=\oplus_{j<0} \graded(A)_j$ is a Lie algebra subbundle, which
accordingly acts on $\graded(A)$.  Over each point $m \in M$, \eqref{chain5a} is the
classical cochain complex associated with the representation $\adjoint^T$ of
$\graded(A)_{-}|_m$ on $\graded(A)|_m$. Here $\partial$ is defined by
\begin{align*}
  \partial \omega (V_0, \ldots, V_n) &=
  \sum_{i=0}^{n}(-1)^iT(V_i, \omega(V_0, \ldots, \hat V_i, \ldots V_n))\\
  &+ \sum_{i<j}(-1)^{i+j}\omega(T(V_i,V_j), V_0,\ldots, \hat V_i, \ldots \hat V_j, \ldots V_n),
\end{align*}
where hats indicate omission. In the reductive case we will see in \ref{hodge} that
\eqref{chain5a} can be viewed as the ``adjoint'' of \eqrefs{homology}{chain2}.

The formula shows that $\partial$ is grading-preserving if we equip
$\wedge^n \graded(A)_{-}^*\otimes \graded(A)$ with the grading in which a section
$\omega $ has degree $l$ if it is homogeneous of degree $l$, by which we mean that
$\omega(V_1, \ldots V_n)\in \Gamma(\graded(A)_{l+i_1+i_2+\ldots+i_n})$ whenever
$V_j \in \Gamma(\graded(A)_{i_j})$, for each $j \in \{1, \ldots n\}$.

Our interest in the cochain complex \eqref{chain5a} is following:
\begin{proposition}
  Let $(A, \nabla, \tau)$ be an infinitesimal filtered Cartan geometry. Furthermore,
  suppose that $A$ is transitive and that $A^0$ is the anchor kernel of $A$, so that
  $\graded(A)_{-} \cong \graded(TM)$ and $\partial_n$ is a map
  $$\partial_n\wedge^n \graded(T^*\!M) \otimes \graded(A)\rightarrow
  \wedge^{n+1} \graded(T^*\!M) \otimes \graded(A).$$ For some
  $\psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$ that is homogeneous of degree $l \ge 1$, let
  $\nabla'$ and $\tau'$ be the $\psi$-deformations of $\nabla $ and $\tau $, as defined in
  \ref{deformations}, and let $\kappa $, $\kappa'$ denote the model curvatures of the
  infinitesimal parabolic geometries $(A, \nabla, \tau)$ and $(A, \nabla', \tau')$. Then
  $\kappa' - \kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l$ with
  $\graded_l(\kappa' - \kappa) = -\partial \graded_l(\psi)$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  It is not hard to see that the claim follows from the following two facts proven below:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{plat1} The restriction of $\torsion \bar\nabla' - \torsion \bar\nabla$ to a
    section of $\wedge^2\graded(A)_{-}^*\otimes A$ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$.
  \item\label{plat2}
    The restriction $r = \tau' - \tau $ to a section of $\wedge^2\graded(A)_{-}^*\otimes
    A$ is homogeneous of degree $l$, with $\graded_l (r) = -\partial\graded_l(\psi)$.
  \end{conditions}

  Let $X \in \Gamma(A^j)$ and $Y \in \Gamma(A^k)$ arbitrary sections, with $j, k <
  0$. Here are some {\df Preliminary Observations:} Since $\psi$ is homogeneous of degree
  $l \ge 1$, $\psi_{TM} \colon TM \rightarrow TM $ is filtration-preserving, with
  $\graded_0(\psi_{TM})=\identity= \graded_0(\psi_{TM}^{-1})$; $\bar\nabla_X$ and
  $\bar\nabla_Y$ are also filtration-preserving, because $\nabla $ is required to be
  compatible with filtration, by the definition of infinitesimal filtered Cartan
  geometries; because $j, k < 0$, we always have $A^m \subset A^{m + j + 1}$ and
  $A^m \subset A^{m + k + 1}$ for any integer $m$; because $l\ge 1$, we always have
  $A^{m +l} \subset A^m$, for any $m$; and
  $\graded_0(\#) \colon \graded(A)_{-} \rightarrow \graded(TM)\cong \graded(A)_{-}$ is the
  identity.

  On expanding the definition of $\bar\nabla'$ (Equation \eqrefs{deformations}{saturn2})
  and the definition of $\Adjoint_\psi$ (Equation \eqrefs{deformations}{opal2a}) and its
  inverse (Equation \eqrefs{deformations}{opal2}) one obtains
  \begin{equation*}
    \bar\nabla'_XY - \bar\nabla_XY  =\bar\nabla_X(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y))) +
    \psi(\#\bar\nabla_XY) +
    \psi(\#\bar\nabla_X(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y)))).
  \end{equation*}
  By our Preliminary Observations, the terms on the right-hand side lie in
  $A^{k+l}$, $A^{k+l}$ and $A^{k + 2l}$, respectively, and accordingly,
  \begin{equation*}
    \bar\nabla'_XY - \bar\nabla_XY \in \Gamma(A^{j+k+l}) \ni
      \bar\nabla'_YX - \bar\nabla_YX,
  \end{equation*}
  where the second part follows by symmetry. Claim \eqref{plat1} follows immediately.

  Similarly expanding $\tau'=(\Adjoint_\psi)_*\tau$, one derives
  \begin{multline}
    \tau'(X, Y) - \tau(X, Y) = -\tau(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#X)), Y) -
    \tau(X, \psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y)))\\
    + \tau(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#X)), \psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y)))\\
    + \psi(\# \tau(X, Y))\\
    - \psi(\#\tau(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#X))), Y) - \psi(\#\tau(X, \psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y))))\\
    + \psi(\#(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#X)), \psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y)))).
  \end{multline}
  By our Preliminary Observations, the terms on the second and fourth lines lie in
  $A^{j+k+2l}$, while that on the last line lies in $A^{j+k+3l}$, which means both lie in
  $A^{j+k+l +1}$. The remaining terms lie in $A^{j+k+l}$, which shows that $r$ is indeed
  homogeneous of degree $l$, and
  \begin{multline*}
    \tau'(X, Y) - \tau(X, Y) \equiv \\
      -\tau(\psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#X)), Y) -
      \tau(X, \psi(\psi_{TM}^{-1}(\#Y))) +  \psi(\#\tau(X, Y))
    \modulo A^{j+k+l+1},
  \end{multline*}
  from which \eqref{plat2} now readily follows.
\end{proof}

\subsection{The Bianchi identity}\label{section_bianchi}
The next result will be used later to prove Theorem \eqrefs{harmonic}{poker3}.
\begin{proposition}
  Let $(A, \nabla, \tau)$ be a transitive, infinitesimal, filtered Cartan geometry, as in
  Proposition \ref{curv_deform}, and suppose $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l \ge
  1$. Then $\partial \graded_l(\kappa) = 0$.
\end{proposition}
\noindent%
This result is the degree-reduced version of the following {\df Bianchi identity} for the model curvature,
\begin{multline}
  \label{bianchi}
  (\bar\nabla_{X_3}\kappa)(\#X_1, \#X_2) + \kappa (\kappa (\#X_1, \#X_2), \#X_3) \\
   + \kappa(\# \tau(X_1, X_2), \#X_3) + \tau(\kappa(\#X_1, \#X_2), X_3) + \text{terms cyclic in 1-2-3} = 0,
\end{multline}
which we prove below.
\begin{proof}[Proof of proposition]
   Suppose $X_1, X_2, X_3 \in \Gamma(A)$ (which are arbitrary
   above) have {\em negative} filtration degrees, summing to $m$. Then, as $\bar\nabla $
   is filtration-preserving, and as $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l\ge 1$, it
   follows that $(\bar\nabla_{X_3}\kappa)(\#X_1, \#X_2)$, and its cyclic permutations, have at
   most degree $m + l + 1$. The second term in \eqref{bianchi} will have degree at most
   $m + 2l$, which likewise, cannot exceed $ m + l + 1$. So,
  \begin{equation*}
    \kappa(\# \tau(X_1, X_2), \#X_3) + \tau(\kappa(\#X_1, \#X_2), X_3) +
    \text{terms cyclic in 1-2-3} \equiv 0 \modulo A^{m + l + 1}.
  \end{equation*}
  Evidently, both the terms on the left-hand have degree $m + l$, and from this the
  proposition now follows easily, noting that under our identification
  $\graded(A)_{-}\cong \graded(TM)$, $\graded_0(\#)$ is the just the projection
  $\graded(TM) \oplus \graded(A^0) \rightarrow \graded(TM)$.
\end{proof}

For a proof  of \eqref{bianchi}, first note that any infinitesimal Cartan connection
$\nabla $ on a Lie algebroid, one has the Bianchi identity
\begin{equation}
    \label{pq1}
    (\bar\nabla_{X_3}\torsion \bar\nabla )(X_1, X_2) + \torsion \bar\nabla
      (\torsion    \bar\nabla (X_1, X_2), X_3) + \text{terms cyclic in 1-2-3} = 0.
\end{equation}
See \cite[Example 6.5]{Blaom_12}. Since $\bar\nabla \tau  = 0$
(compatibility), it follows from the definition of $\kappa $ that
\begin{equation*}
  (\bar\nabla_{X_3}\kappa )(\#X_1, \#X_2) = (\bar\nabla_{X_3}\torsion \bar\nabla )(X_1, X_2)
\end{equation*}
holds, as do the cyclicly permuted variants thereof. Using these in \eqref{pq1}, we obtain
\begin{equation*}
  (\bar\nabla_{X_3}\kappa)(\#X_1, \#X_2) + \torsion \bar\nabla
  (\torsion    \bar\nabla (X_1, X_2), X_3) + \text{terms cyclic in 1-2-3} = 0.
\end{equation*}
Rearranging the definition of $\kappa $, we have
$\torsion \bar\nabla(X,Y) = \kappa(\#X, \#Y) + \tau(X, Y)$, which we can use to rewrite
this last equation as \eqref{bianchi} above.


\subsection{Hodge theory}\label{hodge}
To the blanket assumptions of \ref{homology} we now add the following assumptions: $\tau $
is reductive, $A^0$ is a fibre-wise parabolic subalgebra of $(A, \tau)$, and the
filtration of $A$ is the associated canonical one. Then $T=\graded (\tau)$ is reductive
and $|A| \le k$ for some $k \in {\mathbb Z} $ (Lemma
\ref{parabolics_from_filtrations}). In this case, as we now explain, $\partial^*$ and
$\partial $ enjoy a relationship analogous to that between the differential and
codifferential operators of classical Hodge theory \cite{Warner_83}. Here we are trivially
extending to semisimple Lie algebra {\em bundles} observations about semisimple Lie {\em
  algebras} originating in Kostant \cite{Kostant_61}, and detailed in
\cite[\S3.3]{Cap_Slovak_09}.

Fixing some multiple of the Killing form for $T$, we may identify
$\graded(A^1)=\graded(A)_1 \oplus \graded(A)_2 \oplus \cdots \graded(A)_k$ with
$\graded(A)_{-}^*=(\graded(A)_{-k}\oplus \cdots \oplus \graded(A)_{-2}\oplus
\graded(A)_{-1})^*$. Under this identification, we can combine
\eqrefs{curv_deform}{chain5a} and \eqrefs{homology}{chain2} into one diagram
\begin{equation}
  \label{chain5} 0 \xrightleftharpoons[\partial^*]{\partial}  \graded(A)
  \xrightleftharpoons[\partial^*]{\partial} \graded(A^1) \otimes \graded(A)
  \xrightleftharpoons[\partial^*]{\partial} \wedge^2 \graded(A^1) \otimes \graded(A)
  \xrightleftharpoons[\partial^*]{\partial} \cdots.
\end{equation}
As we shall see, the following non-trivial decomposition, together with the calculation
summarised in Proposition \ref{curv_deform}, is what makes normalization of curvature in
parabolic geometry possible:
\begin{equation}
  \label{nontrivial} \image\partial_{n-1}\oplus\kernel \partial_n^* =
  \boxed{\wedge^n \graded(A^1) \otimes \graded(A) =
  \kernel \partial_n \oplus \image \partial_{n+1}^*}\,.% \qquad n \in {\mathbb Z}.
\end{equation}
As in Hodge theory, this follows from the existence (over each point $m \in M$) of an
inner product with respect to which $\partial_n$ is the adjoint of $\partial_{n+1}^*$, for
any $n$; in the construction, omitted here, the non-degenerate Killing form of $T$ plays
an essential role. See \cite[\S3.3]{Cap_Slovak_09} for details. And, as in Hodge theory,
we can deduce from \eqref{nontrivial}:

\begin{theorem}
  If $\square := \partial^* \partial + \partial \partial^*$, then
  \begin{gather}
    \kernel\square_n = \kernel_n \partial_n\cap \kernel_n \partial_n^*\label{hodge1}\\
    \text{and}\qquad\wedge^n\graded(A^1)\otimes \graded(A) =
    \image \partial_{n-1} \oplus \kernel\square_n \oplus \image \partial_{n+1}^*.
    \label{hodge2}
  \end{gather}
  In particular, the projection
  $\kernel \partial_n \rightarrow H^n(\graded(A)_-, \graded(A))$ restricts to an
  isomorphism $\kernel \square_n \cong H^n(\graded(A)_-, \graded(A))$, while the
  projection $\kernel \partial_n^* \rightarrow H_n(\graded(A^1), \graded(A))$ restricts to
  an isomorphism $\kernel \square_n \cong H_n(\graded(A^1), \graded(A))$.
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
Here $H^n(\graded(A)_-, \graded(A))$ is the degree-$n$ $\partial$-cohomology of
\eqref{chain5}, which by the theorem, is isomorphic to the $\partial^*$-homology
$H_n(\graded(A^1), \graded(A))$, which is canonically isomorphic to the
$\partial^\tau$-homology $H_n(A^1, A)$, by Proposition \ref{homology}. That is, the
(co)homologies of the chain complexes \eqrefs{homology}{chain1}, \eqrefs{homology}{chain2}
and \eqrefs{curv_deform}{chain5a} are all canonically isomorphic.

The next result is an immediate consequence of \eqrefs{homology}{hay3} and transitivity,
which gives an identification $\graded(A)_-\cong \graded(TM)$. It connects us to the point
of view in the principal bundle approach, but is also important because, following from
the work of \cite{Kostant_61}, the cohomology groups $H^n({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})$
are known in the cases we will need them \cite[\S3.3]{Cap_Slovak_09}.
\begin{proposition}
  Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a parabolic geometry with Lie algebroid $A$, and let
  ${\mathfrak g} $ be a filtered Lie algebra constituting a model for the semisimple Lie
  algebra bundle structure $\tau$; without loss of generality, we may suppose
  ${\mathfrak g} $ is actually graded (Proposition \eqrefs{canonical_filtrations_}{hure}),
  and let ${\mathfrak g}_-=\oplus_{j<0} {\mathfrak g}_j$. Then the mutually isomorphic
  (co)homology vector bundles
  $$H_n(A^1,A),\quad H_n(\graded(A^1),\graded(A)),\quad H^n(\graded(TM),\graded(A))$$
  are associated bundles for a representation of the vertex group of
  ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ over some point $m_0 \in M$ on the Lie algebra cohomology
  $H^n({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g}) $.
\end{proposition}
\begin{remark}
  By Remark \ref{regularity_}, the vertex group is reductive, its Lie algebra being
  identifiable with the reductive Levi quotient
  ${\mathfrak p}/{\mathfrak g}^1 \cong {\mathfrak g}_0$, where ${\mathfrak p}
  =\oplus_{j<0}{\mathfrak g}_j$ and ${\mathfrak g}^0=\oplus_{j\ge0}{\mathfrak g}_j$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Tractor bundles}\label{tractors}
This section, not referenced in the sequel, is included to explain the relationship
between the infinitesimal Cartan connections of the current approach, and the adjoint
tractor connections of the principal bundle approach. Tractor bundles are relevant to the
problem of constructing invariant differential operators, which is beyond the scope of the
present exposition.

Let $({\mathcal G}, D, \tau)$ be a transitive, filtered, Cartan geometry and $\nabla $ the
corresponding infinitesimal Cartan connection on the Lie algebroid $A$ of ${\mathcal G} $.
Recall that strong compatibility means that the representation $\bar\nabla$ of $A$ on
itself as a Lie algebroid, and the representation of $A$ on itself as a Lie algebra
bundle, $X \mapsto \adjoint^\tau(X) := \tau(X,\,\cdot\,)$, restrict to the same
representation of the anchor kernel $A^0$. So, we have
\begin{gather*}
  \adjoint^\tau_Y X = \bar\nabla_YX;\qquad Y \in \Gamma(A^0),\, X \in \Gamma(A)\\
  \bar D_g\adjoint^\tau_Y X = \adjoint^\tau_{\bar D_gY}\bar D_gX; \qquad Y,\,X \in A|_m,\,m \in M,\, g \in {\mathcal G},
\end{gather*}
where the second identity is just the $\bar D $-invariance of $\tau $, and we
understand $g$ to be some arrow beginning at $m$.

More generally, a {\df tractor bundle} is any representation $E$ of ${\mathcal G}$,
together with some infinitesimal representation $\adjoint^\tau$ of the Lie algebra bundle
$(A, \tau)$ on $E$, satisfying
\begin{gather}
  \adjoint^\tau_Y\sigma  = Y \sigma;\qquad Y \in \Gamma(A^0),\, \sigma \in \Gamma(E)\label{hj1}\\
  g\cdot\adjoint^\tau_Y \sigma = \adjoint^\tau_{\bar D_gY}(g\cdot \sigma);
  \qquad Y \in A|_m,\,m \in M,\, \sigma \in E,\, g \in {\mathcal G}.\label{hj2}
\end{gather}
Here we use juxtaposition to denote the action of $A$ on $E$ as a Lie algebroid (the
infinitesimalization of the representation of ${\mathcal G}$ on $E$). Note that \ref{hj2}
has the infinitesimalization
\begin{equation}
  \label{apfel}
  X\adjoint_Y^\tau \sigma = \adjoint_Y^\tau (X\sigma) + \adjoint^\tau_{\bar\nabla_XY}\sigma;
    \qquad X, Y \in \Gamma(A),\, \sigma \in \Gamma(E).
\end{equation}

The significance of tractor bundles is that they come equipped with Koszul
connections, called {\df tractor connections}, well-defined by
\begin{equation*}
  \nabla^E_{\#X} \sigma = X \sigma - \adjoint^\tau_X \sigma.
\end{equation*}
Here $\#$ denotes anchor. With the help of \eqref{apfel}, one computes
\begin{equation*}
  \curvature \nabla^E(U, V) \sigma = -\adjoint^\tau_{\kappa(U, V)}\sigma.
\end{equation*}

Returning to the special case $E=A$, where $\nabla^A$ is known
as the {\df adjoint tractor connection}, we readily derive the formula
\begin{equation}
  \nabla^A_VY = \nabla_VY + \kappa(V, \#Y),\label{tractor1}
\end{equation}
which proves the following:
%
\begin{aside}
  The infinitesimal Cartan connection is the adjoint tractor connection if and only if the
  model curvature vanishes.
\end{aside}

For an explicit construction of tractor bundles, connecting them with the definition
appearing in \cite{Cap_Slovak_09}, we adopt the principal bundle point of view. Fix some
arbitrary $m_0 \in M$, let $\Pi \subset {\mathcal G} $ denote all arrows beginning at
$m_0$, $P \subset \Pi$ the Lie group of arrows beginning and ending at $m_0$,
${\mathfrak g} $ the semisimple Lie algebra $(A|_{m_0}, \tau)$, and ${\mathfrak g}^0$ the
Lie algebra of $P$. Transitivity means $\Pi $ will be a principal $P$-bundle. Now let $V$
be a finite dimensional representation of $P$ and suppose the corresponding infinitesimal
representation ${\mathfrak g}^0 \rightarrow \gl(V)$ is the restriction of a representation
$\rho \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow \gl(V)$. Then, we have:

\begin{proposition}
  The associated bundle $E(\rho) = \Pi \times_P V$ is a tractor bundle, and all tractor
  bundles are of the form $E(\rho)$ for some
  $\rho \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow \gl(V)$ as above.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
For elements $g_1, g_2 \in \Pi $ and $\xi \in {\mathfrak g} $, let us write
\begin{gather*}
  \langle g_1, g_2 \rangle  = g_1g_2^{-1} \in {\mathcal G}\\
  \langle g_2, \xi \rangle  = \bar D_{g_2} \xi \in A.
\end{gather*}
Transitivity means arbitrary elements of ${\mathcal G} $ and $A$ are of this form, and we have
\begin{equation}
  \bar D_{\langle g_1, g_2 \rangle } \langle g_2, \xi \rangle  = \langle g_1, \xi \rangle .\label{hj3}
\end{equation}
If, for each $[g_2, v] \equiv (g_2, v) \modulo P \in E(\rho)$, we define
\begin{equation*}
  \adjoint^\tau_{\langle g_2, \xi \rangle} [g_2, v]= [g_2, \rho_\xi v],
\end{equation*}
then, with the help of \eqref{hj3}, it is easy to see that \eqref{hj1} and \eqref{hj2}
hold.  The proof of the second assertion is left to the reader.
\end{proof}

\refreshpage
\section{Parabolic geometries. Part II}\label{parabolic_proofs}

This section proves results only stated in Section \ref{parabolic_summary}.

\subsection{All parabolic geometries admit compatible Weyl gauges}\label{all}
Suppose $\tau $ is the model data associated with a parabolic geometry (global or
infinitesimal). Then, according to Lemma \ref{parabolics_from_filtrations},
$T = \graded_0(\tau)$ is a semisimple Lie algebra bundle structure on $\graded(A)$, where
$A$ is the underlying filtered Lie algebroid. By Lemma \ref{grading_elements}, we
accordingly have a {\em unique} section $E$ of $\graded(A)_0$ that is a fibre-wise grading
section of $(\graded(A), T)$, what we shall call the {\df canonical grading section}. We
prove the following after stating some consequences:
\begin{proposition}
  For any global or infinitesimal parabolic geometry, any lift $\epsilon$ of the canonical
  grading section $E$ is a Weyl gauge compatible with the model data $\tau $, and all Weyl
  gauges compatible with $\tau$ are lifts of this form.
\end{proposition}

The existence of Weyl gauges, combined with Corollary \ref{model_data_gradings}, delivers:
%
\begin{corollaryA}
  In a global or infinitesimal parabolic geometry, any Cartan connection compatible with
  the model data is automatically compatible with the Lie algebroid filtration.
\end{corollaryA}

Having already established, in Theorem \ref{uniquely_determined}, that the (infinitesimal)
Cartan connections associated with (infinitesimal) parabolic geometries are
automatically strongly compatible with the model data, we obtain:
%
\begin{corollaryB}
  Every (infinitesimal) parabolic geometry is an (infinitesimal) filtered Cartan geometry.
\end{corollaryB}
\noindent%
Theorem \ref{abstract_levi_}, which describes all filtered Cartan geometries as
semi-direct products, now gives:
%
\begin{corollaryC}
  Every Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ admitting the structure of a parabolic geometry
  $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$, and in particular, every action groupoid
  ${\mathcal G} = G \times G/P$ of a generalized flag manifold, is isomorphic to the
  semi-direct product ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1 \times_{R^D}\exp \graded(T^*\!M)$. Here
  $D$ is a $\tau $-compatible Cartan connection (defined by \eqrefs{cartan}{adjoint}, in
  the flag manifold case) and ${\mathcal G}^1$ and the representation $R^D$ have their
  meanings described in \ref{reduction_} and \ref{existence}.
\end{corollaryC}

\begin{proof}[Proof of proposition]
  This time we begin with the global case which is simpler.  Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be
  a parabolic geometry, $M$ the base manifold, and $D$ a Cartan connection compatible with
  $\tau $ and the filtration of the Lie algebroid $A$ of $\mathcal G$.  Since $\tau $ is
  $\bar D$-invariant ($D$ and $\tau $ are compatible) $T$ is invariant with respect to the
  associated representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on $\graded(A)$, that we have also been
  denoting $\bar D $ (see \ref{reduction_}). In particular, for any arrow
  $g \in {\mathcal G} $ beginning at some $m \in M$, $\bar D_g$ must map grading elements
  of $\graded(A)|_m$ to grading elements of $\graded(A)|_{gm}$, which, by the
  aforementioned uniqueness, means $\bar D_gE(m) = E(gm)$, i.e., $E$ is
  $\bar D$-invariant, which is the same as $\Adjoint$-invariance, since ${\bar D}$
  restricts to the canonical representation on $A^0$. It follows that any lift $\epsilon$
  of $E$ through $A^0 \rightarrow A^0/A^1$ will be a Weyl gauge for ${\mathcal G} $. It is
  not hard to see that every Weyl gauge is of this form.

  Turning to the infinitesimal case, let $(A, \tau)$ be an infinitesimal parabolic
  geometry and $\nabla$ a Cartan connection compatible with $\tau $ and the filtration of
  $A$. Since $\tau $ is $\bar\nabla $-invariant (compatibility) $T$ is invariant with
  respect to the associated representation of $A$ on $\graded(A)$, which we have also been
  denoting by $\bar\nabla $. Let $X \in \Gamma(A)$, $j \in {\mathbb Z} $ and
  $Y \in \Gamma(\graded(A)_j)$ be arbitrary. Then $T(E, Y) = jY$ and, applying
  $\bar\nabla_X $ to both sides, we obtain, courtesy of the $\bar\nabla$-invariance of
  $T$,
  \begin{equation}
    \label{won}
    T(\bar\nabla_X E, Y) + T(E, \bar\nabla_X Y) = j \bar\nabla_X Y.
  \end{equation}
  On the other hand, the grading of $\graded(A)$ is $\bar\nabla$-invariant (the filtration
  of $A$ is $\bar\nabla$-invariant) so that $\bar\nabla_X Y \in \Gamma(\graded(A)_j)$,
  implying $T(E, \bar\nabla_X Y) = j \bar\nabla_X Y$. From \eqref{won} we now conclude
  that $T( \bar\nabla_X E, Y) =0$. From the arbitrariness of our choice of $j$ and $Y$
  above, we conclude that $ \bar\nabla_X E$ is fibre-wise in the center of a semisimple
  Lie algebra, and consequently vanishes. As $\bar\nabla $ restricts to the canonical
  representation on $\graded(A^0)$, this shows that $E$ is
  $\adjoint$-invariant. Accordingly, any lift $\epsilon$ of $E$ through
  $A^0 \rightarrow A^0/A^1$ will be a Weyl gauge for $A$. It is not hard to see that every
  Weyl connection is of this form.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Axiom P3 holds everywhere if it holds at a point}\label{parabolic}

\begin{proposition}
  If $M$ is connected then, in the global groupoid case, and in the case of an integrable
  Lie algebroid, it suffices to check that Axiom P3 of \ref{parabolic_defined} holds over
  a single point $m \in M$.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
  We begin with the global case. Assume P3 for parabolic geometries holds at $m_1 \in M$
  and let $m_2 \in M$ be arbitrary. By transitivity, there exists an arrow
  $g \in {\mathcal G} $ from $m_1$ to $m_2$. If $D$ is a Cartan connection on
  ${\mathcal G} $ compatible with $\tau $, then compatibility means $\bar D_g$ is a Lie
  algebra isomorphism between $(A|_{m_1}, \tau|_{m_1})$ and $(A|_{m_2}, \tau|_{m_2})$. It
  is also filtration-preserving, because $D$ is automatically compatible with the
  filtration (Corollary B above). It follows that P3 holds at $m_2$.

  In the integrable, infinitesimal case, we may suppose $A$ is integrated by some Lie
  groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ with simply connected source fibres, so that the representation
  $\bar\nabla $ integrates to a representation $\bar D$, which must leave $\tau $ and the
  filtration of $A$ invariant, because $\bar\nabla $ does. We can now apply the preceding
  arguments verbatim.
\end{proof}

\subsection{On the Lie subgroupoid ${\mathcal G}^1$ integrating $A^1$}\label{subgroups}

\begin{proposition}
  Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a parabolic geometry, and let
  ${\mathcal G}^1 \subset {\mathcal G} $ denote the source-connected subgroupoid
  integrating $A^1$.  Then ${\mathcal G}^1 $ is a bundle of simply-connected nilpotent Lie
  groups; it is also a normal subgroupoid of ${\mathcal G}$, so that
  ${\mathcal G}/{\mathcal G}^1$ is a Lie groupoid, also transitive.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  The first result claim follows from Proposition \eqrefs{weyl2}{xerox2}, the second from
  Theorem \eqrefs{weyl2}{pr1}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{The existence of parabolic geometries with given Levi reduction}\label{existence_}

Corollary B above shows that that following result is a special case of Theorem
\eqrefs{abstract_levi_}{chh02}:

\begin{theorem}[Existence]
  Every Levi structure $({\mathcal H}, T, R)$ is, up to isomorphism, the reduction of some
  parabolic geometry $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$. If the Levi structure is regular, then the
  parabolic geometry can be chosen to be regular.
\end{theorem}

\subsection{Curvature under automorphisms}\label{transform_}
Theorem \ref{uniquely_determined} shows that the model curvature is unambiguously defined
by the model data alone, in the special case of a global or infinitesimal parabolic
geometry.  As remarked in \ref{normalization}, we can use the Killing form to view model
curvature as a section of $\wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A$, which appears in a natural chain
complex with differential $\partial^\tau$ defined by
\eqrefs{uniquely_determined}{form1}. This alternative representation of the curvature
transforms naturally under automorphisms:

\begin{theorem} Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be any
  parabolic geometry and $Q \colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G}'$ a morphism of
  filtered Lie groupoids, in the sense of \ref{morphisms}. Then $({\mathcal G}', \tau')$
  is a parabolic geometry, where $\tau'=q_*\tau $ and $q := dQ \colon A \rightarrow A'$ is
  the infinitesimalization of $Q$. In particular, $(A',\tau')$ is an infinitesimal
  parabolic geometry.

  More generally, for any infinitesimal parabolic geometry $(A, \tau)$, and
  filtration-preserving Lie algebroid morphism $q \colon A \rightarrow A'$, we have: (i)
  $(A', \tau')$ is an infinitesimal parabolic geometry; (ii) $\tau' $ is regular if and
  only if $\tau $ is regular; and (iii) The corresponding model curvatures, $\kappa$ and
  $\kappa'$, when expressed as sections of $\wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A$ and
  $\wedge^2 (A')^1 \otimes A'$, are $q$-related, i.e., $\kappa' = q_* \kappa $, where
  $q_*(Z_1\wedge Z_2 \otimes X) := q_*Z_1\wedge q_*Z_2 \otimes q_*X$. In particular, if
  $\tau $ is normal, then so is $\tau'$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
  The very last assertion follows from the others, for it is easy to see, from the
  definition \eqrefs{normalization}{form1} of $\partial^\tau$, that
  $\partial^{\tau'} \circ q_* = q_* \circ \partial^\tau$. These other claims follow
  readily from the Proposition \ref{transform} and Theorem \ref{uniquely_determined}, with
  the exception of the transformation rule $\kappa' = q_* \kappa $. To prove it, remember
  that the identification $T^*\!M \cong A^1$, needed to view curvature as a section of
  $\wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A$, depends on the model data $\tau $, while in the case of
  $T^*\!M' \cong (A')^1$, it depends on $\tau'$. With this in mind, we see then that the
  rule follows from \eqrefs{transform}{tarragon5} and the lemma below.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}
  Let $\iota\colon A^1 \rightarrow T^*\!M $ denote the isomorphism induced by the
  $\tau$-Killing form, $B$ (or some fixed multiple thereof), and
  ${\iota'} \colon (A')^1 \rightarrow T^*\!M' $ the isomorphism induced by the
  $\tau'$-Killing form, $B'$ (or the same fixed multiple thereof). Then the
  following diagram commutes:
  \begin{equation*}\label{morph}
    \begin{CD}
      A^1 @>{q}>> (A')^1 \\
       @V{\iota}VV @VV{{\iota'}}V \\
       T^*\!M @>{(Tf^{-1})^*}>> T^*\!M
     \end{CD}\enspace.
   \end{equation*}
   Here $f \colon M \rightarrow M'$ is the map covered by $q \colon A \rightarrow A'$ and
   $(Tf^{-1})^*$ denotes the adjoint of $Tf^{-1}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}[Proof of lemma]
  It is easy to see the choice of multiplicative constant is immaterial, so suppose it is
  one. Now $B'(X, Y) = B(q^{-1}X, q^{-1}Y)$, because
  $\adjoint^{\tau'} = q \circ \adjoint^\tau \circ q^{-1}$. On the other hand, $\iota$ is
  defined implicitly by $\langle \iota X, \#Y\rangle = B(X, Y)$, for arbitrary sections
  $X$ and $Y$ of $A^1$ and $A$, respectively, and we have an analogous characterization
  for ${\iota'}$. We now compute, for arbitrary $X \in A^1|_m$, $Y \in A|_m$, $m \in M$,
  \begin{multline*}
    \langle {\iota'}qX, \# Y\rangle = B'(qX, Y) = B(X, q^{-1}Y)
    = \langle \iota X,\# q^{-1}Y\rangle \\
    = \langle \iota X,(Tf^{-1})(\#Y)\rangle
    = \langle (Tf^{-1})^*\iota)X,\#Y\rangle.
  \end{multline*}
\end{proof}

\subsection{Normalizing model curvature}\label{normalization_}

We turn now to the proof of the following central result:

\begin{theorem}[Normalization]
  Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a regular parabolic
  geometry. Then there exists {\bfseries normal}, model data $\tau'$ such that
  $({\mathcal G} , \tau')$ is a regular parabolic geometry with the same Levi reduction.
\end{theorem}

Let $A$ be the Lie algebroid of ${\mathcal G} $.
\begin{lemmaA}
  Suppose $\sigma \in \Gamma(\wedge^nA^1 \otimes A)$ and $\partial^\tau \sigma $ is
  homogeneous of degree $l$. Then there exists
  $\tilde \sigma \in \Gamma(\wedge^n A^1 \otimes A)$, homogeneous of degree $l$, such that
  $\partial^\tau \tilde \sigma = \partial^\tau \sigma $.
\end{lemmaA}
\begin{proof}
  Any lift $\epsilon $ of the canonical grading section $E$ of $\graded(A)$ defines a
  splitting $A = \oplus_{j \in {\mathbb Z}}A_j$ for the filtration of $A$, which in turn
  gives us a grading of $C^n := \wedge^n A^1 \otimes A$, which is naturally filtered;
  specifically, $C^n_m$ is all finite sums of expressions of the form
  $Z_1\wedge \ldots \wedge Z_n \otimes  X$, where $Z_{i_j} \in A_j$ ($i_j \ge 1$), $X \in A_k$
  ($k \in {\mathbb Z}$), and $i_1+ \cdots + i_n + k = m$. In particular, writing
  $\sigma = \sum_m \sigma_m$ (finite sum) for some $\sigma_m \in C^n_m$, we have
  $\sum_m\partial^\tau \sigma_m \in (C^{n-1})^l$, by hypothesis. But from this is follows
  that $\partial^\tau \sigma_j=0$ for all $j< l$, because $\partial^\tau $ maps $C^n_m$ to
  $C^{n-1}_m$, for all $m$. We may therefore take
  $\tilde \sigma = \sum_{j\ge l} \sigma_j$.
\end{proof}
\begin{notation}
  If $\tau'$ is also model data on $A$, and if $\tau'=(\Adjoint_\psi)_*\tau $, for some
  $\psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$, homogeneous of degree $l$, then we will write
  $\tau\equivalent{l} \tau' $.
\end{notation}

\begin{lemmaB}
  If $\sigma \in \Gamma(\wedge^nA^1 \otimes A)$ and $\tau'\equivalent{l}\tau $, then
  $\partial^{\tau'} \sigma -\partial^\tau \sigma $ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$
\end{lemmaB}
\begin{proof}
  Expanding the definition of $\Adjoint_\psi $ (Equation \eqrefs{deformations}{opal2a}) it is
  not difficult to see that $\tau'\equivalent{l}\tau $ implies that $\tau' - \tau $ is
  homogeneous of degree $l$. The lemma follows easily from this fact and the definition of
  $\partial^\tau$ (Equation \eqrefs{normalization}{form1}).
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of theorem]
  Recall that {\df normal} means $\partial^\tau \kappa =0$. To prove existence of normal
  model data it suffices to prove the following:
  \begin{aside}
    If $\partial^\tau\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l \ge 1$, then there exists model
    data $\tau'$ with $\tau'\equivalent{l} \tau $ such that $\partial^{\tau'}\kappa $ is
    homogeneous of degree $l + 1$, where $\kappa'$ is the model curvature of $\tau'$.
  \end{aside}
  For then the theorem follows from an induction on $l$, an induction anchored by the fact
  that $\kappa $ (and hence $\partial^\tau \kappa)$) is homogeneous of degree one
  (Proposition \ref{model_curvature}).

  So, suppose $\partial^\tau \kappa$ is homogeneous of degree $l\ge 1$. Recall that
  $\graded$ is a chain map between the chain complexes \eqrefs{homology}{chain3} and
  \eqrefs{homology}{chain4}, so that we have the identity
  \begin{equation}
    \label{wood0}
    \partial^*\circ \graded_l = \graded_l \circ \partial^\tau.
  \end{equation}
  Identifying $T^*\!M$ with $A^1$ using the Killing form of $\tau$, there exists, by Lemma
  A, $\tilde\kappa \in \Gamma(\wedge^2 T^*\!M \otimes A)$, homogeneous of degree $l$, such
  that $\partial^\tau \tilde \kappa = \partial^\tau \kappa $.  From the boxed equation in
  \eqrefs{hodge}{nontrivial}, there exists $\psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$,
  homogeneous of degree $l$, such that
  $\graded_l(\tilde \kappa) - \partial \graded_l(\psi)$ lies in $\kernel \partial^*$, so
  that $\partial^* \graded_l(\tilde \kappa) = \partial^* \partial \graded_l (\psi
  )$. Applying \eqref{wood0} to the left-hand side, we can write this as
  \begin{equation}
    \label{wood1}
    \graded_l(\partial^\tau \kappa ) = \partial^* \partial \graded_l(\psi),
  \end{equation}
  which eliminates the auxiliary variable $\tilde \kappa$. On the other hand, if we take
  $\tau' =(\Adjoint_\psi)_*\tau $, then Proposition \ref{curv_deform} tells us that
  $\kappa' - \kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l$ with
  \begin{equation}
    \label{wood2}
    \graded_l(\kappa'-\kappa) = -\partial \graded_l(\psi).
  \end{equation}
  Now as both $\kappa' - \kappa $ and $\partial^\tau \kappa $ are homogeneous of degree
  $l$, so is $\partial^\tau \kappa'$. But, applying $\partial^*$ to both sides of
  \eqref{wood2} and applying \eqref{wood0} again on the left, we obtain
  $\graded_l(\partial^\tau \kappa' - \partial^\tau \kappa) = -\partial^* \partial
  \graded_l(\psi)$, which can accordingly be written
  \begin{equation*}
    \graded_l(\partial^\tau \kappa')
    = \graded_l(\partial^\tau \kappa) - \partial^* \partial
  \graded_l(\psi)
  \end{equation*}
  Substituting \eqref{wood1}, we obtain $\graded_l(\partial^\tau \kappa')=0 $ which, on
  account of Lemma B, shows $\graded_l(\partial^{\tau'}\kappa') =0$, as required.

  By construction, the normal model data $\tau'$ is a $\psi$-deformation of $\tau
  $. Keeping Theorem \ref{uniquely_determined} in mind, it follows, as a special case of
  Proposition \eqrefs{deformations}{saturn5}, that $(A', \tau')$ is an infinitesimal parabolic
  geometry with the same Levi reduction as $(A, \tau)$, and in the global case that
  $({\mathcal G}, \tau')$ is a parabolic geometry with the same Levi reduction as
  $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$.

\end{proof}

\subsection{Harmonic curvature as an obstruction to symmetry}\label{top_curvature}
Recall that for model curvature to vanish, it suffices that the harmonic curvature
vanishes (Corollary \ref{harmonic}). The proof of the this result rested on Theorem
\eqrefs{harmonic}{poker3}, restated here for convenience:

\begin{proposition}
  Let $\kappa $ be the model curvature of a regular and normal parabolic geometry
  $({\mathcal G} , \tau)$. Then if $\kappa $ is homogeneous of $l \ge 1$ and
  $(\kappa_{\mathrm H})_l = 0$, then $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  Choose any Weyl gauge $\epsilon$ to get a splitting
  $\tilde \Phi_\epsilon \colon \wedge^2 \graded(A^1) \otimes \graded(A) \rightarrow
  \wedge^2 A^1 \otimes A$, as in Proposition \ref{homology}. Then, as $\kappa $ is
  homogeneous of degree $l$, we may write
  $(\tilde \Phi_\epsilon)^*\kappa =\sum_{i\ge l} \kappa_i$, for some sections $\kappa_i$
  of $(\wedge^2 \graded(A^1) \otimes \graded(A))_i$ satisfying $\partial^* \kappa_i =0$,
  because $\partial^\tau \kappa = 0$. Since $(\kappa_{\mathrm H})_l = 0$, we can see that
  the homology class $[\kappa_l]$ must vanish. This follows from the proposition just
  cited and the definition of the natural grading of $H_2(A^1, A)$ given there. However,
  $\partial \graded_l(\kappa) = 0$, by Proposition \ref{section_bianchi}, i.e.,
  $\partial \kappa_l = 0$, so that in fact
  $\kappa_l \in \kernel \partial_2 \cap \kernel\partial^*_2 = \kernel \square_2$. But,
  according to Theorem \ref{hodge}, the map
  $\omega \rightarrow [\omega] \colon \kernel\partial_2^* \rightarrow H_2(\graded(A^1),
  \graded(A))$ is, when restricted to $\kernel \square_2$, injective. Since $[\kappa_l]=0$,
  so then $\kappa_l=0$, and we conclude $\kappa $ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Uniqueness up to Weyl gauge transformations}\label{un}

\begin{theorem}[Uniqueness] Let $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ be a regular parabolic
  geometry and let ${\mathfrak g} $ be Lie algebra model for $\tau $, identified with a
  graded reductive Lie algebra, as detailed at the end of \ref{harmonic}. Assume
  $H^1({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1=0$.  Then if $({\mathcal G}, \tau')$ is second
  parabolic geometry with $\graded_0(\tau')=\graded_0(\tau)$, then if both $\tau $ and
  $\tau'$ are normal, then $\tau' = q_*\tau$ for some infinitesimal Weyl gauge
  transformation $q$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
  Write $\tau_1 := \tau$. We will construct a sequence of infinitesimal Weyl gauge
  transformations $q_1, q_2, q_3, \ldots$, such that for any $l > 1$,
  $\tau' \equivalent{l} \tau_l$ (see Notation \ref{normalization_}) where
  $\tau_l := (q_{l-1})_*\tau_{l-1}$. Then, for sufficiently large $k$, we will have
  $\tau' = \tau_k$, because any $\psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$ of sufficiently high a
  degree of homogeneity vanishes, making $\Adjoint_\psi $ the identity. In that case, we
  will have $\tau' = q_* \tau$, where $q = q_{k-1} \circ q_{k-2} \circ \cdots \circ q_1$,
  and the theorem will be proven, because normality is preserved by Weyl gauge
  transformations (Theorem \ref{transform_}), as is regularity, because we will have
  $\graded_0(\tau')=\graded_0(\tau)$, by Lemma \ref{deformations}.

  So, for an induction, suppose $\tau_l$ is model data such that
  \begin{gather}
    \tau' \equivalent{l} \tau_l,\label{eins}\\
    \text{$\tau_l$ is regular with normal curvature $\kappa_l$,}\label{zwei}
  \end{gather}
  If $l=1$, \eqref{eins} holds, by Proposition \eqrefs{deformations}{saturn1};
  \eqref{zwei} holds by hypothesis. This anchors our induction. Our remaining task is to
  construct an infinitesimal Weyl gauge transformation $q_l$ such that
  $\tau_{l+1} := (q_l)_*\tau_l$ satisfies the above with $l$ replaced with $l + 1$.

  Now \eqref{eins} is shorthand for the existence of some
  $\psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$, homogeneous of degree $l$, such that
  $\tau'=(\Adjoint_\psi)_*\tau_l$. By Proposition \ref{curv_deform}, $\kappa' - \kappa_l$
  will be homogeneous of degree $l$, with
  \begin{equation}
    \label{vier}
    \graded_l(\kappa' - \kappa_l) = -\partial\graded_l(\psi).
  \end{equation}
  Since $\tau_l $ is normal,
  $\partial^{\tau_l} \kappa' = \partial^{\tau_l}(\kappa' - \kappa_l) -
  \partial^{\tau_l}\kappa_l= \partial^{\tau_l}(\kappa' - \kappa_l)$ must be homogeneous of
  degree $l$. Since $\partial^{\tau'}\kappa' = 0$, it follows from Lemma B, that
  $\graded_l(\partial^{\tau_l}\kappa')=0$. Applying $\partial^*$ to both sides of
  \eqref{vier}, using the normality of $\kappa_l$, and the identity
  $\partial^*\circ \graded_l=\graded_l \circ \partial^{\tau_l}$, we obtain
  $\graded_l(\partial^{\tau_l}\kappa') = -\partial^*\partial \graded_l(\psi)$, and whence
  $\partial^*\partial \graded_l(\psi) = 0$. So $\partial \graded_l(\psi) = 0$, by the
  direct sum decomposition on the left of \eqrefs{hodge}{nontrivial}. But the hypothesis
  on vanishing cohomology and Proposition \ref{hodge} show that
  $H^1(\graded(TM), \graded(A))_l=0$, so that there exists $Z \in \Gamma(A^l)$ such that
  \begin{equation}
    \label{funf}
    \graded_l(\psi) = \partial \graded_l(Z).
  \end{equation}

  Define $q_l := e^{-\adjoint_Z}$, and $\tau_{l+1}=(q_l)_*\tau_l$. Applying Proposition
  \ref{gauge_transforms}, there exists $\tilde \psi \in \Gamma(T^*\!M \otimes A)$,
  homogeneous of degree $l$, such that $\tau_{l+1} = (\Adjoint_{\tilde \psi})_*\tau_l$ and
  $\tilde \psi(\#X) \equiv \tau_l(Z, X) \modulo A^{k+l+1}$, whenever $X \in A^k$. This
  last can be expressed compactly as $\graded_l(\tilde \psi) = \partial \graded_l(Z)$. By
  \eqref{funf} we therefore have $\graded_l(\psi - \tilde \psi) = 0$, i.e.,
  $\psi - \tilde \psi $ is homogeneous of degree $l + 1$. Viewing $\psi, \tilde \psi $ as
  sections of the totally intransitive groupoid $\automorphism(TM, A)$, we conclude, using
  the product formula \eqrefs{kernel}{clost2}, that $\psi \tilde \psi^{-1}$ is also
  homogeneous of degree $l + 1$, which is relevant because
  \begin{equation*}
    \tau' = (\Adjoint_\psi)_*\tau_l =
      (\Adjoint_\psi)_*(\Adjoint_{\tilde \psi^{-1}})_*\tau_{l+1}
    = (\Adjoint_{\psi \tilde \psi^{-1}})_*\tau_{l+1},
  \end{equation*}
  which shows $\tau' \equivalent{l+1} \tau_{l+1}$. The new model curvature $\tau_{l+1}$ is
  normal and regular because $\tau_l$ is normal and regular, and these properties are
  preserved by infinitesimal Weyl gauge transformations.
\end{proof}


\subsection{Prolongation as functor}\label{functor}
Morphisms of filtered Lie algebroids and Lie groupoids have been described in
\ref{morphisms} (where morphisms of abstract Levi structures were also defined). As it
suffices for current purposes, we only define morphisms of (non-abstract) Levi structures
that cover the identity on $M$. So, a {\df morphism} of infinitesimal Levi structures
$(B, R, T)$ and $(\tilde B, \tilde R, \tilde T)$ consists of a morphism
$q \colon B \rightarrow \tilde B$ of filtered Lie algebroids covering the identity such
that the induced morphims of graded vector bundles
\begin{gather*}
  q_* \colon \graded(TM)\oplus B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M)
     \rightarrow \graded(TM)\oplus \tilde B^0 \oplus \graded(T^*\!M)\\
  (V, C, Z) \mapsto (V, qC, Z)
\end{gather*}
is: (i) a morphism of Lie algebra bundles, with respect to the brackets $T$ and
$\tilde T$; and (ii) an intertwining of the infinitesimal representations $R$ and
$\tilde R$. Note that (i) implies that $T$ and $\tilde T$ have the same restrictions to
$\graded(TM)$ and $\graded(T^*\!M)$. A {\df morphism} of Levi structures
$({\mathcal H}, R, T)$ and $(\tilde {\mathcal H}, \tilde R, \tilde T)$ is a morphism
$Q \colon {\mathcal H} \rightarrow \tilde {\mathcal H} $ of filtered Lie groupoids such
that: (i) $dQ$ is a morphism of the corresponding infinitesimal Levi structures; and (ii)
$(dQ)_*$ intertwines the {\em global} representations $R$ and $\tilde R$.

The {\df prolongation} of a morphism $q$ of abstract infinitesimal Levi
structures as above is the morphism of firmly filtered Lie algebroids
\begin{gather*}
  B \oplus_R \graded(T^*\!M) \rightarrow \tilde B \oplus_{\tilde R} \graded(T^*\!M)\\
  (X, Y) \mapsto (qX, Y).
\end{gather*}
The {\df prolongation} of a morphism $Q$ of global Levi structures as above is
the morphism of firmly filtered Lie groupoids,
\begin{align*}
  {\mathcal H} \times _R \exp\graded(T^*\!M)
  &\rightarrow \tilde {\mathcal H}  \times_{\tilde R} \exp \graded (T^*\!M)\\
  (h, k) &\mapsto  (Q(h), k).
\end{align*}

With these definitions, one readily establishes the following facts:
\begin{proposition}
  Abstract prolongation is a functor from the category of infinitesimal or global
  Levi geometries to the category of firmly filtered Lie algebroids, resp., Lie
  groupoids.
\end{proposition}

\begin{corollary}
  If two infinitesimal or global Levi structures are isomorphic, then so are their
  corresponding prolongations.
\end{corollary}

\subsection{The equivalence problem}\label{equivalence_}

Recall that we can associate a graded semisimple Lie algebra model ${\mathfrak g} $ with
any parabolic geometry or Levi structure, and that vanishing of
$H({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1$ depends only on the isomorphism class in each case.

\begin{theorem}[Equivalence]
  The map that sends a regular normal parabolic geometry over $M$ to its corresponding
  Levi reduction, induces a bijection $\Xi$ between: (i) isomorphism classes of regular
  normal parabolic geometries over $M$ satisfying
  $H({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1 = 0$; and (ii) isomorphism classes of regular Levi
  structures over $M$ satisfying $H({\mathfrak g}_-, {\mathfrak g})^1 = 0$.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
  The surjectivity of $\Xi$ follows immediately from the Existence Theorem
  \ref{existence_} and Normalization Theorem \ref{normalization_}. To prove injectivity,
  suppose that $\gamma$ and $\tilde \gamma$ are isomorphism classes of parabolic
  geometries that have representatives $({\mathcal G}, \tau)$ and
  $(\tilde{\mathcal G}, \tilde \tau)$ with isomorphic Levi reductions, ${\mathcal L}$ and
  $\tilde {\mathcal L}$. Our task is to show that $\gamma = \tilde \gamma $.

  By Corollary \ref{functor}, the prolongations $\prolongation ({\mathcal L})$ and
  $\prolongation (\tilde {\mathcal L})$ of ${\mathcal L}$ and $\tilde {\mathcal L}$ are
  isomorphic, as filtered Lie groupoids. So there exists a Lie groupoid isomorphism
  $Q \colon \prolongation(\tilde {\mathcal L}) \rightarrow \prolongation({\mathcal L})$
  such that $q:=dQ$ is filtration-preserving.

  On the other hand, every regular parabolic geometry is isomorphic, as a filtered Lie
  groupoid, to the prolongation of its Levi reduction (Theorem \eqrefs{inflevi}{cch3},
  specialized to the parabolic case) so that
  ${\mathcal G} \cong \prolongation( {\mathcal L})$ and
  $\tilde {\mathcal G} \cong \prolongation( \tilde {\mathcal L})$. We push the model data
  $\tau $ and $\tilde \tau $ to $\prolongation({\mathcal L})$ and
  $\prolongation(\tilde {\mathcal L})$; denoting these by the same symbols $\tau $ and
  $\tilde \tau$, Theorem \ref{transform_} shows that
  $(\prolongation( {\mathcal L}), \tau)$ and
  $(\prolongation( \tilde {\mathcal L}), \tilde \tau)$ are also regular normal parabolic
  geometries. By construction, they are representatives of $\gamma $ and $\tilde \gamma $
  respectively.

  According to Theorem \ref{transform_}, $\tau' := Q_* \tilde \tau $ is regular normal
  model data on $\prolongation({\mathcal L})$ that makes
  $(\prolongation({\mathcal L}), \tau')$ into a parabolic geometry. By the Uniqueness
  Theorem \ref{un}, there exists an infinitesimal Weyl gauge transformation
  $r \colon A \rightarrow A$ such that $\tau' = r_*\tau$. But every infinitesimal Weyl
  gauge transformation is integrated by a global Weyl gauge transformation, i.e.,
  $r = dR$, for some Weyl gauge transformation
  $R \colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $, which is, in particular, an
  isomorphism $({\mathcal G}, \tau) \rightarrow ({\mathcal G}, \tau')$. But then, by
  construction, $Q^{-1} \circ R$ is an isomorphism
  $(\prolongation({\mathcal L}), \tau) \rightarrow (\prolongation((\tilde {\mathcal L}),
  \tilde \tau)$, which shows $\gamma = \tilde \gamma $.

\end{proof}


\appendix
\refreshpage
\section{Reductive Lie algebras and their parabolic subalgebras}\label{lie_algebra_facts}
The material below is mostly adapted from \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}, to which the
reader is referred for omitted proofs. The results there are established by rather direct
methods the reader will not readily find in standard texts, which tend to emphasize
semisimple Lie algebras and algebraically closed base fields.

In this appendix all Lie algebras are finite-dimensional over a field of characteristic
zero.

\subsection{Nilpotency}\label{nilpotency}
A subalgebra ${\mathfrak m} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is a {\df nil subalgebra} if $\rho(X)$
is nilpotent for any representation $\rho \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow \gl(V)$. The
{\df nilpotent radical} of ${\mathfrak g} $, denoted here by $\nil({\mathfrak g})$, is the
largest nil subalgebra that is also an ideal of ${\mathfrak g} $. See
\cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16} for other characterizations.\footnote{For perfect Lie
  algebras, such as semisimple ones, the nilpotent radical coincides with the so-called
  {\em nilradical}, sometimes denoted $\nil({\mathfrak g})$ also, but it may be different
  more generally.}

\subsection{Reductive Lie algebras and gradings thereof}\label{par}
Recall that a Lie algebra is {\df reductive} if it satisfies
\eqref{gra3}, \eqref{gra1}, or \eqref{gra2} below.

\begin{proposition}[Theorem 2.20 of \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}]
  The following are equivalent:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{gra3} $\nil({\mathfrak g})=0$.
  \item\label{gra1}
    ${\mathfrak g} $ admits a faithful, completely reducible representation.
  \item\label{gra2}
    There exists a non-degenerate trace-form $B_\rho$ on ${\mathfrak g}$.
  \end{conditions}
  Moreover, for any Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g} $, the quotient
  ${\mathfrak g}/\nil({\mathfrak g})$ is reductive, and called the {\df reductive Levi
    quotient} of ${\mathfrak g} $.

\end{proposition}
\noindent%
Recall here that a {\df trace-form} $B_\rho$ is defined by
$B_\rho(X, Y)=\trace(\rho_X \circ \rho_Y)$, for some representation
$\rho \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow \gl(V)$, and this is always $\adjoint$-invariant;
$B_{\adjoint}$ is the {\df Killing form}, which is non-degenerate when ${\mathfrak g} $ is
semisimple (a direct sum of simple ideals) by Cartan's criterion \cite[Proposition
2.19]{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}.

\begin{theorem}[Theorem 2.20 of \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}]
  If ${\mathfrak g} $ is reductive, then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{hra1} ${\mathfrak g} = Z({\mathfrak g})\oplus [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$
    and this decomposition is orthogonal, for any non-degenerate trace-form.
  \item\label{hra2} $[{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$ is semisimple.
  \item\label{hra3} A derivation $D \in \selfmorphism({\mathfrak g}) $ is of the form
    $D(X) = [Y, X]$, for some $Y \in {\mathfrak g} $ (i.e, is inner) if and only if $D$
    vanishes on $Z({\mathfrak g})$, in which case $Y$ is uniquely determined by requiring
    $Y \in [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$.
  \end{conditions}
\end{theorem}

The $\adjoint$-invariance of a non-degenerate trace-form has strong consequences for the
components in any grading of a reductive Lie algebra, which we need later on:

\begin{lemma}
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be a graded reductive Lie algebra
  and $B$ a trace-form. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{ira2} If $B$ is the Killing-form, then its kernel ${\mathfrak g} ^\perp$ is
    exactly $Z({\mathfrak g})$.
  \end{conditions}
  If $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$, then:
 \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{ira0} There exists a unique grading element
    $E \in {\mathfrak g}_0\cap[{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$.
  \item\label{ira1} With respect to $B$, we have ${\mathfrak g}_j \perp {\mathfrak g}_k$
    whenever $j+k \ne 0$.
  \item\label{ira3} If ${\mathfrak g}^\perp \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$ (e.g., $B$ is the
    Killing form) then ${\mathfrak g}_j$ and ${\mathfrak g}_{-j}$ are $B$-dual, for all
    $j\ne 0$, and in particular $|{\mathfrak g}| = m$ for some $m \ge 0$ (see Notation
    \ref{filtered}).
  \item\label{ira4} If $B$ is non-degenerate, then ${\mathfrak g}_j$ and
    ${\mathfrak g}_{-j}$ are $B$-dual for all $j \in {\mathbb Z}$, and
    $|{\mathfrak g}| = m$, for some $m\ge 0$.
  \end{conditions}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
  \eqref{ira2}: Suppose that $B$ is the Killing form. Then $\adjoint_X = 0$ for any
  $X \in Z({\mathfrak g})$, which shows $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}^\perp$. To
  prove the reverse inclusion, note that for any $X, Y \in {\mathfrak g}$, the derivations
  $\adjoint_X$, $\adjoint_Y$, and hence $\adjoint_X \circ \adjoint_Y$, send
  $Z({\mathfrak g})$ to $0$ and $[{\mathfrak g} , {\mathfrak g}]$ to itself. It follows
  from \eqref{hra1} that $B(X, Y) = B_\mathrm{ss}(X_{\mathrm{ss}}, Y_{\mathrm{ss}})$,
  where $B_\mathrm{ss}$ is the Killing form on $[{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}] $, while
  $X_{\mathrm{ss}}$ and $Y_{\mathrm{ss}}$ are the projections of $X$ and $Y$ along
  $Z({\mathfrak g})$ onto $[{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$ , which is semisimple,
  according to \eqref{hra2}. In particular, $B_{\mathrm{ss}}$ is non-degenerate, and
  therefore $X \in {\mathfrak g}^\perp$ only if $X_\mathrm{ss}=0$, i.e.,
  $X \in Z({\mathfrak g})$.

  \eqref{ira0}: Define $D(X) = jX$, for $X \in {\mathfrak g}_j$. Then $D$ is a
  derivation vanishing on ${\mathfrak g}_0\supset Z({\mathfrak g})$. By \eqref{hra3},
  there exists a unique $E \in [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$ such that $D(X) = [E, X]$,
  and $E \in {\mathfrak g}_0$ because $[E, E]=0$.

  \eqref{ira1}: For any $X \in {\mathfrak g}_j$ and $Y \in {\mathfrak g}_k$, we have
  $(j + k) B(X, Y) = B([E, X], Y) + B(X, [E, Y]) = 0$, by $\adjoint$-invariance of $B$.



  \eqref{ira3} \& \eqref{ira4}: Suppose
  ${\mathfrak g}^\perp \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$.  Let $X \in {\mathfrak g}_j$ and suppose
  $B(X, Y) = 0$ for all $Y \in {\mathfrak g}_{-j}$. Then in fact, $B(X, Y)=0$ for all
  $Y \in {\mathfrak g}$, by \eqref{ira1}, i.e., $X \in {\mathfrak g}^\perp$. So
  \eqref{ira4} is clear. If ${\mathfrak g}^\perp \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$,
  then $X \in {\mathfrak g}_j\cap {\mathfrak g}_0$ and so \eqref{ira3} holds.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Parabolic subalgebras and Levi quotients}
\label{parabolic_characterizations}
Recall that a subalgebra ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is {\df parabolic} if it
contains the normalizer of a maximial nil subalgebra \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}.

\begin{theorem}[Theorem 3.4 of \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}]
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be reductive and fix an arbitrary non-degenerate
  trace-form. Then the following are equivalent:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{par1} ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ is parabolic.
  \item\label{par2} ${\mathfrak p}^\perp \subset [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$ and
    ${\mathfrak g}^\perp$ is nilpotent.
  \item\label{par3} ${\mathfrak g}^\perp$ is a nil subalgebra of ${\mathfrak g} $.
  \item \label{par4} ${\mathfrak p}^\perp \subset {\mathfrak p} $ and
    $N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak p}) = {\mathfrak p}$.
  \item\label{par5} ${\mathfrak p}^\perp=\nil({\mathfrak p})$.
  \end{conditions}
\end{theorem}
\noindent%
In particular, \eqref{par5} shows that ${\mathfrak p}^\perp $ does not depend on the
choice of trace-form.

Proposition \ref{in_terms_of_gradings} characterizes parabolics in terms of gradings.

\begin{remark}
  If ${\mathfrak g} $ is reductive {\em and the base field is algebraically closed}, then
  the normalizers of mimimal subalgebras coincide with the Borel (maximal solvable)
  subalgebras. However, Borel subalgebras play no role here.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Canonical filtrations}\label{can}
When ${\mathfrak g}^1 \subset {\mathfrak g}^0 \subset {\mathfrak g} $ and
${\mathfrak g}^1$ is an ideal of ${\mathfrak g}^0$ then we have the {\df
  pre-filtration} associated with $({\mathfrak g}^0, {\mathfrak g}^1)$, introduced in
\ref{canonical_filtrations_}.

\begin{proposition}
  Fix a non-degenerate trace-form on a reductive Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g}$ and let
  ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ a parabolic subalgebra.
  \begin{conditions}
  \item\label{hosp1} The pre-filtration of ${\mathfrak g} $ associated with
    $({\mathfrak p}, {\mathfrak p}^\perp )$, which is independent of the choice of form,
    is well-defined and is even a bone fide Lie algebra filtration, with
    $|{\mathfrak g}| = m $ for some $m\ge 0$ (see Notation \ref{filtered}). This is the
    {\df canonical filtration} associated with ${\mathfrak p} $.
  \item\label{hosp2} In the canonical filtration,
    $({\mathfrak g}^j)^\perp = {\mathfrak g}^k$, whenever $j + k =1$.
  \end{conditions}
\end{proposition}
\noindent%

\begin{proof}
  Since ${\mathfrak p} $ is parabolic, ${\mathfrak p}^\perp $ is an ideal of
  ${\mathfrak p} $, by Theorem \ref{par}. In particular Lemma \ref{canonical_filtrations_}
  applies with ${\mathfrak g}^0:={\mathfrak p} $ and
  ${\mathfrak g}^1:={\mathfrak p}^\perp$, so that the pre-filtration associated with
  $({\mathfrak p}, {\mathfrak p}^\perp)$ is well-defined.  The triviality
  $({\mathfrak g}^0)^\perp={\mathfrak g}^1$ anchors a straightforward inductive argument
  that establishes \eqref{hosp2}. Since ${\mathfrak p}^\perp $ is nilpotent, there is
  $m \ge 0$ such that ${\mathfrak g}^m\ne 0$ but ${\mathfrak g}^{m+1} = 0$. In that case
  ${\mathfrak g}^{-m} = ({\mathfrak g}^{m+1})^\perp = \{0\}^\perp = {\mathfrak g} $, while
  ${\mathfrak g}^{-m+1}=({\mathfrak g}^m)^\perp \ne {\mathfrak g}$. This shows the
  pre-filtration is a filtration and $|{\mathfrak g}|=m$.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Proposition 3.9 of \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}]
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be a reductive Lie algebra. Then, with respect to the canonical
  filtration associated with any parabolic subalgebra
  ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g}$, $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ is a reductive Lie
  algebra with centre lying in $\graded({\mathfrak g})_0$.
\end{theorem}

While a proof of the theorem is given in \cite{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}, some details were
not immediatlely obvious to us, and we accordingly provide them below.
\begin{proof}

  Let $B_\rho $ be a non-degenerate trace form on ${\mathfrak g} $. By \eqref{hosp2}, we
  have ${\mathfrak g}^j \perp {\mathfrak g}^k$, whenever $j + k =1$, which allows us to
  drop $B_\rho$ to an invariant quadratic form $Q$ on $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ satisfying
  $Q(\graded({\mathfrak g}_j), \graded({\mathfrak g}_k)) = 0$ for $j + k \ne 0$ and
  $Q(\graded_j(X), \graded_{-j}(Y)) = B_\rho(X, Y)$ for $X \in {\mathfrak g}^j$ and
  $Y \in {\mathfrak g}^{-j}$, $j \in {\mathbb Z}$. Since
  $({\mathfrak g}^j)^\perp = {\mathfrak g}^k$, whenever $j + k =1$, $Q$ is
  non-degenerate. To show $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ is reductive, it therefore suffices to
  show $Q=B_{\bar \rho}$ for some representation $\bar\rho$ of $\graded({\mathfrak
    g})$. If we can find a filtration of $V$ that makes $\rho $ into a {\em filtered}
  representation, i.e., such that
  \begin{equation}\label{dep}
        \rho_{{\mathfrak g}^j}(V^k) \subset V^{j+k},
  \end{equation}
  for all $j, k \in {\mathbb Z} $, then we can take $\bar \rho $ to be the corresponding
  representation of $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ on $\graded(V)$ \cite[Proposition
  2.14]{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}.

  To construct such a filtration of $V$, first define $V^j=0$, for $j>0$, and let
  $V^0 = \{v \in V \suchthat \rho_{{\mathfrak g}^1}(v) = 0\}$. Now $V^0\ne 0$, by Engel's
  theorem \cite[Lemma 2.7]{Calderbank_Noppakaew_16}, because
  ${\mathfrak g}^1=\nil({\mathfrak p})$ acts nilpotently under $\rho $. For $j\le -1$, we
  inductively define $V^j=\{v \in V\suchthat \rho_{{\mathfrak g}^1}v \subset
  V^{j+1}\}$. Since ${\mathfrak g}^1$ acts nilpotently on $V$, it acts nilpotently on
  every space $V/V^k$, $k \in {\mathbb Z} $, and so $V^k$ is a proper subset of $V^{k-1}$
  by Engel's theorem, and $\cup_j V^j =V$, by finite-dimensionality.

  By definition, \eqref{dep} holds for $j=1$ and all $k \in {\mathbb Z} $. For $j\ge 2 $,
  we have ${\mathfrak g}^j = [{\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g}^{j-1}]$, so a simple
  induction shows that \eqref{dep} holds for all $j \ge 2$ and $k \in {\mathbb
    Z}$. Finally, supposing $j \le 0$, we have \eqref{dep} if $k \ge - j + 1$, because
  $V^k = 0$ in that case. So, to deal with the $j \le 0$ case, we proceed with an
  induction, supposing \eqref{dep} holds whenever $k + j \ge m$, for some $m \ge 1$, and
  show it must also hold if $k + j \ge m - 1$.  So, supposing, $X \in {\mathfrak g}^j$ and
  $v \in V^k$, with $j + k \ge m - 1$, we need to show $\rho_{X^j}v \in V^{j+k}$, which
  means showing, for any $X^1 \in {\mathfrak g}^1$, that
  $\rho_{X^1}\rho_{X^j}v \in V^{j+k+1}$. But by the induction hypothesis, we will have
  $\rho_{[X^1, X^j]}v \in V^{j+k+1}$, because $[X^1, X^j] \in {\mathfrak g}^{j +1}$ and
  $(j + 1) + k \ge m $, while $\rho_{X^j}\rho_{X^1}v \in V^{j+k+1}$ because
  $\rho_{X^1}v \in V^{k+1}$ and $j + (k+1) \ge m$. So the desired result follows from the
  identity $\rho_{X^1}\rho_{X^j}v = \rho_{[X^1, X^j]}v + \rho_{X^j}\rho_{X^1}v$.

  If an element of $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ is central, then it's homogeneous components
  must be central. So suppose $\graded_j(X)\ne 0$ is central, for some
  $X \in {\mathfrak g}^j$ and, initially, suppose $j \ge 1$. Then
  \begin{equation}
    \label{pearl}
    {\mathfrak g}^j = [{\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g}^{j-1}].
  \end{equation}
  In case $j \ge 2$, this follows from the definition of ${\mathfrak g}^j$. In case
  $j =1$, this follows because
  $[{\mathfrak g}^1, {\mathfrak g}^0] = ([{\mathfrak p}^\perp, {\mathfrak
    p}]^\perp)^\perp=N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak p}^\perp)^\perp = N_{\mathfrak
    g}({\mathfrak p})^\perp={\mathfrak p}^\perp={\mathfrak g}^1$. The fourth equality
  holds because parabolic subalgebras are self-normalizing (Theorem
  \eqrefs{par}{par4}). Regarding the rest of the argument, note that $\adjoint$-invariance
  of the trace-form guarantees
  $N_{\mathfrak g} ({\mathfrak p}) \subset [{\mathfrak p}, {\mathfrak p}^\perp]^\perp =
  N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak p}^\perp)$ for {\em any} subspace
  ${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g}$, so that the reverse inclusion
  $N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak p}^\perp)\subset N_{\mathfrak g} ({\mathfrak p})$ follows
  from ${\mathfrak p}^{\perp \perp}={\mathfrak p}$, which we have by non-degeneracy. The
  point of \eqref{pearl} is that it shows
  $\graded({\mathfrak g})_j \subset [\graded({\mathfrak g}), \graded({\mathfrak g})]$. By
  Theorem \eqrefs{par}{hra1}, $\graded({\mathfrak g})_j$ must intersects the centre of
  $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ trivially, because $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ is reductive, as we
  have already shown. So $\graded_j(X)=0$, a contradiction, and therefore $j \le 0$.

  Since $\graded_j(X)$ is central, we have, in particular,
  \begin{equation}
    \label{pearl2}
    [X, {\mathfrak g}^1] \subset {\mathfrak g}^{j+2}.
  \end{equation}
  If $j = -1$, then we conclude $X \in N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak g}^1)=N_{\mathfrak g}({\mathfrak p}^\perp)={\mathfrak p}={\mathfrak g}^0$ by preceding arguments. But again this implies the contradiction, $\graded_j(X)=0$.  If $j\ge -2$, then \eqref{pearl2} implies $X \in {\mathfrak g}^{j+1}$, by the definition of ${\mathfrak g}^{j+1}$, and again the disallowed $\graded_j(X)=0$ follows. The only remaining possibility is $j=0$, which proves the stated property of the centre.
\end{proof}
\begin{corollary}
  Let ${\mathfrak g} $ be a reductive Lie algebra filtered canonically by some parabolic
  subalgebra ${\mathfrak p}$. Then the associated graded $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ admits a
  unique grading element $E \in [\graded({\mathfrak g}), \graded({\mathfrak g})]$, so that
  ${\mathfrak g} $ and $\graded({\mathfrak g}) $ are isomorphic Lie algebras, and the
  parabolic ${\mathfrak p} $ contains $Z({\mathfrak g})$.
\end{corollary}
\begin{proof}
  The theorem and Lemma \eqrefs{par}{ira0} establish the existence of the grading element
  $E$. According to arguments recalled in \ref{splittings}, any lift
  $\epsilon \in {\mathfrak p}={\mathfrak g}^0$ of $E$ defines a Lie algebroid isomorphism
  $\Phi_\epsilon \colon \graded({\mathfrak g}) \rightarrow {\mathfrak g} $. Since
  $\graded({\mathfrak g})_0$ contains the centre, by the theorem, so must
  ${\mathfrak p} $, because of the isomorphism.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Parabolics in terms of gradings}\label{in_terms_of_gradings}
\begin{proposition}
  Suppose ${\mathfrak g}$ is reductive. Then a subalgebra ${\mathfrak p} $ is parabolic if
  and only if there exists a grading of ${\mathfrak g} $ such that
  $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$ and
  ${\mathfrak p} = \oplus_{j\ge 0} {\mathfrak g}_j$. In that case the corresponding
    filtration, defined by ${\mathfrak g}^j:=\oplus_{i\ge j}{\mathfrak g}_i$, is the
    canonical filtration associated with ${\mathfrak p} $ if and only if ${\mathfrak g}_1$
    generates ${\mathfrak g}^1$, or equivalently, if
    ${\mathfrak g}^1 \supset {\mathfrak g}^2 \supset {\mathfrak g}^3 \supset \cdots $ is
    the lower central series of ${\mathfrak g}^1$.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
  Suppose ${\mathfrak p} $ is parabolic and we filter ${\mathfrak g} $ with the canonical
  filtration defined by ${\mathfrak p}$. Then, as in the proof of the preceding corollary,
  we can identify ${\mathfrak g} $ with the associated graded $\graded({\mathfrak g})$ and
  have ${\mathfrak p} = \oplus_{j\ge 0}{\mathfrak g}_j$. Any element of $Z({\mathfrak g})$
  must commute with $E \in {\mathfrak g}_0$, so $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak p} $
  already forces $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$.

  Conversely, suppose ${\mathfrak g} $ is a graded reductive Lie algebra with
  $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$ and define
  ${\mathfrak p}:=\oplus_{j\ge 0}{\mathfrak g}_j$. Fix an arbitrary non-degenerate
  trace-form. Then, to show that ${\mathfrak p} $ is parabolic, it suffices, according to
  Theorem \ref{parabolic_characterizations}, to show that ${\mathfrak p}^\perp $ is
  nilpotent and ${\mathfrak p}^\perp\subset [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$. Now suppose
  $j + k =1$. Then, writing ${\mathfrak g}^j := \oplus_{j'\ge j}{\mathfrak g}_{j'}$, we
  claim
  \begin{equation}\label{gluck}
    {\mathfrak g}^j = ({\mathfrak g}^k)^\perp.
  \end{equation}
  Indeed, for any $j'\ge j$ and $k' \ge k$, we have $j' + k' \ne 0$, so that
  ${\mathfrak g}_{j'} \perp {\mathfrak g}_{k'}$, by Lemma \eqrefs{par}{ira1}, and
  consequently ${\mathfrak g}^j \subset ({\mathfrak g}^k)^\perp$. But, counting
  dimensions, it's easy to see that ${\mathfrak g}^j$ and ${\mathfrak g}^k$ have
  complementary dimensions, if
  $\dimension({\mathfrak g}_i)=\dimension({\mathfrak g}_{-i})$ for all $i$, which is true,
  by Lemma \eqrefs{par}{ira4}. So \eqref{gluck} holds. In particular, taking $j=1$ and
  $k=0$, we have ${\mathfrak g}^1={\mathfrak g}^\perp$, which shows that
  ${\mathfrak p}^\perp$ is nilpotent. Also, as ${\mathfrak g}_0 \subset {\mathfrak p} $,
  we have
  ${\mathfrak p}^\perp \subset {\mathfrak g}_0^\perp \subset Z({\mathfrak g})^\perp =
  [{\mathfrak g}, {\mathfrak g}]$, by Theorem \eqrefs{par}{hra1}.

  It remains to show the filtration defined by the grading is the canonical filtration
  associated with ${\mathfrak p} $, under the additional assumption that ${\mathfrak g}_1$
  generates ${\mathfrak g}^1$. We leave it to the reader to establish the characterization
  of this condition in terms of the lower central series. As the canonical filtration also
  satisfies \eqref{gluck} for $j + k = 1$ (Proposition \eqrefs{can}{hosp2}) it suffices to
  show the filtration components of degree $j$ coincide, for $j\ge 0$. For $j=0$, this is
  tautological, and we just established the $j=1$ case. For the canonical filtration,
  ${\mathfrak g}^1 \supset {\mathfrak g}^2 \supset {\mathfrak g}^3 \supset \cdots $ is the
  lower central series, by definition, which finishes the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Parabolic subalgebras are separating}\label{separating_}
The notion of a {\df separating} or {\df infinitesimally separating} subalgebra
${\mathfrak p} \subset {\mathfrak g} $ has been introduced in \ref{separating}.
\begin{proposition}
  Every parabolic subalgebra ${\mathfrak p} $ of a reductive Lie algebra $\mathfrak g$ is
  separating, and hence also infinitesimally separating.
\end{proposition}
The following proof was obtained after David Calderbank pointed out to the author a
similar proof for the infinitesimal case.
\begin{proof}
  Suppose $\phi \in \automorphism({\mathfrak g})$ fixes elements of ${\mathfrak p}$. By
  Theorem \ref{can} and its corollary, we may as well suppose ${\mathfrak g} $ is graded
  with ${\mathfrak p} =\oplus_{j\ge0}{\mathfrak g}_j$ and
  $Z({\mathfrak g}) \subset {\mathfrak g}_0$.  Supposing $j \ge 1$, it suffices to show
  $\phi$ fixes elements of ${\mathfrak g}_{-j}$. By uniqueness of the grading element $E$,
  we must have $\phi(E) = E$, which already shows that $\phi $ must map
  ${\mathfrak g}_{-j}$ to itself. Suppose $Y \in {\mathfrak g}_j$ is arbitrary, and let
  $B$ denote the Killing form. Then as $\phi \in \automorphism({\mathfrak g})$, we must
  have $B(\phi X, \phi Y) = B(X, Y)$, i.e., $B(\phi X, Y) = B(X, Y)$, because $\phi $
  fixes elements of ${\mathfrak g}_j$, as $j\ge 1$. So, by Lemma \eqrefs{par}{ira3}, we
  conclude that $\phi X = X$.
\end{proof}

\section{The space of splittings of a filtered Lie algebra}
\label{appendix_splittings}
In this section we prove Lemma \ref{splittings}, which applies to arbitrary graded Lie
algebras, not just reductive ones. While a known result, we have given a formulation fit
for current purposes. For convenience, here is a restatement:

\begin{lemma}
  Let $\mathfrak g$ be a filtered Lie algebra and let $\epsilon \in {\mathfrak g}^0 $ be a
  lift of some grading element $E \in \graded({\mathfrak g})$. Let $G^0$ be {\em any} Lie
  group integrating ${\mathfrak g}^0 $, and let $G^1 \subset G^0$ be the connected Lie
  subgroup with Lie algebra ${\mathfrak g}^1$. Then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item Under the adjoint action, $G^1$ acts freely and transitively on the affine space
    $\epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1\subset {\mathfrak g}^0$, and consequently on $E$-splittings of
    ${\mathfrak g} $. In particular, $G^1$ is simply connected (i.e.,
    $G^1 \cong \exp({\mathfrak g}^1)$) so that the infinitesimal adjoint action of
    ${\mathfrak g}^1$ on ${\mathfrak g} $ has a unique globalization
    $\Adjoint\colon G^1 \rightarrow \automorphism({\mathfrak g})$ extending the adjoint
    action just mentioned.\label{splittings1_}
  % \item For any $g \in G^1$, $\Adjoint_g - \identity$ sends ${\mathfrak g}^j$ to
  %   ${\mathfrak g}^{j+1}$, for any $j \in {\mathbb Z}$, i.e., is homogeneous of degree
  %   one.\label{splittings30}
  % \item The only linear map $\varphi \colon {\mathfrak g} \rightarrow {\mathfrak g} $ that
  %   commutes with $\adjoint_e$ and such that $\varphi - \identity$ is homogeneous of degree
  %   one is the identity.\label{splittings40}
  \item For any $\epsilon_1, \epsilon_2 \in \epsilon + {\mathfrak g}^1$, we have
    $\Phi_{\epsilon_2} = \Adjoint_g\circ \Phi_{\epsilon_1}$, where $g \in G^1$ is the unique element for
    which $\epsilon_2 = \Adjoint_g \epsilon_1$. \label{splittings2_}
  \end{conditions}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
  Note first that the filtration property ensures that the action of ${\mathfrak g}^1$ on
  ${\mathfrak g}^0 $ is filtration-preserving, and the corresponding action on
  $\graded({\mathfrak g}^0)$ is trivial. The same must be true for adjoint action of
  $G^1$, as $G^1$ is connected. In particular $e + {\mathfrak g}^1$ must be
  $G^1$-invariant, for any lift $e \in {\mathfrak g}^0$ of $E$. Suppose that the action of
  $G^1$ on $e + {\mathfrak g}^1$ is infinitesimally free. Then, by a dimension count, this
  action is infinitesimally transitive, and so transitive, since $G^1$ is
  connected. Infinitesimal freeness means the stabilizer subgroup $H$ for the action is
  discrete, so that $G^1 \rightarrow G^1/H \cong e + {\mathfrak g}^1$ is a covering
  space. Since $e + {\mathfrak g}^1$ is simply connected, $H$ is trivial and the remaining
  claims in \eqref{splittings1_} follow.

  To show that the action of $G^1$ on $e + {\mathfrak g}^1$ is infinitesimally free, for
  each $X \in {\mathfrak g}^1$, let $X^\dagger$ denote the infinitesimal generator of $X$,
  a vector field on $e + {\mathfrak g}^1$. We have $X^\dagger(e') = [X, e']$, for
  $e' \in e + {\mathfrak g}^1$. Supposing $X^\dagger(e') = 0$, write $X=\sum_{j>0} X_j$
  for some $X_j \in {\mathfrak g}_j$, where ${\mathfrak g}=\oplus_j {\mathfrak g}_j$ is
  the $E$-splitting corresponding to $e'$. Then we conclude that $\sum_{j>0}jX_j=0$, from
  which it follows that $X=0$.

  To prove \eqref{splittings2_}, observe that $\Phi_e$ is characterized among all
  isomorphisms $\graded({\mathfrak g}) \rightarrow {\mathfrak g}$ by the requirement
  \begin{equation*}
    [\Phi_e(X), e] = j \Phi_e(X)\quad
    \text{for all $X \in \graded_j({\mathfrak g}),\, j \in {\mathbb Z}$}.
  \end{equation*}
  Taking $e = e_1$ and applying $\Adjoint_g$ to both sides, we obtain
  $[\Adjoint_g \Phi_{e_1}X, \Adjoint_g e_1] = j\Adjoint_g e_1$, i.e.,
  \begin{equation*}
    [(\Adjoint_g \circ\, \Phi_{e_1})X, e_2] = je_2.
  \end{equation*}
  So, by the aforementioned characterization, we conclude
  $\Adjoint_g\circ\,\Phi_{e_1} = \Phi_{e_2}$.
\end{proof}

\section{Cartan connections from representations}%
\label{rut}
In this section we prove \ref{cartan}, restated here for convenience:

\begin{proposition}
  Suppose ${\mathcal G} $ is a Lie groupoid with a transitive Lie algebroid $A$. Then for
  every representation $\rho $ of ${\mathcal G} $ on $A$, extending the canonical
  representation of ${\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel, there exists a unique Cartan
  connection $D$ on ${\mathcal G} $ such that $\bar D = \rho $.
\end{proposition}

First note that the kernel of the canonical projection
$J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $ is a totally intransitive Lie groupoid whose
form we can show is independent of the particular integration ${\mathcal G} $ of $A$. This
kernel is denoted $\automorphism(TM, A)$ and described further in Section
\ref{deform}. For present purposes, the following properties suffice:

\begin{lemma}
  If the Lie algebroid $A$ of a Lie groupoid ${\mathcal G} $ is transitive, then:
  \begin{conditions}
  \item The adjoint representation of $J^1 {\mathcal G} $ on $A$ restricts to
    representation of $\automorphism(TM, A) \subset J^1 {\mathcal G} $ that is
    faithful.\label{kr1}
  \item For any $m \in M$ and each linear map $L \colon A|_m \rightarrow A|_m$,
    restricting to the identity on the anchor kernel, and whose corresponding factoring
    $T_mM \rightarrow T_mM$ through the anchor $\# \colon A \rightarrow TM$ is an
    isomorphism, there exists a unique $\psi \in \model|_m$ such that
    $\Adjoint_\psi = L$.\label{kr2}
  \end{conditions}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}[Proof of lemma]
  Part \eqref{kr1} follows from Proposition \ref{kernel}, while part \eqref{kr2} follows
  from \eqrefs{deformations}{opal2a} and transitivity.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of proposition]
  We seek a Lie groupoid morphism $S \colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G} $
  that is a right-inverse for the projection
  $\pi \colon J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G} $ and satisfies
  \begin{equation}
    \Adjoint_{S(g)} = \rho_g \qquad g \in {\mathcal G}.\label{kr3}
  \end{equation}
  To that end, let $\tilde S \colon U \rightarrow \pi^{-1}(U)$ be any smooth, local, right
inverse for $\pi $, defined on some open neighborhood $U \subset {\mathcal G}$ (not
necessarily respecting groupoid multiplication).  Then, for each arrow $g \in U$ beginning
at some $m \in M$, $L = \Adjoint_{\tilde S(g)}^{-1}\circ \rho_g $ satisfies the
condition in \eqref{kr2}, because $\rho$ extends the adjoint representation of
${\mathcal G} $ on the anchor kernel of $A$. Accordingly, there exists unique
$\psi(g) \in \model$ such that
$\Adjoint_{\psi(g)} =\Adjoint_{\tilde S(g)}^{-1}\circ \rho_g$, so that \eqref{kr3} holds
if we define $S(g) = \tilde S(g)\psi(g)$. It is not hard to see that
$S \colon U \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G} $ is smooth. Moreover, from the uniqueness, it
is clear that $S(g)$ does not actually depend on the choice of local right inverse
$\tilde S$, which means we can collate local definitions of $S$ as just described into a
global map $S \colon {\mathcal G} \rightarrow J^1 {\mathcal G} $. From \eqref{kr3}, we
will have $\Adjoint_{S(g)S(h)}=\Adjoint_{S(gh)}$ for all multipliable
$g, h \in {\mathcal G} $. Using \eqref{kr1} it is not hard to see that $S(gh)=S(g)S(h)$
must follow, because $S(g)S(h)$ and $S(gh)$ share a common image under the projection
$J^1 {\mathcal G} \rightarrow {\mathcal G}$, namely $gh$. A similar argument shows that
$S$ is uniquely determined by requirement \eqref{kr3}.

\end{proof}
